4 min
Divine Mercy and the Sincere Seeker
Exposition on divine mercy and the seeker's disposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imam's exposition on how Allah's mercy guides the sincere seeker and deceives the obstinate.

Core Idea
Sincere intention attracts divine guidance; obstinacy invites deprivation.

The Imam begins with a profound insight into divine mercy and justice. He relates from Ibn Dahaq that the seeker of truth with sincere intention, free from hatred of scholars, pride, and envy, will be granted the jewel of faith. Conversely, the obstinate one is denied guidance. This principle is captured in a concise statement that forms the basis for understanding how spiritual success depends on one's inner disposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divine mercy
Read the Arabic passage about the mercy to the truthful and deception to the deceivers. Notice the contrast between the two groups.
The passage is attributed to Imam Ibn Dahaq.
2
Identify the key condition
Find the conditions that lead to guidance: sincere intention, not hating scholars, no pride or envy.
3
Understand the principle
The Imam summarizes with a sentence: 'No one listened with the intention of seeking guidance except that he was guided...' Reflect on how this applies to learning.
This is a universal spiritual law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه البديع في رحمة الله تعالى بالصادقين ، ومكره بالماكرين وقد نقل هذا عن الإمام ابن دهاق ؛ بأنَّ طالب الحقِّ بصدق نيَّة ، غيرَ المبغض للعلماء ، والذي ليس في قلبه كبر أو حسدٌ . . لا بدَّ أن يُنيلَهُ المولى جوهرة الإيمان ، والمعاند على العكس ، تلك الكلمة التي بسط القول فيها هي : ( ما استمع أحد بنية الاسترشادِ إلا اهتدى ، وما قصد أحد المعاندة إلا حرمَهُ الله تعالى الهدى ) - بيان خطورة المتسننة ؛ وهم الذي ينتسبون إلى أهل السنة والسلف الصالح ولا نصيب لهم من ذلك إلا ثرثرة اللسان ، وذلك في أبحاث التنزيه ، ومن ذلك قوله : ( وقد لطَّخَتِ الحشوية بهذا المذهب الفاسد بعض أئمة أهل السنَّةِ ، فربما نسبوه لأحمد بن حنبل رضي الله تعالى عنه ؛ إذْ هم مقلّدون له في الفروع ، فأوهموا أنَّهم كما تبعوه في الفروع تبعوه في العقائد ) - تأكيده على أنَّ من عرف مذهب أهل الحق فقد جمع بين الشريعة والحقيقة ، ويتجلى هذا جليّاً في ردَّ شُبه المعتزلة في مسألة خلق أفعال العباد ، والتحسين والتقبيح العقليين
His beautiful exposition on the mercy of Allah Almighty to the truthful and His deception of the deceivers, and he transmitted this from Imam Ibn Dahaq: that the seeker of truth with sincere intention, not hating the scholars, and who has no pride or envy in his heart... Allah will surely grant him the jewel of faith, and the obstinate one is the opposite. That word which he elaborated is: 'No one listened with the intention of seeking guidance except that he was guided, and no one intended obstinacy except that Allah deprived him of guidance.' - Explanation of the danger of the 'mutasannina' (those who pretend to be Sunnis); they are those who claim affiliation to Ahl al-Sunna and the righteous Salaf but have no share of that except chatter of the tongue, and that is in the investigations of tanzih (transcendence). Among that is his statement: 'The Hashwiyya have stained some Imams of Ahl al-Sunna with this corrupt doctrine, so perhaps they attributed it to Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah be pleased with him; since they are his followers in furu' (branches), they gave the false impression that just as they follow him in furu', they follow him in 'aqa'id (creeds).' - His emphasis that whoever knows the madhhab of the people of truth has combined sharia and haqiqa, and this is clearly manifested in his refutation of the doubts of the Mu'tazila on the issue of the creation of human actions (khalq af'al al-'ibad) and rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyan).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents three important insights from the Imam. First, his beautiful exposition on divine mercy and deception: he relates from Ibn Dahaq that the sincere seeker of truth who does not hate scholars and has no pride or envy will be granted the jewel of faith, while the obstinate one is deprived. The Imam states a key principle: whoever listens with the intention of guidance will be guided, but whoever intends obstinacy is deprived by Allah of guidance. Second, he warns about the mutasannina (those who falsely claim to be Sunni). These people attribute corrupt doctrines to the Salaf, for example falsely attributing anthropomorphic views to Ahmad ibn Hanbal because they follow him in legal rulings, thus misleading people that they also follow him in creed. The Imam highlights the danger of this deception. Third, he emphasizes that knowing the madhhab of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) unifies sharia and haqiqa (law and spiritual reality). This is seen in his refutation of Mu'tazilite doubts regarding the creation of human actions and the rational basis of good and evil. This shows the integration of theological reasoning with revealed law.

Key Terms
صادقين
ماكرين
نية
هدى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine guidance is purely arbitrary or based on external factors.
Correction: The Imam shows it is connected to one's inner state: sincerity attracts mercy, obstinacy leads to deprivation.

Checkpoint
What does the Imam say about the one who listens with the intention of seeking guidance?
A) He will be confused.
B) He will be guided.
C) He will be tested.
D) He will be misled.

Key Takeaway
Sincere intention, respect for scholars, and humility are keys to receiving divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to religious talks or read Islamic texts, what is the true intention of your heart? Are you genuinely seeking guidance, or are you looking for confirmation of your own views? Be honest: have you ever approached a lesson with pride or obstinacy, and what was the result?
?
The passage says the sincere seeker does not hate scholars and has no pride or envy. Is there a scholar or teacher you feel dislike toward? Is there any envy in your heart toward someone's knowledge or piety? How might that be blocking your own guidance?
?
You have been given the jewel of faith—or perhaps you are still seeking it. Examine your character: are you truthful in your intention? Are you humble? If you find pride or envy within, what concrete step will you take today to remove it from your heart?

The Danger of the Mutasannina (False Sunnis)
Warning against false claimants to Sunni identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imam's warning about those who falsely claim to be Sunni and attribute corrupt doctrines to the Salaf.

Core Idea
False affiliation to Ahl al-Sunna is a dangerous deception.

The Imam turns to a pressing issue of his time: people who outwardly claim to follow Ahl al-Sunna but actually propagate corrupt beliefs, staining the reputation of true scholars. He specifically warns about the 'mutasannina' – those who pretend to be Sunnis. They have no real share of the sunna except 'chatter of the tongue' and falsely attribute anthropomorphic views to Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, misleading others into thinking they follow him in creed as they do in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mutasannina
Read the Arabic text about the danger of these false claimants. Note the term 'mutasannina' and its description.
2
Identify the corrupt practice
The Imam says the Hashwiyya stained some Sunni imams with their corrupt doctrine. Find how they misrepresented Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
They attributed anthropomorphism to him because they followed him in fiqh.
3
Reflect on the harm
Consider why this is dangerous: it misleads people about true Sunni creed and slanders a respected imam.
The Imam emphasizes that they have no real connection to the Salaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه البديع في رحمة الله تعالى بالصادقين ، ومكره بالماكرين وقد نقل هذا عن الإمام ابن دهاق ؛ بأنَّ طالب الحقِّ بصدق نيَّة ، غيرَ المبغض للعلماء ، والذي ليس في قلبه كبر أو حسدٌ . . لا بدَّ أن يُنيلَهُ المولى جوهرة الإيمان ، والمعاند على العكس ، تلك الكلمة التي بسط القول فيها هي : ( ما استمع أحد بنية الاسترشادِ إلا اهتدى ، وما قصد أحد المعاندة إلا حرمَهُ الله تعالى الهدى ) - بيان خطورة المتسننة ؛ وهم الذي ينتسبون إلى أهل السنة والسلف الصالح ولا نصيب لهم من ذلك إلا ثرثرة اللسان ، وذلك في أبحاث التنزيه ، ومن ذلك قوله : ( وقد لطَّخَتِ الحشوية بهذا المذهب الفاسد بعض أئمة أهل السنَّةِ ، فربما نسبوه لأحمد بن حنبل رضي الله تعالى عنه ؛ إذْ هم مقلّدون له في الفروع ، فأوهموا أنَّهم كما تبعوه في الفروع تبعوه في العقائد ) - تأكيده على أنَّ من عرف مذهب أهل الحق فقد جمع بين الشريعة والحقيقة ، ويتجلى هذا جليّاً في ردَّ شُبه المعتزلة في مسألة خلق أفعال العباد ، والتحسين والتقبيح العقليين

Allah will surely grant him the jewel of faith, and the obstinate one is the opposite. That word which he elaborated is: 'No one listened with the intention of seeking guidance except that he was guided, and no one intended obstinacy except that Allah deprived him of guidance.' - Explanation of the danger of the 'mutasannina' (those who pretend to be Sunnis); they are those who claim affiliation to Ahl al-Sunna and the righteous Salaf but have no share of that except chatter of the tongue, and that is in the investigations of tanzih (transcendence). Among that is his statement: 'The Hashwiyya have stained some Imams of Ahl al-Sunna with this corrupt doctrine, so perhaps they attributed it to Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah be pleased with him; since they are his followers in furu' (branches), they gave the false impression that just as they follow him in furu', they follow him in 'aqa'id (creeds).' - His emphasis that whoever knows the madhhab of the people of truth has combined sharia and haqiqa, and this is clearly manifested in his refutation of the doubts of the Mu'tazila on the issue of the creation of human actions (khalq af'al al-'ibad) and rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyan).

Commentary
This passage presents three important insights from the Imam. First, his beautiful exposition on divine mercy and deception: he relates from Ibn Dahaq that the sincere seeker of truth who does not hate scholars and has no pride or envy will be granted the jewel of faith, while the obstinate one is deprived. The Imam states a key principle: whoever listens with the intention of guidance will be guided, but whoever intends obstinacy is deprived by Allah of guidance. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All who claim to be Sunni are genuine followers of the Salaf.
Correction: The Imam warns that some only 'chatter' and falsely attribute their own views to respected scholars like Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Checkpoint
How do the mutasannina mislead people about Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal?
A) By openly rejecting him.
B) By attributing their own corrupt creed to him because they follow him in fiqh.
C) By claiming he was not a Sunni.
D) By ignoring his teachings.

Key Takeaway
One must be cautious of those who claim Sunni identity but distort the creed of the Salaf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed someone's correctness in creed simply because they claim to follow the Salaf? Will you now commit to examining the actual content of their beliefs on tanzih, not just their label?
?
In what areas of your life do you merely 'chatter' about following the sunna without truly internalizing its teachings? What one step will you take this week to align your practice with your profession?

Unifying Sharia and Haqiqa Against Mu'tazila
Emphasis on integration of law and spiritual reality in Sunni theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imam's assertion that knowing the madhhab of Ahl al-Haqq integrates sharia and haqiqa, as exemplified in refuting Mu'tazila on human actions and rational ethics.

Core Idea
True knowledge of Sunni creed unifies law and spiritual reality.

The Imam concludes with a crucial methodological point: the madhhab of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) is not merely legal or spiritual—it synthesizes both. He demonstrates this through his refutation of Mu'tazilite doctrines on the creation of human actions and rational good and evil. This shows that authentic Sunni theology does not separate external observance (sharia) from inner reality (haqiqa) but integrates them seamlessly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sharia and haqiqa
Read the Arabic sentence emphasizing that knowing the madhhab of truth combines both. Then note the example of refuting Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the unity
The Imam says that the true madhhab unifies sharia and haqiqa. Think about how theological reasoning and legal compliance come together.
3
Examine the example
The refutation of the Mu'tazila on khalq af'al al-'ibad and tahsin wa taqbih 'aqli is the case study. See how the Imam uses rational arguments rooted in revelation.
This shows that haqiqa is not esoteric but the inner truth of sharia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه البديع في رحمة الله تعالى بالصادقين ، ومكره بالماكرين وقد نقل هذا عن الإمام ابن دهاق ؛ بأنَّ طالب الحقِّ بصدق نيَّة ، غيرَ المبغض للعلماء ، والذي ليس في قلبه كبر أو حسدٌ . . لا بدَّ أن يُنيلَهُ المولى جوهرة الإيمان ، والمعاند على العكس ، تلك الكلمة التي بسط القول فيها هي : ( ما استمع أحد بنية الاسترشادِ إلا اهتدى ، وما قصد أحد المعاندة إلا حرمَهُ الله تعالى الهدى ) - بيان خطورة المتسننة ؛ وهم الذي ينتسبون إلى أهل السنة والسلف الصالح ولا نصيب لهم من ذلك إلا ثرثرة اللسان ، وذلك في أبحاث التنزيه ، ومن ذلك قوله : ( وقد لطَّخَتِ الحشوية بهذا المذهب الفاسد بعض أئمة أهل السنَّةِ ، فربما نسبوه لأحمد بن حنبل رضي الله تعالى عنه ؛ إذْ هم مقلّدون له في الفروع ، فأوهموا أنَّهم كما تبعوه في الفروع تبعوه في العقائد ) - تأكيده على أنَّ من عرف مذهب أهل الحق فقد جمع بين الشريعة والحقيقة ، ويتجلى هذا جليّاً في ردَّ شُبه المعتزلة في مسألة خلق أفعال العباد ، والتحسين والتقبيح العقليين

This passage presents three important insights from the Imam. First, his beautiful exposition on divine mercy and deception: he relates from Ibn Dahaq that the sincere seeker of truth who does not hate scholars and has no pride or envy will be granted the jewel of faith, while the obstinate one is deprived. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharia and haqiqa are separate or contradictory; one can follow the law without inner truth or vice versa.
Correction: The Imam shows they are integrated in the true madhhab; the refutation of Mu'tazila demonstrates how theological reasoning supports both.

Checkpoint
What does the Imam say about the one who knows the madhhab of Ahl al-Haqq?
A) He has achieved spiritual purity.
B) He has combined sharia and haqiqa.
C) He has rejected the Mu'tazila.
D) He is a scholar of fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Authentic Sunni theology integrates both revealed law and rational understanding of reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself sometimes focusing on outward rituals while neglecting the inner reality of your faith, or vice versa? How does the Imam's claim challenge you to integrate both starting today?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on revelation? How does the Mu'tazila error remind you of the need to ground ethics in sharia?
?
The Imam says that understanding the true madhhab unifies law and reality. Is your own understanding of Islam fragmented? What one step can you take this week to bring your external practice and inner conviction into harmony?

Sanusi's Defense of Karamat and Critique of Deniers
Editorial summary of Sanusi's stance on karamat and a takfir ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that denying the possibility of karamat (saintly miracles) contradicts Allah's absolute power, and his criticism of those who label belief in specific karamat as kufr.

Core Idea
Denying karamat limits Allah's power; overpolicing them risks takfir.

Sanusi tackles a sensitive issue: the miracles of saints (karamat). Some deny them entirely, while others go so far as to declare belief in a particular karamat as disbelief. In this passage, Sanusi censures both extremes, affirming Allah's unlimited power and defending the integrity of the awliya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice how Sanusi frames his criticism: he first dismisses innovators who deny karamat, then expresses greater surprise at some Sunni jurists.
The passage includes a direct quote from Sanusi.
2
Identify Sanusi's two critiques
Sanusi criticizes two groups: (1) deniers of karamat from the people of desires and innovations, and (2) some fuqaha of Ahl al-Sunnah who declared belief in a specific karamat as kufr. Understand why he calls the second group more surprising.
3
Analyze the underlying principle
The core principle is that Allah's power is limitless. Denying karamat implicitly restricts what Allah can do through His saints. The takfir of those who believe in a karamat is even worse—it wrongly excludes believers from the fold of Islam.
This connects to the lesson's objective.
4
Reflect on the story of Ibrahim ibn Adham
The specific incident involves Ibrahim ibn Adham being seen simultaneously in Basra and Mecca on the same day. Consider why some jurists found this impossible and why Sanusi defends its permissibility.
Sanusi argues that such a miracle is possible by Allah's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشنيعه على نقلة أقوال لأعلام أهل السنة لم تثبت عنهم ، وهذه المسألة وإن ذكرها في شرح العقيدة الكبرى إلا أنَّه زادها بياناً هنا . توسيع قدرة الله تعالى في تجويز الكرامات على أيدي الأولياء ، وتشنيعه على من يستعظم مثل ذلك في حقهم ؛ حتى قال بعد نفي العجبِ من منكري الكرامة من أهل الأهواء والبدع : ( وإنَّما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة ؛ حيث قال فيما رُوي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنَّهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية ، وفي ذلك اليوم بمكَّةَ : إِنَّ مَنِ اعتقدَ جواز ذلك يكفرُ ! )
His criticism of those who transmit statements of the leading scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah that are not established from them, and this issue, although he mentioned it in Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra, he further clarified it here. Expanding the power of Allah in permitting karamat at the hands of the awliya, and his criticism of those who consider such things too great for them; until he said after denying wonderment at the deniers of karamat from the people of desires and innovations: 'Rather the wonderment is from some fuqaha of Ahl al-Sunnah, who said regarding what was narrated from Ibrahim ibn Adham, that they saw him in Basra on the day of Tarwiyyah, and on that day in Mecca: "Indeed whoever believes that this is permissible is a disbeliever!"'
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary of Sanusi's positions. It first notes that Sanusi criticizes those who transmit unauthenticated statements from Ahl al-Sunnah scholars. Then it mentions that Sanusi expanded on the matter of karamat (miracles of saints), emphasizing that Allah's power encompasses allowing such miracles at the hands of saints (awliya). Sanusi then criticizes those who find such miracles too astonishing for saints. He quotes Sanusi as saying that while he is not surprised at the deniers of karamat (who are people of desires and innovations), he is surprised at some fuqaha of Ahl al-Sunnah. These fuqaha, based on a story about Ibrahim ibn Adham appearing simultaneously in Basra and Mecca on the same day, declared that whoever believes such a miracle is possible is a disbeliever. The editor thus highlights Sanusi's defense of the permissibility of karamat and his rejection of those who label belief in them as kufr.

Key Terms
كرامة (karamat)
ولي (wali)
إبراهيم بن أدهم (Ibrahim ibn Adham)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying karamat is a sign of piety and protects tawhid.
Correction: Denying karamat actually limits Allah's power and contradicts the possibility of His acts through saints. Sanusi shows that such denial can lead to unjust takfir.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, who declared belief in the story of Ibrahim ibn Adham as kufr?
A) The people of desires and innovations
B) Some fuqaha of Ahl al-Sunnah
C) The general public
D) Sanusi himself

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the permissibility of karamat as an expression of Allah's power and criticizes both simplistic denial and reckless takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of extraordinary acts attributed to saints, does your heart instinctively shrink back in doubt, or do you see them as a window into Allah's limitless power? What does your reaction reveal about your true belief in His omnipotence? This week, whenever you encounter something seemingly impossible, pause and say 'Allah is capable of all things' and ask Him to increase your certainty.
?
Have you ever been quick to label someone's belief as deviant without first considering whether your judgment might actually confine Allah's power? The fuqaha in Sanusi's time declared takfir based on one story—could you be guilty of a similar hasty condemnation in your heart or speech? Before your next conversation about religious differences, pray for humility and a vaster view of Allah's mercy and power.
?
The lesson shows Sanusi not being surprised at the deniers of karamat, but being astonished at those who over-police them. Which category do you fall into more often—the one who dismisses the spiritual possibilities of Allah's grace, or the one who becomes a gatekeeper of orthodoxy? Today, examine your attitude: If you lean toward dismissal, repent and ask Allah to open your heart to His wonders. If you lean toward policing, repent of self-importance and ask for a heart that trusts Allah's wisdom over your own.

Proof that God is Self-Subsistent
Sanusi defines God's self-subsistence and provides proofs from eternality and impossibility of an accident carrying attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of God's self-subsistence and the logical proofs that establish it.

Core Idea
God's self-subsistence means He needs neither a place to dwell nor a cause to exist.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyām binafsihi). This attribute distinguishes God from all created beings, which either require a substrate (like accidents) or a specifier (like substances). In this passage, he presents two increasingly precise definitions and provides rational proofs for each.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two interpretations and the proofs that follow.
Notice the logical flow: definition, two interpretations, then proofs.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
Identify the first interpretation (no substrate) and the second, more specific one (no substrate and no specifier). Understand why the second excludes substances.
3
Follow the proofs
Notice the proof for no specifier (from God's eternality) and the two proofs for no substrate (from impossibility of accident carrying attributes and from absurd consequences).
The second proof for no substrate is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why God cannot be an accident. Consider the implications for the oneness of God.
This connects to the broader doctrine of tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أيضاً: أنْ يكونَ تعالى قائماً بنفسِهِ؛ أي: ذاتاً لا يفتقر إلى محلّ، ويستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ صفةً. ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَ قيامَهُ تعالى بنفسِهِ؛ باستغنائِهِ عن المحل والمخصص، وهو أخص مِنَ التفسيرِ الأَوَّلِ، ويُخرِجُ مشاركة الجوهر له في هذه الصفة. والدليل على استغنائِهِ تعالى عنِ المُخصّص: ما سبق مِنْ وجوب قدمِهِ تعالى وبقائه. وعلى استغنائِهِ عن المحلَّ: أَنَّهُ لو كانَ صفةً لاستحال اتصافُهُ تعالى بالصفاتِ المعنوية والمعاني؛ إذ الصفةُ لا تقوم بالصفةِ، ولأنَّهُ أيضاً لو كانَ صفةً لافتقر إلى محلّ يقومُ بهِ، ثم إنْ كانَ المحلُّ إلهاً مثل الصفة لزم تعدُّدُ الآلهة، وإنِ انفردَتِ الصفةُ بالألوهية وأحكامها لزم جوازُ قيام صفةٍ بمحل ولا يتّصفُ المحل بحكمها! وهو وأيضاً: فليسَ كونُ الصفةِ إلهاً بأولى مِنْ كون محلّها إلهاً محال.
And it is also obligatory: that the Most High be self-subsistent; i.e., an essence that does not need a substrate, and it is impossible that He be an accident. And among them are those who interpreted His being self-subsistent as His being free of need for a substrate and a specifier, and it is more specific than the first interpretation, and it excludes the participation of the substance (جوهر) in this attribute. And the proof for His being free of need for the specifier is what preceded from the obligation of His eternality and permanence. And for His being free of need for the substrate: that if He were an accident, it would be impossible for Him to be described with the spiritual attributes and meanings, because an accident does not subsist in an accident; and also because if He were an accident, He would need a substrate to subsist in. Then if the substrate were a god like the accident, then multiplicity of gods would follow; and if the accident alone were characterized by divinity and its rulings, then it would be permissible for an accident to subsist in a substrate without the substrate being characterized by its ruling—and that is absurd. Moreover, it is not more likely that the accident be a god than that its substrate be a god—impossible.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines God's being self-subsistent (قائم بنفسه) and gives two interpretations and their proofs. The first interpretation is simply that God is not in need of a substrate (محل) and cannot be an accident. The second, more specific interpretation adds that He also does not need a specifier (مخصص) to bring Him into existence. This second interpretation distinguishes God from substances (جوهر), which are also self-subsistent but need a specifier. Proof for no specifier: God is eternal and permanent. Proof for no substrate: (1) If God were an accident, He could not be described with spiritual attributes, because an accident cannot subsist in another accident. (2) If He were an accident, He would need a substrate; that substrate would either be also divine (leading to multiple gods) or the accident alone would be divine without the substrate, which is absurd because it is not more reasonable for the accident to be divine than its substrate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing self-subsistence with merely not needing a substrate, ignoring the need for a specifier.
Correction: The more specific interpretation includes independence from a specifier, which distinguishes God from substances that are self-subsistent but require a specifier.

Checkpoint
What is the more specific interpretation of God being self-subsistent?
A) That He does not need a substrate.
B) That He does not need a specifier or a substrate.
C) That He is eternal.
D) That He has no beginning.

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence means He depends on nothing—neither a place nor a cause—setting Him apart from all creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God needs nothing—no place, no cause. But do you live as if your existence depends on Him? Identify one created thing (a person, possession, or status) that you unconsciously rely on for security. What would it look like to turn to God alone for that need today?
?
The proof shows that God cannot be an accident because He must be described with attributes. Reflect: Have you ever pictured God as something limited, like an object or a being confined to a direction? Repent from that mental image and affirm with your heart that He is beyond all limitations.
?
Your own existence is caused—you depend on God for every moment. Do you ever act as if you are self-sufficient, even for a moment? What habit can you change today to remind yourself that you are sustained by Him alone?

God is Self-Subsistent and Not an Accident
Sanusi proves God's self-subsistence and refutes that He is an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi nafsihi) and the rational proofs that He cannot be an accident.

Core Idea
God cannot be an accident because that would require a substratum, leading to absurdities.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his proof of God's transcendence by establishing that Allah is self-subsistent (qā'im bi nafsihi) — independent of any substratum. He then demonstrates why it is impossible for Allah to be an accident (ṣifa) or property inherent in something else. The argument builds on the previously established impossibility of God being a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage begins mid-sentence about body parts, connecting to a prior point.
2
Identify the two interpretations of self-subsistence
Look for the statement about two interpretations: one simply 'independence from a substratum', and another more specific that adds 'independence from a specifier'. Understand why the second excludes substances (jawhar).
The second interpretation is more specific because substances also do not need a substratum, but they do need a specifier.
3
Understand the proof of independence from a specifier
Sanusi says the proof for independence from a specifier is based on God's necessary pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā') which were established earlier. Reflect on why something pre-eternal cannot have a specifier.
A specifier is something that determines existence at a particular time — impossible for the eternal.
4
Follow the reductio argument against being an accident
The proof for independence from a substratum is a reductio ad absurdum: if God were an accident, several impossible consequences follow. Trace the logic: accidents cannot have attributes, would need a substratum, leading to multiplicity of gods or absurdity.
Key point: an accident cannot subsist in another accident, so God could not have meaningful attributes.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that an accident being a god is no more appropriate than its substratum being a god, which is impossible. Conclude that God cannot be an accident.
This strengthens the doctrine of tawḥīd and God's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البطْنِ ، وخلفٌ مِنْ عوارضِ الظَّهِرِ ، ومَنِ استحالَ عليهِ أَنْ يكونَ جِرْماً .. استحالَ أنْ يتّصف بهذه الأعضاء ولوازمها على الضرورة .
ويجب أيضاً : أنْ يكونَ تعالى قائماً بنفسِهِ ؛ أي : ذاتاً لا يفتقر إلى محلّ ، ويستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ صفةً
ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَ قيامَهُ تعالى بنفسِهِ ؛ باستغنائِهِ عن المحل والمخصص ، وهو أخص مِنَ التفسيرِ الأَوَّلِ ، ويُخرِجُ مشاركة الجوهر له في هذه الصفة
والدليل على استغنائِهِ تعالى عنِ المُخصّص : ما سبق مِنْ وجوب قدمِهِ تعالى وبقائه .
وعلى استغنائِهِ عن المحلَّ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ صفةً لاستحال اتصافُهُ تعالى بالصفاتِ المعنوية والمعاني ؛ إذ الصفةُ لا تقوم بالصفةِ ، ولأنَّهُ أيضاً لو كانَ صفةً لافتقر إلى محلّ يقومُ بهِ ، ثم إنْ كانَ المحلُّ إلهاً مثل الصفة لزم تعدُّدُ الآلهة ، وإنِ انفردَتِ الصفةُ بالألوهية وأحكامها لزم جوازُ قيام صفةٍ بمحل ولا يتّصفُ المحل بحكمها ! وهو وأيضاً : فليسَ كونُ الصفةِ إلهاً بأولى مِنْ كون محلّها إلهاً محال
the abdomen, and khalf ('back') from the accidents of the back, and for whom it is impossible to be a body, it is impossible that he be characterized with these limbs and their necessities necessarily. And it is also necessary: that the Exalted be self-subsistent (qā'im bi nafsihi); i.e., an essence that does not depend on a substratum (maḥall), and it is impossible that He be an accident (ṣifa). And among them are those who interpreted His self-subsistence by His independence from substratum and specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ), and it is more specific than the first interpretation, and it excludes the substance (jawhar) from sharing this attribute with Him. And the proof of His independence from the specifier is what preceded of the necessity of His pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā'). And as for His independence from the substratum: if He were an accident, it would be impossible for Him to be characterized with the meaningful attributes (ṣifāt ma'nawiyya) and meanings (ma'ānī), because an accident does not subsist in an accident; and also because if He were an accident, He would depend on a substratum in which He subsists. Then if the substratum were a god like the accident, there would be a multiplicity of gods; and if the accident alone were characterized with divinity and its judgments, then it would be permissible for an accident to subsist in a substratum without the substratum being characterized by its judgment! And also: that the accident's being a god is not more appropriate than its substratum's being a god. Impossible.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi nafsihi) and a refutation of the notion that He could be an accident (ṣifa). The text begins with a statement that if something is impossible to be a body, it is necessarily impossible for it to have body parts and their requisites. This is a continuation of an earlier argument denying corporeality to God. Then the author states: 'It is also necessary that the Exalted be self-subsistent.' This means He is an essence that does not depend on a substratum (maḥall); He cannot be an accident because accidents require a substance to inhere in. Two interpretations of 'self-subsistent' are given: the first is simply independence from a substratum; the second adds independence from a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) as well, which is more specific and excludes substances (jawhar) from sharing this attribute, since substances also do not depend on a substratum but still need a specifier (i.e., a cause). The proof for independence from a specifier relies on God's necessary pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā'), which were established earlier. The proof for independence from a substratum is a reductio ad absurdum: if God were an accident, He could not have meaningful attributes (ṣifāt ma'nawiyya) because an accident cannot subsist in another accident. Moreover, if God were an accident, He would need a substratum. If that substratum were also a god, there would be multiple gods; if only the accident were divine, then the substratum would not share in divinity, which is absurd because the accident is dependent on the substratum. Finally, the author concludes that it is impossible for an accident to be God, as it would entail unacceptable consequences.

Correction: In kalam, 'self-subsistent' (qā'im bi nafsihi) means an essence that does not depend on a substratum at all, distinguishing God from accidents (which need a substratum) and also from substances (which need a specifier).

God is necessarily self-subsistent, meaning He depends on neither a substratum nor a specifier, and that impossibility of His being an accident safeguards His absolute oneness.

The passage argues that God cannot be an accident because that would lead to absurdities. What would it look like to let Him be the substratum, not the accident, of your daily decisions?

The Necessity and Attributes of Divine Power
Sanusi proves that God must possess eternal power related to all possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why God's power (qudra) must be an eternal, distinct attribute encompassing all possibilities.

Core Idea
God's power is necessary, eternal, distinct, and universal.

Sanusi begins a critical demonstration: that the Creator must possess power as a distinct, eternal attribute. This proof not only establishes a key divine attribute but also teaches how kalam reasoning works—by considering logical impossibilities if the opposite were true. As you read, notice the careful reductio ad absurdum arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own argument structure come through before you interpret.
The passage includes a chapter heading and the main argument.
2
Identify the conditions Sanusi imposes on power
Sanusi states several necessary conditions for God's power: it must exist (power as attribute), it is not identical to His essence, it is eternal, and it relates to all possibles. Find each condition and notice the supporting reasoning.
Each condition is defended by an argument showing the absurdity of its opposite.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum arguments
For the eternality condition, Sanusi provides two arguments: one from the impossibility of an eternal opposite (impotence) and the other from infinite regress. Grasp why these prove eternality.
This connects to the learning objective on rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الدليل على وجوب صفات المعاني ووجوب احكامها له تعالیٰ
ووجوب القدم والبقاء بجميعها، وما يقلق بذلك
وفيه فصول :
الفضل الأول
في وجوب القدرة وأحكامها
ويلزمُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدث العالم قادراً - وإلا لما أوجد شيئاً مِنَ العالم - بقدرة ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ قادر لا قدرة لهُ ، غيرِ مُتَّحِدةٍ بذاتِهِ ، وإلا لزم كون الاثنين واحداً ، وهو محالٌ لا يُعقَلُ ، قديمةٍ ، وإلا كانَ ضدُّها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً ، فلا ينعدم أبداً ؛ لما عرفتَ أنَّ القديم لا يقبلُ العدمَ ، فيلزم ألا يقدر أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد باستحالة ذلك ، وأيضاً : لو كانَتِ القدرة حادثة لاحتاجت في إحداثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل
ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة مُتعلقةً
بجميع الممكنات ؛
Chapter of the proof of the necessity of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī) and the necessity of their judgments (aḥkām) for Him, Exalted, and the necessity of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā') for all of them, and what pertains to that. And it contains sections: The first section (al-faḍl al-awwal) on the necessity of power (qudra) and its judgments. And it is also necessary that the Maker of the world be powerful (qādir) — otherwise He would not have brought into existence anything from the world — with a power (qudra); because a powerful one without power is inconceivable, not identical (muttaḥida) to His essence, otherwise two would become one, and that is inconceivably impossible; eternal (qadīma), otherwise its opposite — which is impotence ('ajz) — would be eternal, and would never cease to exist, because you know that the eternal does not admit non-existence, so it would follow that He would never be powerful, and His creations testify to the impossibility of that; and also: if power were originated (ḥāditha), it would need another power for its origination, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. And it is necessary that this power be related (muta'alliqa) to all possibles (mumkināt).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on the proof of the necessity of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī) and their judgments, specifically focusing on pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā') for all of them. The first section deals with power (qudra). The proof begins with the necessity that the Maker of the world (muḥdith al-'ālam) must be powerful (qādir), for otherwise He could not have created anything. This power must meet several conditions: First, it is a power (qudra) because a powerful entity without power is inconceivable. Second, this power is not identical to His essence (ghayr muttaḥida bi dhātihi); if it were identical, then the attribute and the essence would be one, which is impossible because an attribute is distinct from the essence. Third, it must be eternal (qadīma); if it were originated (ḥāditha), then its opposite, impotence ('ajz), would be eternal, and since the eternal cannot cease to exist, God would never be powerful — but His creations (maṣnū'āt) show this to be false. Additionally, if power were originated, it would need another power to bring it into existence, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Finally, this power must be related to all possible things (mumkināt), indicating God's omnipotence.

بقاء

ممكنات
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are identical to His essence.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that power is not identical to His essence; otherwise two would become one, which is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) Because God's essence is eternal
C) Because power cannot be created
D) Because the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's power is necessary, eternal, distinct from His essence, and encompasses all possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible—illness, financial ruin, broken relationships—do you truly believe that God's power encompasses every possibility, or does your heart secretly doubt? What can you do today to strengthen that belief in a concrete way?
?
The proof shows that God's power is not identical to His essence, but distinct and eternal. How does this humbling truth challenge your own tendency to rely on your own limited, temporary power? Identify one area of your life where you are trying to control things that only God can control, and commit to surrendering it to Him today.
?
Sanusi argues that if God's power were originated, it could vanish. Reflect on a time when you felt that God's help was absent or late. Did that experience weaken your trust in His power? How can you now anchor your faith in the fact that His power is eternal and never fades, even when you don't see it at work?

God Must Be Powerful with a Power Not Identical with His Essence
Sanusi states that God must be powerful with a power not identical with His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God must possess a power that is not identical with His essence, and why the alternative is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Power in God is necessary, distinct from His essence.

Sanusi begins the chapter by establishing the foundational proof for God's power. He argues that the creator of the world must be powerful, and that this power cannot be simply identical with His essence. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of Sanusi's argument.
2
Identify the two claims
Notice that Sanusi first argues God must be powerful, then argues that power is not identical with essence.
3
Analyze the non-identity argument
Understand why identity would lead to two things being one, which is impossible.
4
Review the commentary
Read the commentary to solidify your understanding of the rational necessity.

باب الدليل على وجوب صفات المعاني ووجوب احكامها له تعالىٰ ووجوب القدم والبقاء بجميعها، وما يقلق بذلك وفيه فصول : الفصل الأول في وجوب القدرة وأحكامها ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون محدث العالم قادراً - وإلا لما أوجد شيئاً من العالم - بقدرة ؛ لأنه لا يعقل قادر لا قدرة له ، غير متحدة بذاته ، وإلا لزم كون الاثنين واحداً ، وهو محال لا يعقل ، قديمة ، وإلا كان ضدها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً ، فلا ينعدم أبداً ؛ لما عرفت أن القديم لا يقبل العدم ، فيلزم ألا يقدر أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد باستحالة ذلك ، وأيضاً : لو كانت القدرة حادثة لاحتاجت في إحداثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة متعلقة بجميع الممكنات ؛ إذ لو تعلقت ببعضها دون بعض لاحتاجت إلى مخصص ؛ لاستوائها في حقيقة الإمكان ، فتكون حادثة ، وقد عرفت وجوب قدمها ، وإن فرض تخصيصها بغير مخصص لزم انقلاب الجائز مستحيلاً
Chapter on the proof of the necessity of the attributes of meanings and the necessity of their rulings for Him, Most High, and the necessity of eternity and everlastingness by all of them, and what pertains to that. And it contains sections: The first section on the necessity of power and its rulings. It is also necessary that the originator of the world be powerful - otherwise He would not have brought into existence anything of the world - with a power; because a powerful one without power is inconceivable, not identical with His essence, otherwise the being of two as one would be necessary, which is impossible and inconceivable; eternal, otherwise its opposite - which is incapacity - would be eternal, so it would never cease to exist; because you have known that the eternal does not admit non-existence; thus it would be necessary that He never be powerful, and His created things testify to the impossibility of that. Also: if power were originated, it would need another power to bring it into existence, and infinite regress would result. And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things; because if it were related to some and not others, it would need a particularizer due to their equality in the reality of possibility; thus it would be originated, and you have known the necessity of its eternity; and if its particularization were assumed without a particularizer, then the contingent would become impossible.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a chapter heading and a section presenting a proof for the necessity of God's power. The chapter heading announces the topic: proving the necessity of the attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني) for God, their rulings, and the necessity of eternity and everlastingness by them. Then the first section begins. The argument proceeds as follows: The creator of the world must be powerful with a power (قدرة) because otherwise he could not have created anything. This power cannot be identical with His essence, because that would make two things one, which is inconceivable. It must be eternal; if it were originated, its opposite (incapacity) would be eternal and never cease, and the created things show that God is not powerless. Also, if power were originated, an infinite regress would follow. Furthermore, this power must relate to all possible things; if it related only to some, a particularizer would be needed due to equality in possibility, making the power originated, contradicting its eternity. Assuming particularization without a particularizer would turn the contingent into impossible. Thus, God's power is necessary, eternal, non-identical with essence, and all-encompassing.

محدث العالم
صفات المعاني

Misconception: Some think God's power is simply His essence without distinction.
Correction: Sanusi argues that if power were identical with essence, then two distinct concepts would be one, which is rationally impossible. Power is an attribute, not the essence itself.

God must possess a power that is a distinct attribute, not identical with His essence.

How can you ensure your worship is directed to God as He truly is, with all His perfect attributes, rather than a mental image you've constructed?

The Eternity of God's Power
Sanusi proves the eternity of God's power by refutation of its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power must be eternal and why it cannot be originated.

Core Idea
If God's power were originated, His inability would be eternal.

Sanusi now argues that God's power must be eternal. If it were originated, its opposite—incapacity—would be eternal and never cease, which contradicts the observable fact that God creates. This lesson follows the logic of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Focus on the conditional structure: if not eternal, then opposite eternal.
2
Understand the consequence
If incapacity is eternal, it never ceases, so God would never be powerful.
3
Observe the refutation
Sanusi uses God's created things as evidence that He is powerful.
4
Reflect on the logic
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming power is originated leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الدليل على وجوب صفات المعاني ووجوب احكامها له تعالىٰ ووجوب القدم والبقاء بجميعها، وما يقلق بذلك وفيه فصول : الفصل الأول في وجوب القدرة وأحكامها ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون محدث العالم قادراً - وإلا لما أوجد شيئاً من العالم - بقدرة ؛ لأنه لا يعقل قادر لا قدرة له ، غير متحدة بذاته ، وإلا لزم كون الاثنين واحداً ، وهو محال لا يعقل ، قديمة ، وإلا كان ضدها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً ، فلا ينعدم أبداً ؛ لما عرفت أن القديم لا يقبل العدم ، فيلزم ألا يقدر أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد باستحالة ذلك ، وأيضاً : لو كانت القدرة حادثة لاحتاجت في إحداثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة متعلقة بجميع الممكنات ؛ إذ لو تعلقت ببعضها دون بعض لاحتاجت إلى مخصص ؛ لاستوائها في حقيقة الإمكان ، فتكون حادثة ، وقد عرفت وجوب قدمها ، وإن فرض تخصيصها بغير مخصص لزم انقلاب الجائز مستحيلاً
Chapter on the proof of the necessity of the attributes of meanings and the necessity of their rulings for Him, Most High, and the necessity of eternity and everlastingness by all of them, and what pertains to that. And it contains sections: The first section on the necessity of power and its rulings. It is also necessary that the originator of the world be powerful - otherwise He would not have brought into existence anything of the world - with a power; because a powerful one without power is inconceivable, not identical with His essence, otherwise the being of two as one would be necessary, which is impossible and inconceivable; eternal, otherwise its opposite - which is incapacity - would be eternal, so it would never cease to exist; because you have known that the eternal does not admit non-existence; thus it would be necessary that He never be powerful, and His created things testify to the impossibility of that. Also: if power were originated, it would need another power to bring it into existence, and infinite regress would result. And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things; because if it were related to some and not others, it would need a particularizer due to their equality in the reality of possibility; thus it would be originated, and you have known the necessity of its eternity; and if its particularization were assumed without a particularizer, then the contingent would become impossible.

The Universal Scope of God's Power
Sanusi argues against origination of power via infinite regress and for its universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's power encompasses all possible things and why it cannot be limited to some.

Core Idea
God's power extends to all possibles without exception.

Sanusi presents two additional arguments: first, that power cannot be originated because that would lead to infinite regress; second, that it must relate to all possible things, because limiting it would require a particularizer, which would imply origination. This lesson examines these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Notice the two separate arguments introduced by 'وأيضاً' and 'ويلزم'.
2
Analyze the infinite regress argument
Understand why origination requires another power, leading to an impossible chain.
3
Analyze the universality argument
See how equality of possibles forces a particularizer, implying origination.
4
Synthesize
Connect both arguments to the overall proof that God's power is eternal and all-encompassing.

The Necessity of God's Will and Refutation of the Nature Objection
Sanusi proves God's will necessary and refutes the nature objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must possess will (إرادة) to specify the world's existence and why the objection that nature or an obstacle suffices is invalid.

Core Idea
Without divine will, nothing in creation would be specified or exist at all.

Sanusi continues his proof of divine attributes by showing that the creator must be willing, i.e., intentional. He then refutes the idea that God could be a blind cause or nature, because that would make the world eternal or require an unexplained obstacle. This passage lays the foundation for understanding why will is an essential attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the author's flow come through.
The text cuts off at the end; focus on the full argument presented.
2
Identify the core argument for divine will
Notice how Sanusi argues that without intention (qasd), the world would remain non-existent. Find the key term 'takhṣīṣ' (specification).
This is the central proof: will is needed for particularization.
3
Understand the alternative and its implication
Sanusi considers the possibility that God's essence is a cause or nature. What would that imply about the world's eternity?
If God were a cause, the world would be eternal due to necessary conjunction.
4
Examine the objection and why it fails
The objection posits an eternal obstacle. Why does Sanusi reject this? The text cuts off, but the logic points to an infinite regress or unsupported assumption.
Think: what caused the obstacle to cease? This leads to a vicious circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في إثبات الإرادة وأحكامها ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون محدث العالم مريداً ؛ أي : قاصداً لفعله ؛ إذ لولا قصده لتخصيص الفعل بالوجود في زمن على مقدار مخصوص وصفة مخصوصة .. للزم بقاؤه على ما كان عليه من عدم ذلك كله أبد الآباد . فإن قُدِّرَت ذاته علة لوجود العالم ، أو موجدة له بالطبع ؛ حتى لا يحتاج في وجود العالم عنه إلى إرادة .. لزم حينئذ قدم العالم ؛ لوجوب اقتران العلة بمعلولها . والطبيعة بمطبوعها ، وقد عرفت وجوب حدوثه والاعتراض على هذا ؛ بأن صانع العالم طبيعة ، وإنما لم يوجد العالم معها في الأزل لوجود مانع أزلي منع من وجوده حينئذ ، فلما انتفى المانع فيما لا يزال أوجدت الطبيعة حينئذ العالم.. فاسد ؛ لأن هذا التقدير يستلزم ألا
The second section on establishing the will and its rulings. It is also necessary that the originator of the world be willing; i.e., intending His action; because if not for His intention to specify the action with existence in a time of a specific measure and specific attribute... then its remaining in its state of non-existence of all of that forever would be necessary. If His essence were determined as a cause for the existence of the world, or as a producer of it by nature, so that it does not need will for the existence of the world from Him, then the eternity of the world would be necessary due to the necessary conjunction of the cause with its effect, and nature with its natural product, and you have known the necessity of its origination. And the objection to this: that the maker of the world is a nature, and the world only did not come into existence with it in eternity due to the existence of an eternal obstacle preventing its existence then; so when the obstacle ceased in the future, nature then brought the world into existence... is invalid; because this supposition necessitates that...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage proves the necessity of God's will (إرادة) and refutes an objection. Sanusi first argues that the creator must be willing (مريد), i.e., intending His action, because without intention to specify the action with a particular time, measure, and attribute, the world would remain non-existent forever. Then he addresses the alternative: if God's essence were a cause or nature producing the world necessarily, the world would be eternal, but we know it is originated. Then he presents the objection: perhaps the maker is a nature, and an eternal obstacle prevented the world from existing initially; when the obstacle ceased, nature produced the world. Sanusi calls this invalid, because that supposition would necessitate... (the text cuts off). The argument seems to be that the obstacle itself would need explanation, leading to an infinite regress or a vicious circle. The key point is that will is necessary to explain particularization.

تخصيص
علة
طبيعة
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power or nature is sufficient for creation without will.
Correction: Sanusi shows that without will, there is no specification of time, measure, or attribute; nature would imply an eternal world or an unexplained obstacle, so will is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would be the consequence if God's essence were a cause for the world?
A) The world would be eternal due to the necessary conjunction of cause and effect.
B) The world would be contingent and require a will.
C) God would not be powerful.
D) The world would be created in time.

Key Takeaway
God's will is necessary to explain the particularization of the world; without it, the world would either be eternal or its existence would be left to unexplained obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that without God's specific will, nothing would exist at all. In your life, where do you act as if things happen by chance or nature, forgetting that every detail of your existence is intentionally willed by God? How can you shift your mindset to see His hand in every moment?
?
Sanusi argues that the objection of an eternal obstacle is invalid. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on 'obstacles' or excuses to explain why you haven't acted or changed? What obstacle are you hiding behind that you need to remove by turning to God's will?
?
If God's will is necessary for the world to be specified, then your very identity, time of birth, and circumstances are chosen by Him. How does this truth challenge your tendency to complain or feel dissatisfied with where God has placed you? What specific aspect of your life do you need to embrace as intentionally chosen by God today?

Proof that the Maker Must Possess Will
Sanusi proves the necessity of divine will and refutes two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maker of the world must possess will (irada) and how Sanusi refutes objections that God could be a cause or nature.

Core Idea
Without will, creation would be eternal or impossible; only a willing agent can create a temporal world.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of will (al-irada), showing that the Maker must intend His actions. He argues that if God were merely a cause or nature, the world would be eternal or never exist. Then he refutes objections that invoke pre-eternal obstacles or missing conditions, demonstrating that only a willing, choosing agent can account for the world's actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument. Note the logical flow from necessity of will to refutations.
The argument uses technical terms like علة (cause), طبيعة (nature), مانع (obstacle), شرط (condition).
2
Identify the main claim
Sanusi begins by stating that the Maker must be willing (مريد), otherwise the world would remain non-existent forever. This is the central thesis.
3
Understand the failed alternatives
He then considers what if the Maker were a cause (علة) or a nature (طبيعة). Both would force the world to be eternal, which contradicts its known temporality.
A cause necessitates its effect instantly; a nature produces its natural product constantly.
4
Examine the two objections and refutations
Two objections are raised: (1) a pre-eternal obstacle prevented creation; (2) a missing condition delayed creation. Sanusi refutes both: a pre-eternal obstacle cannot cease, and a missing condition leads to infinite regress.
The refutation relies on the impossibility of pre-eternal things ceasing to exist.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that the Maker is willing and choosing, not a cause or nature. Consider how this affirms divine will as a necessary attribute.
This passage is a classic kalam argument for divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الثاني في إثبات الإرادة وأحكامها ويلزم أيضاً أنْ يكونَ محدث العالم مريداً أي : قاصداً لفعله إذْ لولا قصده لتخصيص الفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ على مقدار مخصوص وصفة مخصوصة للزم بقاؤُهُ على ما كانَ عليهِ مِنْ عدمِ ذلكَ كَلِّهِ أَبدَ الآباد . فإِنْ قُدْرَتْ ذاتُهُ علَّةً لوجودِ العالم ، أو مُوجِدةٌ لهُ بالطبع حتى لا يحتاج في وجودِ العالم عنه إلى إرادة لزمَ حينَئِذٍ قدمُ العالمِ لوجوبِ اقتران العلَّةِ بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها وقد عرفت وجوب حدوثِهِ والاعتراض على هذا بأنَّ صانع العالم طبيعةٌ وإِنَّما لم يُوجد العالم معَها في الأزلِ لوجود مانع أزلي منعَ مِنْ وجودِهِ حينئذٍ فلما انتفى المانع فيما لا يزالُ أوجدَتِ الطبيعةُ حينَئِذٍ العالم فاسد لأنَّ هذا التقدير يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ العالم أبداً لأن مانعَهُ على هذا الفَرْضِ أَزلي فيستحيلُ عدمُهُ لما عرفْتَ أنَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحال عدمُه وكذا الاعتراض بأن الصانع طبيعة وتأخر العالم عنها في الأزلِ لتوقف وجودِهِ على شرط لم يُوجَدُ في الأزل فلمَّا وُجِدَ الشرطُ فيما لا يزالُ وُجِدَ العالم عنِ الطبيعة حينَئِذٍ فاسد أيضاً لأنَّ الكلام في حدوثِ ذلكَ الشرطِ وتأخُرِهِ عنِ الأزلِ كالكلام في العالم فيحتاج هو أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانع أزلي فيلزم ألا يُوجَدَ شرطُ العالم أبداً فلا يُوجَدَ العالم مشروطه أبداً أو تقدير شرط آخر حادث فيُنقلُ الكلامُ إليه ويلزم التسلسل فثبت بهذا أَنَّ مُوجِدَ العالم مريد مختار لا علة ولا طبيعة
The second chapter: On proving the will and its rulings. It is also necessary that the Maker of the world be willing, i.e., intending His action; for if not for His intention to specify the act of existence at a time of particular measure and particular description, it would remain as it was from non-existence of all that forever. If His essence were supposed to be a cause for the existence of the world, or a producer of it by nature, so that it does not need will for the world's existence from Him, then the world would necessarily be eternal because of the necessity of the cause's conjunction with its effect and nature with its natural product, and you have known the necessity of its temporal origin. The objection to this — that the Maker of the world is a nature, and the world only did not exist with it in pre-eternity because of the existence of a pre-eternal obstacle that prevented its existence then, and when the obstacle ceased in the future the nature then produced the world — is invalid, because this supposition entails that the world would never exist, since its obstacle on this assumption is pre-eternal, so its non-existence is impossible, as you have known that what is proven pre-eternal cannot cease. Likewise the objection — that the Maker is a nature, and the world was delayed from it in pre-eternity because its existence depended on a condition that did not exist in pre-eternity, and when the condition came to exist in the future the world came to exist from the nature then — is also invalid, because the discussion about the temporal origin of that condition and its delay from pre-eternity is like the discussion about the world, so it also needs the supposition of a pre-eternal obstacle, so the condition of the world would never exist, so the world conditioned by it would never exist; or the supposition of another temporal condition, and the discussion is transferred to it, leading to infinite regress. Thus it is proven by this that the Maker of the world is willing and choosing, not a cause nor a nature.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. Sanusi begins by stating that the Maker must be willing (murid), otherwise the world would remain non-existent forever. Then he considers two alternatives: if God were a cause ('illa) or a nature (tabi'a), the world would be eternal, which is false. Then he presents and refutes two objections: (1) that the Maker is a nature but a pre-eternal obstacle (mani') prevented creation; (2) that the Maker is a nature but a condition (shart) was missing. Both are refuted because the obstacle or condition would also be pre-eternal, leading to an infinite regress or impossibility. Therefore, the Maker is willing, not a cause or nature.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
علة
طبيعة
مانع
شرط
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God could create by nature or cause without will.
Correction: Sanusi shows that either scenario would make the world eternal, which contradicts its observed temporality.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the consequence of supposing that the Maker is a nature and that a pre-eternal obstacle prevented the world's existence?
A) The world would have existed eternally.
B) The world would never exist because the obstacle would be impossible to remove.
C) The obstacle would be temporal and removable.
D) The Maker would need will to remove the obstacle.

Key Takeaway
The Maker must be a willing agent; otherwise the world would be eternal or never exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if God's will was not involved in your life, treating events as mere causes or nature? What would change if you truly believed every moment is specified by His will?
?
This lesson shows that without divine will, the world would never exist or would be eternal. How does the fact that God intentionally created you at this specific time and with your specific qualities affect your sense of purpose and gratitude?
?
If God were a mere cause, you would be a necessary effect—no freedom, no relationship. But He is a willing Chooser who created you knowingly. How does this truth demand a personal response from you today?

Three Attributes of the Divine Will
Sanusi states that God's will is eternal, general to all possibles, and not for a purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three necessary attributes of God's will according to Sanusi: its eternity, its generality over all possible things, and its lack of purpose.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal, all-encompassing, and without need.

Sanusi now turns to the divine will, establishing three essential attributes that distinguish it from human will. This passage affirms that God's will is not limited by time, scope, or need. Understanding these attributes safeguards against misconceptions about God's sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the three attributes clearly.
Notice the logical connectors: 'ويلزم أن يكون' (it is necessary that).
2
Identify the three attributes
Pick out the three descriptions of God's will: eternal (قديمة), general (عامة) to all possible things, and not for a purpose (لا لغرض).
The passage mentions 'خيراً كانت أو شراً' (whether good or evil) to emphasize generality.
3
Understand the logical necessity
Ask: Why must God's will be eternal? Why must it be general? Why must it not be for a purpose? The passage gives a reason for the third: otherwise He would be deficient.
The attribute of power is referenced to for analogy: 'as you have known before concerning power'.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how these attributes protect God's perfection and self-sufficiency. They rule out any need or limitation in God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزم أن يكون ذلك بإرادة قديمة عامة في جميع الممكنات خيراً كانت أو شراً لما عرفت قبل في القدرة وأن تكون إرادته لا لغرض له وإلا لكان ناقصاً
And it is necessary that this be with an eternal will, general in all possible things, whether good or evil, as you have known before concerning power, and that His will not be for a purpose, otherwise He would be deficient.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage lists three necessary attributes of God's will: (1) it is eternal (qadima), (2) it is general (amma) in containing all possible things, both good and evil, as with power, and (3) it is not for any purpose (gharad), because that would imply deficiency. This emphasizes God's self-sufficiency and sovereignty.

Key Terms

عامة
غرض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will only applies to good things or has a purpose.
Correction: The passage affirms that God's will is general to all possibles (good and evil) and not for any purpose, as purpose would imply deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why must God's will not be for a purpose (gharad)?
A) Because purposes imply deficiency in the Creator.
B) Because God is not affected by creation.
C) Because will and purpose are synonymous.
D) Because purpose would require the will to be temporal.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal, all-encompassing, and without purpose, highlighting His absolute perfection and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is general to all things, including evil, and not for any purpose, does that unsettle you? Have you ever blamed God for allowing harm in your life? ?
You live as though your choices are outside God's will. But this lesson says His will encompasses all possibles—including your sins. Does that make you complacent or more accountable? Examine your heart: do you truly accept that even your worst moments are under His will, and that nothing escapes it? What must change in your attitude toward repentance?
?
The lesson proves God's will has no purpose because that would imply need. Do you treat God as if He needs your worship or obedience? When you pray, are you trying to persuade God to change His will? How can you worship Him purely as the Self-Sufficient, without expecting anything in return? Write one concrete way you will adjust your du'a today to reflect this truth.

The Divine Will: Eternal, Universal, and Purposeless
Sanusi derives necessary implications of the divine will: eternal, general, and purposeless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four necessary implications of God's will: its eternity, generality over all possibles, and its freedom from any purpose—whether for Himself or for creation.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal, universal, and entirely self-sufficient—unlike human will.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi established that the Creator is willing. Now he draws out what necessarily follows from that attribute. The divine will cannot be temporal, limited, or motivated by any need—either within God or from His creatures. This passage builds on the earlier discussion of divine power and sets the stage for later arguments about God's justice and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical connectors like 'ويلزم أن' and 'وإلا لكان' which signal necessary implications.
The passage is dense; take time to absorb each implication.
2
Identify the four implications
The passage presents four conclusions about God's will. Try to list them in order: eternal, general over all possibles, not for a purpose in Himself, not for a purpose in creation.
Each implication is supported by a brief reasoning.
3
Understand the reasoning for 'no purpose'
The text argues that if God's will were for a purpose, He would be deficient and perfected by His act—impossible. Reflect on why this reasoning is necessary for divine transcendence.
This connects to the attribute of self-sufficiency (al-ghina).
4
Consider the 'general will' and its implications
God's will covers all possible things, both good and evil. Reflect on how this does not make Him the author of evil—the moral quality is separate.
This will be clarified in later lessons on human action and divine decree.
5
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint question to check your grasp of the main point: Why can't God's will be for a purpose?
The answer should be based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزم أن يكون ذلك بإرادة قديمة عامة في جميعِ الممكنات، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً؛ لما عرفت قبلُ في القدرةِ، وأن تكونَ إرادتُهُ لا لغرض له، وإلا لكان ناقصاً في ذاتِهِ مُتكملاً بفعلِهِ، وذلكَ محال، ولا لغرض لخلْقِهِ وإلا وجب عليهِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم، وهو محال لما سيأتي.
And it necessarily follows that this is by an eternal will general over all possible things, whether good or evil; because you have known before regarding [divine] power; and that His will is not for a purpose, otherwise He would be deficient in His essence, being perfected by His act, and that is impossible; and not for a purpose of His creation, otherwise it would be obligatory upon Him to observe what is beneficial and most beneficial for them, and that is impossible as will come.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a set of necessary implications (لوازم) drawn from the previous proof that the Creator is willing. First, the will by which He creates must be eternal (إرادة قديمة), because the power is eternal, and the will is an attribute like power. Second, this will is general over all possible things (عامة في جميع الممكنات), both good and evil, because all possibles are equal before His power. Third, His will is not for a purpose (لا لغرض له), because if He willed for a purpose, He would be deficient in His essence and would need to be perfected by His act, which is impossible (محال). Fourth, He does not create for a purpose related to His creatures, because that would obligate Him to observe what is beneficial (الصلاح) and most beneficial (الأصلح) for them, which is impossible as will be explained later. The author thus establishes the transcendence of God's will from any need or purpose.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة
عامة
غرض
نقص
صلاح
أصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God's will is general over all possibles, He directly wills evil acts as evil.
Correction: The passage only says the will covers all possibles (good and evil) in terms of existence; the moral quality of evil is not willed for itself but as part of a larger order, as clarified elsewhere in kalam.

Checkpoint
Why can't God's will be for a purpose?
A) Because it would make Him dependent.
B) Because it would imply deficiency in His essence.
C) Because it would limit His power.
D) Because it would contradict His eternity.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal, all-encompassing, and absolutely self-sufficient—neither motivated by need nor obligated to anyone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly believe that God owes you something—that He should make your life easy because you worship Him? The passage says His will is not for any purpose of creation; He is not obligated to observe what is beneficial for you. How does this challenge your assumptions about what you deserve from God? Will you repent from expecting His favors as a right?
?
The text says God's will is eternal and not for a purpose that benefits Himself. This means He is completely self-sufficient. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge your utter dependence on Him in every moment? What would change in your daily routine if you truly believed He has no need of you?
?
God's will covers all possible things, both good and evil. When you see evil in the world, do you question His goodness or His will? The lesson separates the scope of will from moral approval. Can you accept that His will includes things you do not understand, and yet trust that He is wise and just? What specific situation do you need to surrender to His eternal will today?
