Divine Rulings Are Not Based on Purposes
Sanusi extends the principle that God's acts are purposeless to His legal rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians hold that God's legal rulings are not based on purposes and are known only through revelation.

Core Idea
God's commands are not caused by any purpose; they are pure divine choice.

Sanusi extends the principle of God's actions to His legal rulings. Just as God's will and acts are purposeless, so too are His commands. This passage explains the Ash'ari rejection of rational causation in divine law, asserting that only scripture can inform us of what is obligatory or prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Understand the parallel between action and ruling
Notice how Sanusi draws an analogy: just as God does not act for a purpose, His rulings are not for a purpose. All human actions are equally God's creation, so their moral status cannot be rationally deduced.
2
Grasp the implications for moral knowledge
The passage states that rulings are known only by revelation, leaving no scope for reason to determine divine law independently.
3
Interpret the summary on causal explanations
Be aware that any causal language in religious texts is interpreted metaphorically, not as attributing real causes to God's decrees.
This guards against thinking that God's commands have ulterior motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما استحالَ أنْ يريد سبحانه أو يفعل لغرض .. . كذلك استحالَ أنْ يكونَ حكمه على فعل بوجوب أو تحريم أو غيرهما مِنَ الأحكام الشرعيَّةِ لغرضِ مِنَ الأعْراضِ ؛ لأنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مستويةٌ في أنَّها خلقه واختراعُهُ ، فتعينُ بعضها للإيجاب وبعضها للتحريم أو غيره.. واقع بمحض الاختيار ، لا سبب له ولا مجال للعقل فيه أصلاً ، وإِنَّما يُعرَفُ بالشرع فقط. وبالجملة : فأفعالُهُ تعالى وأحكامه لا علة لها وما يُوجَدُ مِنَ التعليلِ لذلكَ في كلام أهل الشرعِ فَمُؤوَّلٌ بالأمارات أو نحوها مما يصح.
And just as it is impossible that He, exalted, wills or acts for a purpose, likewise it is impossible that His ruling on an action with obligation or prohibition or other than them from the legal rulings is for a purpose among accidental purposes; because all actions are equal in that they are His creation and invention; so specification of some for obligation and some for prohibition or other is by pure choice, having no cause and no scope for reason in it at all; it is known only by revelation. And in summary: His acts and rulings, exalted be He, have no cause, and whatever of causal explanations for that appears in the speech of the people of revelation is interpreted as referring to signs or the like that are valid.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage applies the same reasoning about God's actions to His legal rulings (الأحكام الشرعية). The author makes an analogy: just as it is impossible for God to will or act for a purpose, it is also impossible for His rulings—such as obligation (وجوب) or prohibition (تحريم)—to be for a purpose among accidental purposes (أغراض الأعراض). The reason given is that all human actions (الأفعال) are equal from God's perspective, as they are all His creation and invention (خلقه واختراعه). Therefore, the specification of some actions as obligatory and others as prohibited is purely by divine choice (محض الاختيار), with no cause (سبب) and no scope for reason (العقل) to determine it. Such rulings are known only through revelation (الشرع). Finally, the author summarizes that God's acts and rulings have no cause, and any apparent causal explanations in the speech of religious scholars (أهل الشرع) must be interpreted metaphorically (مؤول) as referring to signs (الأمارات) or similar indications, not as true causes. This underscores the Ash'ari principle that divine actions are not subject to rational causation.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي
وجوب
تحريم
اختيار
تعليل
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's rulings are based on divine wisdom that reason can uncover, making revelation unnecessary for moral knowledge.
Correction: The passage asserts that God's rulings have no cause (ʿilla) and are known only by revelation. Reason cannot determine divine law; any perceived wisdom is not a cause.

Checkpoint
How are God's legal rulings known according to this passage?
A) By reason and revelation together.
B) By revelation only.
C) By reason alone.
D) By consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
God's legal rulings are not based on purposes; they are known solely through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command from God that you cannot see any purpose for, does your heart accept it without hesitation, or do you find yourself searching for reasons before submitting? What does that reveal about your trust in His absolute sovereignty?
?
This lesson says God's rulings have no cause and reason has no scope in them. Are you willing to obey God's law even when your mind cannot understand its wisdom? What would you need to surrender to make that obedience genuine?
?
If God's commands are purely by His choice and not for any purpose, then your obedience is not about you gaining something or avoiding harm—it is purely about His right to command. Does that change how you approach your daily acts of worship? How will you remind yourself of this when you feel your devotion is unreciprocated or pointless?

The Necessity and Eternity of God's Knowledge
Sanusi introduces the third chapter on the necessity and eternity of God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that God is knowledgeable and that His knowledge is eternal, based on the evidence of creation and the nature of divine power.

Core Idea
The intricate design of the world proves that its Creator is all-knowing and eternal.

In this short but dense passage, Sanusi opens the third chapter on divine knowledge. He presents a concise argument linking the world's intricate design to the necessity of God's knowledge. He also clarifies that this knowledge is eternal and free from any external compulsion, grounding it in what was earlier established about divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the logical connectors (ويلزم, لما, لأن).
The phrase 'دقائق الصنع وعجائب الأسرار' is key.
2
Identify the argument for God's knowledge
Find the premise (the world contains subtle craftsmanship) and the conclusion (the Originator is knowledgeable). Ask: how does the premise support the conclusion?
This is the classic argument from design (دليل الصنعة).
3
Note the additional claims
Sanusi adds two more points: that this knowledge is eternal (by analogy with power) and that it is free from necessity (not compelled). Understand why these are important for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الثالث في وجوب علمه تعالى وما يتعلق به. ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم عالماً لما احتوى عليهِ العالمُ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع وعجائب الأسرارِ، وأنْ يكونَ ذلكَ بعلم قديم؛ لما سبق في القدرة، متنزه عن الضرورة.
The third chapter: On the necessity of His knowledge, exalted be He, and what pertains to it. And it necessarily follows that the Originator of the world is knowledgeable because of what the world contains of the subtleties of craftsmanship and wonders of secrets; and that that is by an eternal knowledge; because of what preceded regarding [divine] power; free from necessity.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (الفضل الثالث) dedicated to proving the necessity of God's knowledge and its related matters. The author states that it necessarily follows (ويلزم) that the Originator (محدث) of the world is knowledgeable (عالماً) because the world contains subtle craftsmanship (دقائق الصنع) and wonderful secrets (عجائب الأسرار). This is a classic argument from design: the intricate order and complexity of the universe point to a knowledgeable Creator. Furthermore, this knowledge must be eternal (علم قديم), similar to the divine power as previously established. Finally, this knowledge is described as free from necessity (متنزه عن الضرورة), meaning it is not compelled or constrained by anything external. The passage thus establishes both the existence and eternity of God's knowledge based on the evidence of creation and consistency with other divine attributes.

Key Terms
وجوب العلم
محدث العالم
دقائق الصنع
عجائب الأسرار
علم قديم
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the 'subtleties of craftsmanship' refers to specific scientific discoveries or modern intricate details like DNA or quantum mechanics.
Correction: It is a generic reference to the overall design and order in creation, not a specific set of facts. Sanusi uses it as a universal indication of a knowing Maker.

Checkpoint

A) Divine revelation.
B) The intricate design of the world.
C) Logical necessity alone.
D) Consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The world's intricate design proves that its Creator possesses necessary and eternal knowledge, free from any external constraint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's knowledge is eternal and free from any compulsion, then He knew every choice you would make before you existed—yet He still created you. Does that realization fill you with gratitude or fear? How should it change the way you approach your next decision?
?
The world is filled with 'wonders of secrets' that point to God's knowledge. Have you ever sincerely thanked Allah for the knowledge He has placed in creation, or do you treat it as ordinary? Write down one wonder you have experienced or seen, and form a concrete plan to praise Him for it today.

Necessity of God's Eternal Knowledge
Sanusi proves that God must be knowing with eternal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that Allah's knowledge is necessary, eternal, and encompasses all rational judgments.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is proved by the world's intricate design and must be eternal.

This passage forms the third chapter on divine attributes. Sanusi presents a concise proof that the Creator must be knowing, drawing evidence from the world's craftsmanship. He then establishes that this knowledge is eternal and free from human limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the compact style and logical connectors like 'li-mā' and 'wa-illā'.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
The passage asserts that the Originator must be knowing because of the world's intricate craftsmanship and wondrous secrets. Identify this causal link.
This is the core proof for knowledge.
3
Trace the logical connections to eternity
Sanusi says knowledge must be eternal 'because of what preceded regarding power'. Understand that this refers to an earlier argument that God's attributes are eternal.
4
Understand the negation of necessity and speculation
Sanusi denies that knowledge is acquired through necessity or reasoning. Consider why this would be problematic—if it were, it would entail harm or origination.
This guards against likening God's knowledge to human learning.
5
Relate to rational judgments and the particularizer
The passage ends by saying knowledge relates to all rational judgments, otherwise a particularizer would be needed. Reflect on how this completes the proof of comprehensiveness.
This ties back to earlier discussion of the particularizer (mukhassis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في وجوب علمه تعالى ، وما يتعلق به ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم عالماً لما احتوى عليهِ العالمُ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع وعجائب الأسرارِ ، وأنْ يكونَ ذلكَ بعلم قديم ؛ لما سبق في القدرة ، متنزه عن الضرورة والنظر ، وإلا قارنه الضرر ، أو كان حادثاً ، ويتعلق بجميعِ أقسام الحكم العقليّ ، وإلا لزم الافتقار إلى المخصص كما سبق .
The third chapter on the necessity of His exalted knowledge and what pertains to it. And it is necessary that the Originator of the world be knowing, because of what the world contains of intricacies of craftsmanship and wonders of secrets, and that this [knowledge] be eternal, because of what preceded regarding power, free from necessity and acquisition; otherwise, harm would accompany Him, or it would be originated, and it relates to all divisions of rational judgment; otherwise, the need for a particularizer would follow, as preceded.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God's knowledge. The author (presumably Sanusi) states the third chapter on the necessity of His knowledge. He argues that the Maker of the world (muhdith al-'alam) must be knowing ('aliman) because the world contains intricate craftsmanship and wondrous secrets that indicate a knowing maker. This knowledge must be eternal (qadim) based on the earlier argument about power (qudra). It is free from being acquired by necessity or by speculative reasoning (nazar), otherwise it would be accompanied by harm or would be originated (hadith). It encompasses all categories of rational judgment (hukm 'aqli), otherwise a need for a particularizer (mukhassis) would arise, as previously explained.

Key Terms
علم قديم
قدرة
حكم عقلي
مخصص
نظر
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is acquired or originated like human knowledge.
Correction: Divine knowledge is eternal and not acquired through necessity or speculation; otherwise, it would entail harm or origination.

Checkpoint
What does the intricate craftsmanship of the world prove about God?
A) That He is powerful.
B) That He is knowing.
C) That He is eternal.
D) That He is willing.

Key Takeaway
The world's perfect design proves that its Creator possesses eternal, comprehensive knowledge, free from human limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though God knows every intricate detail of your life—your secret thoughts, your hidden struggles—or do you act as if some things escape His notice? What would change if you fully believed in His all-encompassing eternal knowledge starting today?
?
When you look at the wonders of creation—your own body, the stars, the complexity of life—do you pause in awe, or have you become numb to the signs? What specific practice will you adopt to be more mindful of His craftsmanship?
?
The passage says God's knowledge is free from necessity and acquisition. Do you ever think of God's knowledge as if He learns or discovers things like you do? How would reframing that assumption change your trust in His decree, especially in moments of confusion or pain?

The Necessity of Divine Hearing and Sight
Sanusi states the necessity and definition of divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God must be described with eternal hearing and sight that encompass all existents.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight are eternal and relate to everything that exists.

Sanusi begins the fourth chapter by establishing that it is necessary for God to be hearing and seeing. He then describes these attributes as eternal and all-encompassing. This lesson unpacks the definition of divine hearing and sight as foundational attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

2
Identify the main claim

3
Understand the necessity

The Two Proofs: Reason and Revelation
Sanusi states the two proofs for God's attributes and prioritizes revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the evidence for God's attributes comes from both reason and revelation, with revelation being the stronger proof.

Core Idea
Reason and revelation both prove God's attributes, but revelation is the primary evidence.

Sanusi now presents the proof for the three attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. He cites two sources: rational necessity (impossibility of opposites) and transmitted revelation. He also notes that revelation is the stronger evidence, which sets the stage for a key position on perception.

Guided Path

2
Understand the rational proof
The rational proof is that it is impossible for God to have the opposite attributes (deafness, blindness, muteness).
This is an argument from impossibility of opposites.
3
Understand the priority of revelation
The text says 'وهو أولى' meaning revelation is more appropriate or stronger.
This leads to the next lesson on perception.

The Ruling on Perceiving God
Sanusi concludes with the suspension of judgment on perceiving God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correct position regarding perceiving God in the afterlife is suspension (waqf) due to lack of clear revelation.

Core Idea
We suspend judgment on perceiving God because revelation neither affirms nor denies it clearly.

In the final part of this chapter, Sanusi applies the primacy of revelation to the question of perception (idrak). Since revelation does not clearly affirm or deny that believers will perceive God (seeing with the eyes in the afterlife), the chosen position is to suspend judgment. This lesson explains the concept of waqf.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the sentence about 'المختار في الإدراك الوقف'.
2
Understand the reasoning
The reason for suspension is the lack of clear revelatory text ('عدم ورود النقل' ).
3
Connect to previous lesson
This shows the consequence of prioritizing revelation: where revelation is silent, we stop.

Proving God's Hearing, Sight, and Speech
Sanusi defines the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech and provides proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are eternal and unlike human attributes, and how reason and revelation prove them.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not letters or sounds.

Sanusi now turns to three essential divine attributes: hearing, sight, and speech. In this passage, he not only affirms these attributes but also clarifies their nature—especially God's speech, which is often misunderstood. He then presents the twofold proof from reason and revelation, and concludes with the scholarly position of suspending judgment on other forms of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Pay attention to the definitions of each attribute.
2
Identify the three attributes and their descriptions
Notice how hearing and sight are described as eternal and relating to every existent. Then note the longer description of speech: not letters or sounds, not renewed, etc.
Speech is given more detail because it is often misunderstood.
3
Understand the twofold proof
The passage says reason and revelation prove these attributes. Reason shows that their opposites are impossible for God. Revelation is the stronger proof.
4
Learn about suspension (waqf) on other perceptions
The author states that for other senses (touch, taste, smell), we suspend judgment because no scripture affirms or denies them.
This is a cautious approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في اثبات السمع والبصر والكلام، وما يتلق بذلك بسمع ويلزم أن يكون تعالى سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً ؛ وبصر قديمينِ مُتعلقين بكل موجود ، وبكلام قديم قائم بذاتِهِ ليس بحرفِ ولا صوتٍ ، ولا يتجدد ولا يطرأ عليه سكوت، ولا يتّصفُ بتقديم ولا تأخير، ولا ابتداء ولا انتهاء، ولا كل ولا بعض، ويتعلق بكل ما يتعلق به العلم. ويدلُّ على اتصافِهِ تعالى بهذه الثلاثة : العقل لاستحالة اتصافه بأضدادها ، والنقل ، وهو أَولى ، ومِنْ ثَمَّ كان المختارُ في الإدراك الوقف ؛ لعدم ورودِ النقل فيهِ بالإثبات أو النفي
Chapter four: On establishing hearing, sight, and speech, and what is connected to that. It is necessary that the Most High be Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking; His hearing and sight are eternal, relating to every existent; His speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, not a letter or sound, not renewed nor overtaken by silence, not characterized by precedence or delay, nor beginning nor ending, nor whole nor part, and it relates to everything that knowledge relates to. That He is described by these three is indicated by reason (al-ʿaql) — because it is impossible for Him to be described by their opposites — and by transmitted revelation (al-naql), which is more appropriate. Hence the chosen position regarding [other] perception is suspension (al-waqf), because no transmitted revelation has come regarding it either affirming or denying.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines and proves three essential divine attributes: hearing, sight, and speech. The author first states the necessary implication: God must be Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking. He then specifies that His hearing and sight are eternal attributes that relate to every existing thing. For speech, he emphasizes that it is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not composed of letters or sounds, not subject to renewal or silence, and free from temporal characteristics like precedence, delay, beginning, end, universality, or particularity. Speech relates to everything that knowledge relates to. The proof for these three attributes is twofold: first, reason, which shows that their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness) are impossible for God; second, transmitted revelation, which is the stronger proof. Finally, the author notes the position of suspension (al-waqf) concerning other modes of perception (like touch, taste, smell) because there is no scriptural evidence to affirm or deny them.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
متكلم
قديم
كلام نفسي
وقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is like human speech consisting of letters and sounds.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's speech is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds, not similar to human speech.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the proof for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech?
A) Reason alone
B) Reason and transmitted revelation, with revelation being more appropriate
C) Transmitted revelation alone
D) Consensus of the early Muslims

Key Takeaway
God's hearing, sight, and speech are eternal attributes; His speech is not composed of letters or sounds, and both reason and revelation confirm these attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's speech, do you ever imagine it as words like ours? How does this lesson challenge the way you conceive of God's attribute of speech? Will you strive to correct this mental image?
?
This lesson says God's hearing and sight encompass everything. Does this thought change how you act in private, knowing that nothing escapes His perception? What is one thing you might change because of this belief?
?
The author says that regarding other perceptions, we suspend judgement. Are you sometimes quick to affirm or deny matters about God without textual evidence? How can you adopt the attitude of waqf in areas where you lack knowledge?

Necessity of God's Life
Sanusi proves the necessity, eternity, and perpetuity of God's life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's life is a necessary attribute and how it serves as the condition for His other attributes.

Core Idea
God's life is the necessary foundation for all His attributes.

Sanusi begins by asserting that God must be living, because without life He could not possess the attributes of knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and speech. This life is not merely an attribute but a condition that must be eternal and perpetual. In this lesson, we explore the rational proof for the necessity and eternity of God's life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on God's life
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument step by step.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Sanusi argues that if God were not living, He could not be described with other attributes. Find the statement 'otherwise He would not be described with...' and see how life is a prerequisite.
3
Understand the eternity and perpetuity of life
Notice that Sanusi says life is eternal and perpetual because it is the condition for the eternal attributes. Ask yourself: why must the condition be eternal if the conditioned is eternal?
The key principle: the condition cannot be posterior to the conditioned.

The fifth section: On the necessity of His life, the Most High. And he establishes decisive proofs for the necessity of pre-eternity and perpetuity for all that the essence of our Lord, the Mighty and Sublime, is described with, and that He is far from being characterized by temporal events. It is necessary that the Most High be living, otherwise He would not be described with knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, or speech; with an eternal life because of what preceded about the necessity of pre-eternity of its condition, and it is impossible for the condition to be posterior to its conditioned, and it is necessarily perpetual, otherwise its pre-eternity would be negated, and you have now known its necessity. Likewise, pre-eternity and perpetuity are necessary for the rest of the attributes that subsist in His essence; because if they were susceptible to nonexistence, they would be temporal; because you have known that the eternal does not accept nonexistence, and it is impossible for the Most High to be described with a temporal attribute; otherwise His essence would have been receptive to it in pre-eternity, because its acceptance of it is inherent (dhātī). (1) And if it were also temporal for the essence... the essence would need acceptance. (1) His statement: 'its acceptance' — as in all the relied-upon copies — and the pronoun refers to the essence, because masculine and feminine are permissible with it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage establishes the necessity of God's life (ḥayāh) as a prerequisite for other attributes, and then extends the argument to prove that all divine attributes are eternal (qadīm) and perpetual (bāqī). The author first states that it is necessary for God to be living, because without life He could not be described with knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, or speech. This life is eternal, because the conditioned (attributes) are eternal, and the condition (life) cannot be posterior to the conditioned. Life also must be perpetual, otherwise its pre-eternity would be negated. Then the argument is extended to all attributes: they must be pre-eternal and perpetual. If they were subject to non-existence, they would be temporal. The fundamental principle is that the eternal does not accept non-existence. If any attribute were temporal, God's essence would have had to be receptive to it in pre-eternity, because acceptance of an attribute is inherent to the essence. The footnote clarifies that the pronoun in 'its acceptance' (qabūluhu) refers to the essence, and masculine or feminine pronouns are permissible.

Eternity and Perpetuity of Attributes
Sanusi proves that all divine attributes are eternal and perpetual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi extends the argument for eternity and perpetuity from God's life to all of His attributes.

Core Idea
All divine attributes are eternal and perpetual, just like God's life.

After establishing the necessity of God's life, Sanusi now argues that the same applies to all other attributes: they must be pre-eternal and perpetual. He uses the principle that the eternal does not accept nonexistence, and that God cannot acquire temporal attributes. This lesson explores that extension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the eternity of attributes
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Sanusi explicitly extends the ruling from life to 'the rest of the attributes'.
2
Understand the argument from impossibility of temporal attributes
Sanusi says if an attribute could cease to exist, it would be temporal. The eternal does not accept nonexistence. Also, if God had a temporal attribute, His essence would have been receptive to it in pre-eternity, which is impossible.
This is a key principle in kalam: the eternal does not accept nonexistence.
3
Reflect on the implication
This argument proves that all attributes of God are eternal and perpetual, reinforcing the doctrine of divine perfection.

The Necessity, Eternity, and Perpetuity of God's Life
Sanusi proves God's life is necessary, eternal, and perpetual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's life must be necessary, eternal, and perpetual, based on its being a condition for other attributes.

Core Idea
God's life is the necessary condition for all His other essential attributes.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of life. He argues that life is not optional—it is necessary for God to possess knowledge, power, and other attributes. But he goes further: this life must be eternal and perpetual, because the attributes it enables are eternal. This short but dense passage lays out a logical chain that students of kalam must grasp firmly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi moves from necessity to eternity to perpetuity in a single argument.
The passage is only a few lines, but each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
Find where Sanusi states that God must be living. Then trace his reasoning: if not living, then no attributes. Life is a condition, and the condition must be eternal because the conditioned (the attributes) is eternal.
The argument uses the concept of 'condition' (شرط) and 'conditioned' (مشروط).
3
Understand the eternity and perpetuity arguments
Sanusi says life must be eternal because its conditioned is eternal. Then he says it must be perpetual, otherwise its eternity would be negated. Ask yourself: why would ceasing to exist negate eternity?
Eternity here means without beginning; perpetuity means without end.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi ends by saying 'you have now known its necessity.' This confirms the lesson's goal: you should now be convinced that God's life is necessary, eternal, and perpetual.
Compare this with the misconception that life could be temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الخامس في وجوب حياته تعالى. ويلزم أن يكون تعالى حياً، وإلا لم يتصف بعلم ولا قدرة ولا إرادة ولا سمع ولا بصر ولا كلام، بحياة قديمة؛ لما سبق من وجوب قدم مشروطها، والشرط يستحيل تأخره عن مشروطه، واجبة البقاء، وإلا لانتفى قدمها، وقد عرفت الآن وجوبه.
The fifth section on the necessity of His life, exalted. It is necessary that the Exalted be living, otherwise He would not be described with knowledge, nor power, nor will, nor hearing, nor sight, nor speech, with an eternal life; because of what preceded regarding the necessity of the eternity of its conditioned thing, and the condition is impossible to be delayed from its conditioned thing; and it is necessary in perpetuity, otherwise its eternity would be negated, and you have now known its necessity.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's life being necessary, eternal, and perpetual. Sanusi begins by stating the topic: the necessity of God's life. He then argues: God must be living, because if He were not living, He could not possess the attributes of knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and speech. These attributes require life as a condition. Life, therefore, must be eternal because what is conditioned (the attributes) is eternal, and the condition (life) cannot be posterior to its conditioned. Furthermore, this life must be perpetual, for if it were not perpetual, its eternity would be negated (since something that ceases is not eternal). Sanusi concludes that the necessity of God's life is now established.

Correction: Sanusi argues that life must be eternal (without beginning) and perpetual (without end) because it is a condition for other eternal attributes, and the condition cannot be delayed or cease without negating the eternity of the conditioned.

Key Takeaway
God's life is necessary, eternal, and perpetual because it is the condition for His other essential attributes, which are themselves eternal.

?
God's life is necessary and perpetual—He cannot cease to exist. In what area of your life have you been acting as if God might 'die' or lose power? Confess that and realign your heart with the truth of His perpetual life.

Proof That All Attributes Are Eternal and Perpetual
Sanusi proves that all attributes of God are eternal and not contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every attribute subsisting in God's essence must be eternal and cannot be contingent, based on the logical impossibility of a contingent attribute being accepted in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
God's attributes cannot be contingent; they are all eternal by logical necessity.

Sanusi extends the reasoning from the previous lesson on God's life to all other attributes. He shows that if any attribute could cease to exist, it would be contingent—something impossible for God. The argument delves into the nature of acceptance: why God's essence cannot accept a contingent attribute even in pre-eternity, leading to a powerful conclusion about the eternity of all divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the source passage in Arabic and its literal translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind before analysis.
Pay attention to the key terms: قديم (eternal), حادث (contingent), قبول (acceptance), تسلسل (infinite regress).
2
Understand the initial premise
Sanusi first asserts that eternity is necessary for all attributes. He then gives the reason: if an attribute accepted non-existence, it would be contingent, but the Eternal cannot accept non-existence and cannot have a contingent attribute.
This is a direct application of the nature of the Eternal.
3
Follow the argument about acceptance in pre-eternity
Sanusi then considers a hypothetical: what if the essence accepted a contingent attribute? He shows that acceptance is essential (nafsi), so it would have to occur in pre-eternity. But that would mean the attribute is both contingent and eternal—a contradiction.
The infinite regress argument (if acceptance itself were contingent) reinforces the point.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The conclusion is clear: any attribute the divine essence accepts is eternal and necessary; what it does not accept in pre-eternity will never be accepted.
This establishes the perpetuity of all established attributes.
5
Test yourself with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you understand the core contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يجب القدم والبقاء لسائر الصفات التي تقوم بذاته تعالى؛ إذ لو قبلت العدم لكانت حادثة؛ لما عرفت أن القديم لا يقبل العدم، وهو تعالى يستحيل أن يتصف بصفة حادثة. وإلا لكانت ذاته قابلة لها في الأزل؛ لأن قبوله لها نفسي. ولو كان أيضاً حادثاً للذات لاحتاجت الذات إلى قبول آخر لذلك القبول، ويتسلسل. وإذا لزم أن يكون قبوله لتلك الصفة المفروضة الحدوث كائناً في الأزل صح أن يتصف بتلك الصفة الحادثة في الأزل؛ إذ لا معنى للقبول إلا ذلك، وذلك محال؛ إذ الحادث لا يمكن أن يكون قديماً، لأن من لازم القديم ألا يقبل العدم، والحادث قد قبل العدم واتصف به، فهما متنافیان. فخرج لك بهذا: أن كل ما قبلته الذات العليا من الصفات فهو أزلي واجب لها، لا يتصور أن يكون حادثاً، وما لم تقبله الذات العليا في الأزل فلا تقبله أبداً.
And likewise, eternity and perpetuity are necessary for the rest of the attributes that subsist in His essence, exalted; for if they accepted non-existence they would be contingent; because you know that the eternal does not accept non-existence, and the Exalted is impossible to be described with a contingent attribute. Otherwise, His essence would have been accepting of it in pre-eternity; because its acceptance of it is essential. And if it (the acceptance) were also contingent for the essence, then the essence would need another acceptance for that acceptance, leading to an infinite regress. And if it is necessary that its acceptance of that supposed contingent attribute existed in pre-eternity, then it would be correct that it be described with that contingent attribute in pre-eternity; for the meaning of acceptance is nothing other than that, and that is impossible; because the contingent cannot be eternal, since of the necessary properties of the eternal is that it does not accept non-existence, and the contingent has accepted non-existence and been described by it, so they are contradictory. There emerges for you from this: that every attribute which the supreme essence accepts is eternal, necessary for it, not conceivable to be contingent, and what the supreme essence did not accept in pre-eternity, it will never accept.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the eternity and perpetuity of all attributes that subsist in God. Sanusi extends the previous reasoning: just as life must be eternal, so too must all other attributes (power, will, etc.) be eternal. The proof: if an attribute accepted non-existence (i.e., could cease), it would be contingent (haditha). But the eternal cannot accept non-existence, and God cannot have a contingent attribute. Then Sanusi considers the possibility that an attribute could be contingent even though God's essence accepts it. He argues that if the essence were to accept a contingent attribute, that acceptance would have to occur in pre-eternity because acceptance is an essential property of the essence (nafsi). If the acceptance itself were contingent, that would necessitate an infinite regress of acceptances. Thus, if the essence accepts an attribute in pre-eternity, then the attribute would be described as contingent in pre-eternity, which is impossible because a contingent thing cannot be eternal (the eternal does not accept non-existence, while the contingent does). Therefore, the two are contradictory. The conclusion: every attribute that the divine essence accepts is eternal and necessary, and what it does not accept in pre-eternity will never be accepted.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
قبول
نفسي
تسلسل
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could acquire a new attribute or that some attributes might be contingent.
Correction: Sanusi proves that any attribute the divine essence accepts must be accepted in pre-eternity, making it impossible for a contingent attribute to be so accepted; thus all attributes are eternal and unchangeable.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God's essence were to accept a contingent attribute in pre-eternity?
A) The attribute would be eternal, which is impossible for a contingent thing.
B) The attribute would be temporal and created.
C) The essence would change its nature.
D) The acceptance would be eternal but the attribute contingent.

Key Takeaway
Every attribute that God's essence accepts is necessarily eternal and cannot be contingent, nor can any new attribute ever be acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are eternally perfect and can never change, what does that say about your own need to constantly seek knowledge of Him? Is your pursuit of knowing His attributes just a casual intellectual exercise, or is it urgent—because He is already complete and you are not?
?
The proof shows that nothing can be added to God. In your life, do you act as if you can add something to God by your worship or obedience? Or do you truly believe He needs nothing? Let this transform your worship from trying to fulfill His needs to humbly acknowledging your own.
?
Sanusi argues that what God did not accept in pre-eternity, He will never accept. Are there matters in your faith that you hope God will accept later even though you know they contradict His eternal attributes? Examine one such hope, and ask yourself: am I trusting in His mercy or ignoring His perfection?

Second Argument Against Contingent Attributes
Sanusi gives a second proof that God cannot have a contingent attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second argument that a contingent attribute in God would lead to the absurd consequence that God could be devoid of all attributes.

Core Idea
A contingent attribute in God would make possible His being devoid of all attributes.

Sanusi now presents a second argument that reinforces the impossibility of a contingent attribute in God by examining what would logically follow if such an attribute existed. This argument uses the principle of essential acceptance of attributes and the necessity of either possessing an attribute or its opposite or similar. The passage builds on previous knowledge about the impossibility of God being devoid of essential attributes like knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Note the conditional structure: 'if God were described with a contingent attribute...' This sets up the logical argument.
The passage is incomplete, but the argument's direction is clear.
2
Understand the logical consequence
Sanusi argues that if God could be devoid of a contingent attribute without having its opposite or like, then by the same reasoning He could be devoid of all attributes. Grasp why this is problematic.
This step is crucial for the argument's force.
3
Recall previous knowledge
The passage references earlier proof that God cannot be devoid of essential attributes like knowledge, power, will, and life. Ensure you recall this, as it grounds the argument.
Without this, the argument loses its foundation.
4
Identify the established principle
From the above, Sanusi deduces that every attribute God accepts is either perpetually present or replaced by its opposite or similar. Write this principle in your own words.
This principle is key to divine attribute theology.
5
Consider the incomplete conclusion
The passage cuts off as it begins to apply the principle to the contingent attribute. Reflect on what the conclusion likely is: the opposite of a contingent attribute would also be contingent, leading to an infinite regress or a contradiction.
This anticipation prepares you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً: لو اتصف تعالى بصفة حادثة لم يجز أن يعرى عنها، أو عن ضدها، أو عن مثلها، وإلا لجاز عروه عن جميع الصفات؛ لأن قبوله لها ذاتي لا يختلف، وقد عرفت فيما سبق استحالة عروه عن العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة. فثبت: أن كل ما يقبله من الصفات لا يعرى عنه إلا للاتصاف بضده أو مثله، لكن ضد تلك الصفة الحادثة
And also: if the Exalted were described with a contingent attribute, it would not be permissible that He be devoid of it, or of its opposite, or of its like; otherwise, it would be permissible for Him to be devoid of all attributes; because His acceptance of them is essential and does not differ; and you have known from what preceded the impossibility of His being devoid of knowledge, power, will, and life. Thus it is established: that every attribute He accepts, He is not devoid of it except by being described with its opposite or its like; but the opposite of that contingent attribute...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against contingent attributes in God. Sanusi argues: if God were described with a contingent attribute, it would be impossible for Him to ever be devoid of that attribute, its opposite, or its like. The reason is that if it were possible for Him to be devoid of it without acquiring an opposite or similar, then it would be possible for Him to be devoid of all attributes, because the acceptance of attributes is essential and does not vary. But we already know from previous discussions that it is impossible for God to be devoid of knowledge, power, will, and life. Therefore, it is established that every attribute He accepts, He is never devoid of it except when He is described with its opposite or its like. However, the opposite of that contingent attribute would itself have to be considered, leading to a difficulty. The text cuts off here, but the argument likely concludes that a contingent attribute would require an infinite chain or contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God could temporarily lack an attribute without contradiction.
Correction: Sanusi shows that essential acceptance necessitates perpetual possession or possession of its opposite or like; otherwise, God could be devoid of all attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, if God had a contingent attribute, why would it be impossible for Him to be devoid of it without having its opposite or like?
A) Because God’s acceptance of attributes is essential and invariable, so if He can be devoid of one, He could be devoid of all, which is impossible.
B) Because contingent attributes are always present by definition.
C) Because God cannot change.
D) Because the attribute is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's second argument shows that accepting a contingent attribute in God would logically require the absurd possibility of God lacking all attributes.

?

This lesson proves that God cannot be devoid of power or mercy. Let that truth confront your heart: do you live as if God's mercy is contingent on your behavior, or as an eternal attribute that never wavers? Commit to one concrete change in how you approach Him tonight.

The Proof That God's Attributes Are Eternal
Sanusi proves that all attributes God accepts must be eternal via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that any attribute God accepts must be eternal, and why temporality in God's attributes leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's attributes cannot be temporal; if they were, infinite regress would result.

Sanusi presents a rigorous proof that God's attributes are all eternal. He argues that if God were to accept a temporal attribute, that acceptance would have to be either in time or in eternity—both leading to contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the argument unfold in its own terms before analyzing it.
Notice the logical flow: if acceptance were in eternity, the attribute would be eternal (impossible); if in time, infinite regress.
2
Identify the contradiction
Identify the contradiction between temporal and eternal. The passage says the eternal cannot accept non-existence, while temporal things have accepted non-existence.
This contradiction is central to the proof.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that every attribute God accepts in eternity is eternal, and what He does not accept in eternity will never be accepted.
This means God's attributes are fixed and eternal.
4
Reflect on the significance
Reflect on why this matters: if God could acquire temporal attributes, He would be subject to change, which is incompatible with perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر لذلك القبول ، ويتسلسل • وإذا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ قبوله لتلك الصفة المفروضة الحدوثِ كائناً في الأزلِ.. صح أنْ يتّصف بتلك الصفة الحادثة في الأزلِ ؛ إذْ لا معنى للقبولِ إلا ذلك ، وذلك محال ؛ إذ الحادث لا يمكن أن يكون قديماً ، لأنَّ مِنْ لازم القديم ألا يقبل العدمَ ، والحادث قد قبل العدمَ واتَّصفَ بهِ ، فهما متنافیان فخرجَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ كلَّ ما قبلَتْهُ الذات العليَّةُ مِنَ الصفات فهو أزلي واجبٌ لها ، لا يتصوَّرُ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً وما لم تقبله الذاتُ العليَّةُ في الأزلِ فلا تقبله أبداً
The end of that acceptance and it would regress infinitely. And if it is necessary that His acceptance of that supposed temporal attribute be in eternity, then it would be correct that He be described by that temporal attribute in eternity; for acceptance means nothing other than that, and that is impossible because a temporal cannot be eternal, since from the necessity of the eternal is that it does not accept non-existence, while the temporal has accepted non-existence and been described by it, so they are contradictory. So it becomes clear to you by this: that every attribute that the Supreme Essence accepts is eternal, necessary for it, not conceivable that it be temporal, and whatever the Supreme Essence does not accept in eternity, it will never accept it ever.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction and infinite regress against the possibility of God acquiring temporal attributes. The author begins by stating that if acceptance of a temporal attribute were to occur in eternity, the attribute would have to be eternal, which is impossible because a temporal attribute cannot be eternal. He reasons that the eternal cannot accept non-existence, while temporal things have accepted non-existence, so they are opposites. Therefore, any attribute God accepts must be eternal, and any attribute not accepted in eternity will never be accepted.

قديم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could acquire a new attribute if He wills, because He is all-powerful.
Correction: This proof shows that acquiring a temporal attribute would lead to an infinite regress or a contradiction. Since God is perfect and free from change, His attributes are eternal and do not come into being.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can a temporal attribute not be considered eternal?
A) Because the eternal does not accept non-existence.
B) Because God is eternal.
C) Because temporal attributes are limited.
D) Because acceptance implies eternity.

Key Takeaway
Any attribute that God truly possesses must be eternal; temporal attributes cannot be ascribed to Him.

Why a Temporal Attribute Would Make God Temporal
Sanusi argues that a temporal attribute would force God to be temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God cannot possess a temporal attribute because it would necessitate His own temporality.

Core Idea
A temporal attribute's opposite is also temporal; God cannot be free from temporals, so He would be temporal.

Sanusi presents a second proof that God cannot have a temporal attribute. The argument uses a chain of reasoning: if God had a temporal attribute, He could never be free from either that attribute or its temporal opposite; being constantly accompanied by temporals would make God temporal, which contradicts His pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The author starts with 'if the Exalted were described by a temporal attribute...'. Identify what follows: the impossibility of being devoid of that attribute, its opposite, or its like.
This is the key move.
3
Understand the reasoning about opposites and likes
Why must God have either the attribute, its opposite, or its like? Because if He could be devoid of all three, then He could be devoid of all attributes, which is impossible for the essential attributes.
This connects to earlier premises about essential attributes.
4
Grasp why the opposite or like is temporal
The opposite or like of a temporal attribute is also temporal because it comes into existence after non-existence. The eternal does not cease to exist, so anything that cannot be free from temporals must itself be temporal.
This is the crucial inference.
5
Conclude the proof
Thus, if God had a temporal attribute, He would be temporal. But we know God is pre-eternal. Therefore, God cannot have a temporal attribute.
The proof is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : لوِ اتَّصف تعالى بصفة حادثة لم يجز أنْ يَعرى عنها ، أو عن ضدّها ، أو عن مثلها ، وإلا لجاز عروه عن جميع الصفاتِ ؛ لأنَّ قبوله لها ذاتي لا يختلفُ ، وقد عرفتَ فيما سبق استحالة عروه عن العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة . فثبت : أَنَّ كلَّ ما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ لا يعرى عنه إلا للاتصاف بضدّه أو مثلِهِ ، لكنَّ ضدَّ تلك الصفة الحادثة أو مثلها لا يكون إلا حادثاً ؛ بدليل طريانِ عدمِهِ ؛ إذ القديم لا ينعدم ، وما لا يعرى عن الحوادثِ يكون حادثاً ضرورةً ، فلزمَ أنَّهُ لو اتّصف تعالى بصفة حادثة لوجبَ حدوثه ضرورةً ، وقد عرفت وجوبَ قدمِهِ جلَّ وعلا .
And also: if the Exalted were described by a temporal attribute, it would not be permissible for Him to be devoid of it, or of its opposite, or of its like; otherwise it would be permissible for Him to be devoid of all attributes, because His acceptance of them is essential and does not differ. And you have known previously the impossibility of His being devoid of knowledge, power, will, and life. So it is established: that every attribute that He accepts, He is not devoid of it except for being described by its opposite or like. But the opposite of that temporal attribute or its like is only temporal, evidenced by the advent of its non-existence; for the eternal does not cease to exist, and whatever is not devoid of temporal attributes is necessarily temporal. So it necessarily follows that if the Exalted were described by a temporal attribute, His temporality would be necessary, and you have known the necessity of His pre-eternity, may He be glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is a second proof against temporal divine attributes. The author argues that if God were described with a temporal attribute, He could not be free from that attribute, its opposite, or its like at any time; otherwise He would be free from all attributes. But it is established that God cannot be free from essential attributes. Therefore, He must always have either the attribute, its opposite, or its like. However, the opposite or like of a temporal attribute is also temporal, because it comes into existence after non-existence. Since the eternal cannot cease to exist, anything that cannot be free from temporal things must itself be temporal. Hence, if God had a temporal attribute, He would be temporal, which is impossible given His pre-eternity.

Key Terms

ضد
مثل
قديم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could alternate between opposite temporal attributes without becoming temporal.
Correction: The proof shows that even alternation would require God to be never free from temporals (either the attribute, its opposite, or its like), which forces temporality.

Checkpoint
Why does the opposite or like of a temporal attribute have to be temporal?
A) Because it is also created by God.
B) Because it is subject to non-existence.
C) Because it is similar to the original.
D) Because it is necessary for balance.

Key Takeaway
A temporal attribute would force God to be temporal, contradicting His pre-eternity.

?
If God's attributes cannot be temporal, then His knowledge of your changing circumstances is not itself changing. How does this truth challenge the way you imagine God's awareness of your daily struggles? Do you ever speak to God as if He only knows part of your life?
?
The proof says that a single temporal attribute would make God temporal, stripping Him of His divinity. Considering that your own existence is filled with temporal attributes—your moods, your knowledge, your very life—does this realization humble you before the Eternal? How does it affect your understanding of your own dependency on Him?
?
Sanusi's argument forces us to confront the absurdity of attributing temporality to God. Yet, in your daily life, do you ever treat God's commands or promises as if they are temporary or subject to change? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God's attributes are eternal and unchanging, and to submit to that reality?

Third Proof: Temporal Attribute Implies Deficiency
Sanusi argues that a temporal attribute would imply a lack of perfection in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a temporal attribute would imply a deficiency in God, based on the consensus that God is described only with perfection.

Core Idea
Acquiring perfection later means lacking it earlier, which is deficiency.

Sanusi presents a third proof that God cannot have accidental attributes. The argument relies on the consensus that God is absolutely perfect. If a temporal attribute were to appear in God, it would have to be an attribute of perfection, but since it is 'new', it was not present in pre-eternity, implying God was missing that perfection at some point. This missing perfection is a deficiency, which is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the logic unfold naturally.
Notice the use of 'إجماع' (consensus) as a grounding premise.
2
Identify the key premises
Find three premises: (a) God is only described with perfection by consensus, (b) a supposed temporal attribute would have to be perfect, (c) being temporal means it was absent in pre-eternity.
3
Trace the conclusion
Understand that absence of perfection in pre-eternity implies deficiency, and God is free from deficiency by consensus.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if a temporal attribute existed, it leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فهو جلَّ وعزَّ لا يتّصفُ إلا بالكمال إجماعاً ، فيلزمُ في هذه الصفة الحادثة التي فُرِضَ اتِّصافُهُ تعالى بها أن تكونَ مِنْ صفاتِ الكمال ، وقد فاتَتْ ذاتَهُ العليَّةَ في الأزلِ ؛ لفَرْضِ حدوثِها ، وفوتُ الكمال نقص ، وهو تعالى مُنزَّه عنه بإجماع العقلاء
And also: He, may He be glorified and exalted, is not described except with perfection by consensus. So it follows that this temporal attribute which is supposed to be an attribute of His must be among the attributes of perfection, and yet it escaped the Supreme Essence in pre-eternity due to its being supposed temporal; and the loss of perfection is a deficiency, and He is exalted above that by the consensus of rational people.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This is a third proof: God is described only with perfection by consensus. If He were to acquire a temporal attribute, that attribute would have to be perfect. But being temporal means it was absent from God in pre-eternity, implying God lacked that perfection at that time. Lack of perfection is a deficiency, and God is transcendent above deficiency by consensus. Therefore, temporal attributes cannot be attributed to God.

Key Terms

نقص
أزل
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that acquiring perfection over time does not imply deficiency if it was not needed at that time.
Correction: But the consensus (إجماع) holds that God is perfect in every way eternally; any absence of perfection at any time is a deficiency incompatible with divine perfection.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim follows from God acquiring a temporal attribute of perfection?
A) That attribute is not real.
B) God lacked that perfection in pre-eternity.
C) The attribute is eternal.
D) God's perfection increases.

Key Takeaway
Temporal attributes cannot be attributes of God because any attribute that appears later would have been missing in pre-eternity, implying deficiency, which contradicts God's absolute perfection by consensus.

Refuting the Objection of Infinite Temporal Attributes
Sanusi refutes the objection of infinite temporal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that an infinite succession of temporal attributes could avoid deficiency in God.

Core Idea
An infinite regress of temporal events is impossible.

In this passage, Sanusi considers a potential objection to the idea that God acquires temporal attributes. The objection suggests that such acquisition does not imply deficiency because God could have an infinite series of similar attributes without beginning. Sanusi refutes this by showing the impossibility of an infinite regress of temporal events and the necessary uniqueness of each divine attribute. This lesson will guide you through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection proposes a possibility: God could be described by an endless chain of similar attributes, so no loss of perfection occurs.
The objection is introduced with 'ولا يُعترَضُ على هذا'.
2
Identify Sanusi's two-part answer
The refutation begins with 'لأنَّا نقولُ'. It has two arguments: first, the infinite regress is impossible; second, each attribute must be unique, otherwise absurdities follow.
3
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
Sanusi states that an infinite series of temporal events with no beginning ('حوادث لا أول لها') is manifestly impossible. Reflect on why a chain with no first cause is rationally unacceptable.
This is a standard kalam argument against infinite regress.
4
See the consequence of duplicate attributes
The second part argues that each attribute of God must be unique. Having two similar attributes would lead to 'اجتماع المثلين' (coexistence of likes) and 'تحصيل الحاصل' (acquisition of the already acquired), both impossible.
This reinforces the uniqueness and perfection of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُعترَضُ على هذا : بأَنَّهُ لا يلزم منه فوتُ الذاتِ العليَّةِ كمالُ هذه الصفة الحادثة ؛ لاحتمال اتصافه بأمثالها على التوالي لا إلى أوَّلِ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : لا يخفى أنَّ هذا الاحتمال باطل ؛ لأنَّهُ تسلسل مِنْ بابِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها وهو ظاهر الاستحالة . ويلزمُ أنْ تكونَ كلَّ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى واحدةً ، وإلا لزم اجتماعُ المثلين ، وتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو محال .
And one does not object to this: that the loss of the perfection of this temporal attribute does not necessarily follow, because of the possibility of His being described by its likes in succession without beginning. For we say: it is not hidden that this possibility is false, because it is an infinite regress of temporal events that have no beginning, and it is manifestly impossible. And it also follows that every attribute of God is one (unique), otherwise the coexistence of two likes and the acquisition of what is already obtained would be necessary, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: one might argue that if God acquires a temporal attribute, it does not imply deficiency because He could have an infinite succession of different temporal attributes without beginning. The author refutes this by stating that an infinite regress of temporal events without a first event is impossible. Additionally, the author concludes that each of God's attributes must be unique; having two similar attributes would mean unnecessary duplication and the acquisition of what is already possessed, which is impossible.

Key Terms
اعتراض
تسلسل
حوادث لا أول لها
اجتماع المثلين
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite succession of temporal attributes is possible for God without beginning.
Correction: An infinite regress of temporal events is impossible because it has no first cause, and duplication of attributes is also impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility of an infinite succession of temporal attributes?
A) Because God is eternal and cannot have attributes.
B) Because it would require an infinite regress of temporal events, which is impossible.
C) Because attributes cannot come in succession.
D) Because it would contradict God's unity.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of temporal events is rationally impossible, and each divine attribute is unique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Yet, do you live as if your own life is an endless series of chances to 'add' to your spiritual state, without ever facing a final reckoning? In what area are you procrastinating repentance as if time had no beginning? What step will you take today to break that cycle?
?
If each of God's attributes is one and unique, then His mercy is not increased by your deeds, nor His justice decreased by your sins. Does your heart truly rest in that unchanging perfection, or do you secretly believe you can tip the scales? How will you realign your trust in God's perfect attributes today?

The Possible for God and the Non-Obligation of Benefit
Sanusi defines the possible for God and proves the non-obligation of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'possible' in relation to God and why God is not obligated to create the most beneficial world.

Core Idea
God's action is purely voluntary; He is not bound to consider benefit for His creatures.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a foundational principle: God is not obliged to do what is best for His creation. This sets the stage for understanding divine acts as pure generosity. In this lesson, you will examine the definition of the possible (ja'iz) and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
Notice the opening statement about non-obligation of benefit.
2
Identify the definition of 'possible'
Find the sentence that defines 'ja'iz' and note what it says about obligation and benefit.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Sanusi argues that if benefit were obligatory, there would be no trial or obligation. Think about why this is a logical consequence.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite view of obligatory benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بار ما يجوز في حقه تعالى و بيان الدليل على عدم وجوب مراعاته تعالى الصلاح والاصلح وأن ما وقع من ذلك بمحض اختياره تعالى تفضلاً منه جل وعز [تـ] و بيان جواز رؤية تعالى و ما يتعلق بذلك وأما الجائز فهو كل فعل من أفعاله تعالى لا يجب عليه منه شيء ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح وإلا لما وقعت محنة دنيا ولا أخرى ولا تكليف بأمر ولا نهي ومن الجائزات رؤية المخلوق له تعالى في غير جهة ولا مقابلة إذ كما صح تفضله سبحانه بخلق إدراك لهم في قلوبهم يسمى العلم يتعلق به تعالى على ما هو عليه من غير جهة ولا مقابلة كذلك يصح تفضله تعالى بخلق إدراك لهم في أعينهم أو في غيرها يسمى ذلك الإدراك البصر يتعلق به تعالى على ما يليق به وقد أخبر بوقوع ذلك الشرع في حق المؤمنين في الآخرة فوجب الإيمان به والرؤية عند أهل الحق لا تستدعي بنية ولا جهة ولا مقابلة وإنما تستدعي مطلق محل تقوم به فقط
Chapter on what is possible for Him Almighty and explanation of the proof of the non-obligation upon Him Almighty of observing benefit and the most beneficial, and that what occurred of that is by His sheer choice as a favor from Him, Majestic and Exalted, [and] explanation of the permissibility of seeing Him Almighty and what pertains to that. As for the possible: it is every act of His Almighty, nothing is obligatory upon Him, and no observance of benefit or most beneficial, otherwise there would have occurred no trial in this world or the next, and no obligation of command or prohibition. And among the possibles is the vision of the created of Him Almighty without direction or confrontation; for just as His favor is valid by creating a perception in their hearts called knowledge that relates to Him Almighty as He is without direction or confrontation, so too is His favor valid by creating a perception in their eyes or in other [organs] called that perception sight that relates to Him Almighty in a manner befitting Him, and the Law has informed of its occurrence for the believers in the Hereafter, so belief in it is obligatory. And vision according to the people of truth does not require an organ, direction, or confrontation, but only requires an absolute locus in which it inheres.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on what is possible (ja'iz) for God. Sanusi first states the subject: explaining that God is not obliged to consider what is beneficial (salāh) or most beneficial (aslah) for His creatures; whatever occurs is by His sheer choice (tamahud ikhtiyār) as a favor. He then defines the possible: any act of God that is not obligatory upon Him, and no consideration of benefit is required; otherwise, there would be no purpose for trial or religious obligation. Next, he discusses a specific possible: the vision of God (ru'ya) by creatures in the Hereafter. He provides an analogy: just as God can create knowledge in hearts that relates to Him without direction or confrontation, so too can He create sight in eyes that relates to Him in a befitting manner. Since revelation (al-shar') has informed that this will happen for believers in the Hereafter, belief in it is obligatory. Finally, he clarifies that vision according to Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Ash'aris) does not require a physical organ (biniyya), direction (jiha), or confrontation (muqābila), but only an absolute locus (maḥall) in which it inheres, thereby refuting Mu'tazili objections.

Key Terms
جائز
صلاح
اصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures.
Correction: Sanusi argues that God is not obliged to observe benefit or most beneficial; otherwise, there would be no purpose for trials and obligations.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, if God were obligated to observe benefit, what would not occur?
A) Trial and obligation
B) Creation of the world
C) God's mercy
D) The afterlife

Key Takeaway
God is not bound to create the most beneficial world; His acts are purely by His choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not owe you a comfortable life or freedom from hardship, how should that reshape your response when trials come? Will you still trust His wisdom and mercy, or will you feel entitled to something better?
?
Do you sometimes live as if God must reward your good deeds with immediate ease? What would change in your worship if you truly believed that God's acts are a pure favor, not a wage you deserve?
?
When you see others suffering, do you secretly assume God owes them a better outcome? How does the truth that God is not obligated to create benefit for anyone affect your compassion and your view of divine justice?

The Possibility of Seeing God in the Hereafter
Sanusi proves the possibility of seeing God using analogy with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of seeing God and the refutation of anthropomorphic interpretations.

Core Idea
Seeing God is possible without direction or confrontation, just as knowing Him is possible without such limitations.

Sanusi now turns to a specific possible: the vision of God by believers in the Hereafter. He provides a powerful analogy between knowledge and sight to show that both can relate to God without corporeal constraints. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the analogy between knowledge and sight.
The phrase 'في غير جهة ولا مقابلة' is key.
2
Analyze the analogy
Compare how God creates knowledge in the heart without direction and how He can create sight in the eyes without direction.
3
Understand the Ash'ari position on vision
Note that vision does not require an organ or direction; it only requires a locus. This refutes the Mu'tazilite objection.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing God would mean God is physical or located in a direction.
Correction: Sanusi explains that vision can occur without direction or confrontation, just as knowledge does. It is a perception created by God in a locus.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Sanusi use to prove the possibility of seeing God?
A) God's power to create
B) God's creation of knowledge in hearts without direction
C) God's mercy
D) The resurrection

Seeing God is possible without direction or confrontation, and it is obligatory to believe in it based on revelation.

?

?
If you believe that God can be known without physical limitations, why do you often doubt things of the unseen that are promised? Where do those doubts come from, and what are you doing to uproot them?
?
The Ash'ari argument relies on what God is capable of, not on what you have experienced. Are you willing to let revelation override your limited human logic, even when it feels uncomfortable? What specific step can you take today to strengthen that surrender?
