The Meaning of 'Radiya Allahu Anhu' as Supplication
Sanusi analyzes the phrase 'Radiya Allahu anhu' and whether it is an attribute of action or essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi interprets the supplication 'Radiya Allahu anhu' as an attribute of action rather than an attribute of essence.

Core Idea
Supplication points to a future event, so the attribute must be an action, not an eternal essence.

The common phrase 'Radiya Allahu anhu' (May Allah be pleased with him) is often said as a supplication for the Companions. But what does it mean theologically? Is Allah's pleasure an eternal attribute or an action that occurs in time? Sanusi clarifies this distinction in a short but precise argument. Let's unpack it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and its literal translation. Notice how Sanusi presents two possible interpretations.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion is not given here.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Sanusi explains that 'Radiya Allahu' can be either an attribute of action (bestowing favor) or an attribute of essence (will to bestow favor). Mark these two.
3
Understand the key premise
Sanusi says that supplication is for a future matter not yet existent. This is the reason he favors the first interpretation. Why does that matter?
Attributes of essence are eternal, so they cannot be the object of a supplication.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many assume 'Radiya Allahu anhu' always describes an eternal attribute. But as a supplication, it must refer to a future act of bestowal.
This distinction is important for understanding divine attributes in general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ورضي الله تعالى عن أصحابِ رسولِ اللهِ أجمعينَ ) خبر بمعنى الدعاء ، و ( رضي الله تعالى ( إمَّا صفة فعل ; بمعنى : الإنعام ، أو صفة ذاتٍ ; بمعنى : إرادة الإنعام ، ويتعيَّن هنا الأوَّلُ ; لأنَّ الدعاء إنَّما يكون بمستقبل لم يُوجَدُ في الحال ، وإرادته تعالى
His saying: 'And may Allah the Exalted be pleased with all the Companions of the Messenger of God' is a statement meaning supplication. And 'may Allah the Exalted be pleased' is either an attribute of action, meaning bestowing favor, or an attribute of essence, meaning the will to bestow favor. And here the first is determined, because supplication is only for a future matter not yet existent at the time, and His will...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage explains the theological import of the common phrase 'Radiya Allahu 'anhu' (May Allah be pleased with him). Sanusi first states that when said as a supplication (duʿāʾ), it is a statement expressing a wish. He then analyzes the divine attribute of being pleased (riḍāʾ): it can be either an attribute of action (ṣifat fiʿl) meaning the actual bestowal of favor, or an attribute of essence (ṣifat dhāt) meaning the eternal will to bestow favor. In the context of supplication for the Companions, the first interpretation is more appropriate because supplication is directed toward a future event that has not yet occurred in the present. If it were an attribute of essence, it would be eternal and not subject to supplication. The passage ends mid-argument, likely continuing on a missing page.

صفة ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Radiya Allahu anhu' always describes an eternal attribute of Allah's essence, meaning His pleasure is without beginning or end.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that when used as a supplication, it refers to a future event of bestowal, making it an attribute of action, not an eternal attribute of essence.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi interpret 'Radiya Allahu anhu' as an attribute of action rather than essence when used as a supplication?
A) Because attributes of action are more honorable
B) Because supplication is for a future event not yet present
C) Because all divine attributes are actions
D) Because the Companions were imperfect

Key Takeaway
When used as a supplication, 'Radiya Allahu anhu' refers to a future act of Allah bestowing favor, an attribute of action, not an eternal attribute of essence.

If Allah's pleasure is an action He can grant, are you actively seeking His pleasure through your deeds, or do you passively assume it is already given? What specific action can you take today to increase your hope for His pleasure?
?
This passage shows that even a common phrase carries deep theological implications. Are you conscious of the meanings behind the words you use in worship, or do you let habit dull your awareness? What step will you take to be more deliberate in your supplications?

Understanding Divine Firming (Tathbit) in This Life and the Hereafter
Explains the verse on strengthening believers in this life and the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what divine firming (tathbit) means both in this life and the afterlife according to the interpretation of the verse.

Core Idea
Divine firming is inner knowledge and certainty, not external protection.

This lesson unpacks the meaning of 'tathbit' in the Quranic verse about strengthening believers. The author distinguishes between firming in this world—where Allah creates knowledge and certainty—and firming in the next world—where the believer speaks according to that knowledge. Understanding this distinction clarifies the nature of divine support for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author defines 'tathbit' in two contexts: this life and the afterlife.
2
Identify the two meanings of firming
Find the phrases that explain firming in the life of this world and in the Hereafter. Compare them.
In this life, it's internal (knowledge); in the afterlife, it's external (speech).
3
Connect to the resurrection principle
The passage ends with a statement about resurrection. Consider why this supports the interpretation of firming in the afterlife.
The servant is raised upon what they died upon, so firming in life affects the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في معنى قوله تعالى : ( يُثَبِّتُ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا بِالْقَوْلِ الثَّابِتِ ، فِي الْحَيَوةِ الدُّنْيَا وَفِي الْآخِرَةِ [إبراهيم : ۲۷] : ( لا معنى لتثبيت الله تعالى في الحياة الدنيا إلا خلق معرفة الحق في القلب ببرهانه حصولُ اليقين بإيمانِهِ ، والتثبيتُ في الآخرة لا معنى له إلا النطقُ على نحو ما يعرفُه ؛ لأنَّ العبد على ما مات عليهِ يُبْعَثُ
And he said concerning the meaning of the saying of the Most High: 'Allah firms those who believe with the firm word in the life of this world and in the Hereafter' [Ibrahim 14:27]: 'There is no meaning to the firming of Allah Most High in the life of this world except creating the knowledge of truth in the heart by its proof, the attainment of certainty in his faith; and the firming in the Hereafter has no meaning except speaking according to what he knows, because the servant is raised upon what he died upon.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage provides an interpretation of the Quranic verse about Allah strengthening believers with a firm word. The author explains that in this life, divine firming means that Allah creates in the believer's heart knowledge (ma'rifa) of the truth supported by its proof, resulting in certainty (yaqin) of faith. In the afterlife, firming means that the believer will speak in accordance with the knowledge they had, because a person is resurrected based on the state they died upon. This underscores the importance of having true knowledge and certainty in this life, as it directly affects one's state in the grave and resurrection.

Correction: The author clarifies that firming in this life is internal: creating knowledge of truth in the heart with proof, resulting in certainty.

Checkpoint

B) Creating knowledge of truth in the heart with its proof.
C) Speaking eloquently about faith.
D) Receiving worldly blessings.

Key Takeaway
Divine firming in this life is the creation of knowledge and certainty, and in the afterlife it manifests as speech consistent with that knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When trials strike, do you feel the inner firming of knowledge and certainty, or do you seek only external solutions? How can you remind yourself that true tathbīt begins inside—with knowing and being certain of the truth—and then manifest it in your actions today?

Ibn Abd al-Salam's Justification for the Superiority of Gnostics
Ibn Abd al-Salam explains why knowers of Allah are better than knowers of rulings, with proof and fruits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Abd al-Salam argues that knowledge of Allah is superior to knowledge of rulings, based on the principle that knowledge is honored by its object and the spiritual fruits it produces.

Core Idea
Knowledge is honored by its object; knowing Allah yields the highest honor and fruit.

Ibn Abd al-Salam was asked to justify the claim that the knowers of Allah ('arifun) are superior to the scholars of law. In his response, he lays out a clear argument: knowledge derives its worth from what it knows, and since Allah is the most noble object, knowledge of Him is the most noble. He then shows how this knowledge produces transformative spiritual states that knowledge of rulings does not. This passage is a concise defense of the primacy of theology and spirituality over legalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: statement of agreement, principle, fruits, evidence.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the phrase 'because knowledge is honored by the honor of its object' and understand how this principle elevates knowledge of Allah above all else.
This is the key logical move in the argument.
3
List the spiritual fruits
Ibn Abd al-Salam enumerates specific fruits that come from knowing different divine attributes. Identify each attribute and its corresponding fruit.
For example, knowing mercy yields hope, knowing punishment yields fear, etc.
4
Consider the empirical evidence
The author supports his claim by pointing to reality: many scholars of rulings have fallen into sin or doubt. This shows the superiority of gnostics in practice.
This is an inductive argument based on observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل عن ذلك عز الدين بن عبد السلام رحمه الله تعالى، فقال: (أما تفضيل العارفين بالله تعالى على العارفين بأحكام الله تعالى.. فقول الأستاذ والغزالي فيه متفق؛ لأن العارفين بما يجب الله تعالى من أوصاف الكمال، وبما يستحيل عليه من النقصان.. أفضل من أهل الفروع والأصول؛ لأن العلم يشرف بشرف المعلوم، والعلم بالله تعالى وصفاته أشرف؛ لأن معلومه أشرف المعلومات، وثمرته أفضل الثمرات. فمن عرف سعة رحمة الله تعالى أثمرت معرفته سعة الرجاء، ومن عرف شدة النقمة أثمرت معرفته شدة الخوف، وأثمر خوفه الكف عن الفسوق والعصيان، مع البكاء والأحزان، والورع والإذعان. ومن عرف أن جميع النعم منه تعالى أحبه، وأثمرت المحبة آثارها، ومن عرف تفرده بالنفع والضر لم يعتمد إلا عليه، ومن عرف عظمته عامله بالتعظيم والانقياد، ولا شك أن معرفة الأحكام لا تورث شيئاً من ذلك. ويدل على هذا الوقوع؛ فإن الفسق فاش في كثير من العلماء بالأحكام، بل اشتغل كثير منهم بأقوال الفلاسفة؛ فمنهم من خرج عن الدين، ومنهم من شك، فهم في ريبهم يترددون.)
He ('Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam) was asked about that and said: 'As for preferring the knowers of Allah over the knowers of Allah's rulings... the statement of al-Ustadh and al-Ghazali is agreed upon; because the knowers of what is necessary for Allah of attributes of perfection, and what is impossible for Him of deficiency, are better than the people of furu' and usul; because knowledge is honored by the honor of its object, and knowledge of Allah and His attributes is nobler, because its object is the noblest of known things, and its fruit is the best of fruits. Whoever knows the vastness of Allah's mercy, his knowledge bears the fruit of vast hope; whoever knows the severity of punishment, his knowledge bears the fruit of intense fear, and his fear bears the fruit of refraining from sin and disobedience, with weeping and sorrow, and piety and submission. Whoever knows that all blessings are from Him loves Him, and love bears its fruits; whoever knows His uniqueness in benefit and harm relies only on Him; whoever knows His majesty treats Him with magnification and submission; and there is no doubt that knowledge of rulings does not produce any of that. And this is evidenced by reality; for transgression is widespread among many scholars of rulings, rather many of them occupied themselves with the sayings of the philosophers; some of them left the religion, and some of them doubted, so they waver in their doubt.'
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is an argument and proof for the superiority of the gnostics ('arifun) over scholars of fiqh and usul. It opens with a statement that the authorities (al-Ustadh al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali) agree on this point. The core reason is that knowledge derives honor from its object: since Allah is the most noble object of knowledge, the knowledge of Him is the most noble. The passage then enumerates the fruits of this knowledge: hope from knowing His mercy, fear from knowing His punishment, which leads to piety; love from knowing His blessings; reliance from knowing His sole control; and reverence from knowing His majesty. These are the practical outcomes that knowledge of rulings does not produce. Finally, the argument is supported by empirical observation: many scholars of rulings are sinful or have fallen into doubt. This conclusively shows the superiority of the gnostics.

العارفين بأحكام الله
أهل الفروع
أهل الأصول
يشرف بشرف المعلوم
الرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge of rulings is inherently inferior.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is not inferior in itself, but it does not produce the same spiritual fruits as direct knowledge of Allah.

Checkpoint

B) Because knowledge is honored by the honor of its object
C) Because knowledge of rulings is forbidden

Key Takeaway
The superiority of gnostics over jurists is based on the principle that knowledge is honored by its object, and the transformative spiritual fruits that follow from knowing Allah.

The Obligation of God's Unity and the Sufficiency of Scriptural Proof
Sanusi asserts the necessity of God's oneness and that scriptural evidence suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi asserts that God's oneness is obligatory and how the Sunna and consensus suffice to prove that God alone is eternal.

Core Idea
Scriptural proof can stand alone to establish God's oneness.

Sanusi opens a methodological door: the oneness of God is not only a rational necessity but is also affirmed by the prophetic tradition and scholarly consensus. He argues that even without rational proof of God's divinity, the Sunna and consensus have already established that God alone is eternal and everything else is created. This lesson explores that statement and its implications for theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the opening assertion 'وجوب الوحدانيَّة' – the obligation of oneness.
This is a strong doctrinal statement.
2
Understand the conditional structure
Notice the phrase 'إذا لم يكن إلهاً' – 'if He were not a god'. Sanusi is setting up a scenario where rational proof is lacking.
The conditional is hypothetical, not suggesting doubt.
3
Recognize the role of Sunna and consensus
The passage says that the Sunna and consensus have indicated God's uniqueness in pre-eternity (qadīm) and that everything else is originated (ḥādith). This shows scriptural evidence is sufficient.
Sunna and ijmā' are authoritative sources.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Understand 'qadīm' (pre-eternal) and 'ḥādith' (originated) as they are used here. They are foundational concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الوحدانيَّة لمولانا جل وعزَّ ، وإذا لم يكن إلهاً فقد دلَّتِ السنَّةُ والإجماع على انفرادِ مولانا جل وعزَّ بالقدم ، وأنَّ كلَّ ما سواه فهو حادث ، وحدوث هذا الزائدِ لا يَتوقَّفُ ثبوتُ الشرع على معرفتِهِ ، فلا يمتنع الاستدلال بأدلَّةِ الشرع عليهِ
The obligation of oneness for our Lord, Majestic and Exalted; and if He were not a god, then the Sunna and consensus have indicated our Lord's uniqueness in pre-eternity, and that everything other than Him is originated, and the origination of this additional [attribute] does not depend on knowledge of it for the establishment of the Shari'a, so it is not prohibited to use the proofs of the Shari'a for it.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement asserting the necessity of God's oneness and defending the use of revealed evidence. It begins by stating that God's oneness is obligatory. It then argues that even if one did not have rational proof of God's divinity, the Sunna (prophetic tradition) and consensus (ijmā') have sufficed to prove that God alone is eternal (qadīm) and everything else is originated (ḥādith). The passage then addresses a specific issue: the createdness of an additional attribute (al-zāʼid). It clarifies that knowledge of this attribute's createdness is not a prerequisite for the validity of the Shari'a; therefore, it is permissible to use scriptural proofs (adillat al-shar') for it. This methodological point safeguards the use of revelation in theological reasoning.

قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must always have rational proof before accepting revealed evidence.
Correction: This passage shows that revealed evidence can stand alone to prove God's oneness.

Checkpoint

A) That God is not a god
B) That God alone is eternal and everything else is created

Key Takeaway
The Sunna and consensus suffice to prove God's oneness and that He alone is eternal.

The Permissibility of Using Scriptural Proof for the Origination of Attributes
Sanusi argues that the createdness of the additional attribute does not hinder the use of scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that knowledge of an attribute's createdness is not required for the Shari'a to be valid, and therefore it is permissible to use scriptural proofs for it.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs are valid even without prior rational proof.

Sanusi continues with a subtle methodological point: the fact that a particular attribute is created (ḥādith) does not need to be known for the Shari'a to be established. In other words, you don't have to prove that every attribute is created before you can rely on revelation. This prevents a vicious circle and affirms the autonomy of scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase 'حدوث هذا الزائد'
Read the Arabic and translation. 'al-zā'id' refers to an additional attribute, likely something beyond essence.
This phrase is the focus of the second part.
2
Understand the negative condition
The passage says the origination of this attribute does not depend on knowledge of it for the establishment of the Shari'a.
The Shari'a is valid regardless.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, it is not prohibited to use the proofs of the Shari'a for it. Scriptural proof is legitimate.
This is a safeguard against requiring prior rational proof.
4
Reflect on the methodological significance
Consider why this argument is important: it prevents an infinite regress where you need rational proof for everything before using revelation.
Sanusi protects the role of revelation in theology.

The First Guidance: Purification of the Heart
Ibn Dihaq explains the first guidance as heart purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first guidance from Allah involves an internal purification of the heart from pride, envy, and hatred for scholars, leading to a sincere search for truth.

Core Idea
Guidance begins with a purified heart, not just intellectual acceptance.

Ibn Dihaq, commenting on the divine name Al-Hadi (The Guide), explains that true guidance is not merely intellectual but begins with a profound inner transformation. He describes the first stage of guidance as Allah expanding the chest for Islam by cleansing the heart of spiritual diseases. This passage sets the foundation for understanding how divine guidance operates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full context. Notice how Ibn Dihaq defines the first guidance before elaborating on its meaning.
2
Identify the three heart maladies
Locate the three negative traits that Allah removes from the heart: pride (kibr), envy (ḥasad), and hatred (bughḍah) for scholars. Understand why these are obstacles to guidance.
These are spiritual diseases that block receptivity to truth.
3
Understand the posture of submission
Reflect on the statement 'I will look; if I see truth, I will follow it.' This shows a humble, open attitude contrasted with obstinacy. Note the warning for hardened hearts.
Sincere listening leads to guidance; obstinacy leads to deprivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن دهاق في شرح الإرشاد في تفسير اسمه تعالى ( الهادي ( بعد كلام كثيرٍ : ( فأوَّلُ هداية العبدِ : أَنْ يشرحَ الله تعالى صدرَهُ للإسلام ، ومعناه : أنْ يُذهِبَ اللهُ تعالى مِنْ قلبِهِ الكِبْرَ والحسد والبغْضَةَ للعلماء الداعين إلى الله تعالى ، فإذا استسلم قال : أنظرُ ، فإن رأيتُ حقاً اتبعته ، فهو على نورٍ مِنْ ربَّهِ ، فويل للقاسية قلوبُهم مِنْ ذكرِ اللهِ ، ولذلك قيلَ : ما استمعَ أحدٌ بنية الاسترشادِ إلا اهتدى ، وما قصد أحد المعاندة إلا حرمَهُ الله تعالى الهدى .
Ibn Dihaq said in the commentary on al-Irshad, in interpreting His name 'Al-Hadi', after lengthy discussion: 'The first guidance of the servant is that Allah expands his chest for Islam, and its meaning is that Allah removes from his heart pride, envy, and hatred for the scholars who call to Allah. Then when he submits, he says: "I will look, and if I see a truth I will follow it." So he is upon light from his Lord. So woe to those whose hearts are hardened against the remembrance of Allah. Therefore it was said: No one listens with the intention of seeking guidance except that he is guided, and no one intends obstinacy except that Allah deprives him of guidance.'
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical commentary on the divine name 'Al-Hadi' (The Guide). Ibn Dihaq explains that the initial stage of divine guidance is an internal transformation: Allah expands the believer's chest (sharḥ al-ṣadr) for Islam, which involves purging three spiritual maladies: pride (kibr), envy (ḥasad), and hatred (bughḍah) towards the scholars who call to Him. Once the heart is purified, the person adopts a posture of submission, stating that he will examine truth and follow it. This state is described as being upon light from his Lord. Conversely, those with hardened hearts are warned. The passage concludes with a proverb emphasizing that sincere listening leads to guidance, while obstinacy results in deprivation.

Why God Must Be Eternal and Permanent
Sanusi proves God's eternity and necessary permanence via rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for two of God's negative attributes: that He has no beginning (eternity) and no end (necessary permanence).

Core Idea
God's eternity and necessary permanence are proven by the impossibility of infinite regress and the contingency of anything that can cease.

Sanusi now turns to the negative attributes (سلوب), which describe what God is not. This lesson presents the rational proof that the Creator must be eternal (no beginning) and necessarily permanent (no end). The argument shows that if God had a beginning, an infinite regress would follow; and if He could cease to exist, His existence would be contingent, contradicting His eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage is part of the discussion on negative attributes. Read the opening line to see that Sanusi is proving two specific attributes: وجوب القدم and وجوب البقاء.
Negative attributes are a key category in Islamic theology.
2
Follow the proof for eternity
Read the part about قِدم. Sanusi argues that if the Creator had a beginning, He would need an originator, leading to either infinite regress or circularity. Both are impossible, so He must be eternal.
3
Follow the proof for necessary permanence
Read the part about بقاء. Sanusi argues that if the Creator could cease, His existence would be contingent, requiring a cause. But since He is proven eternal, He cannot be contingent; therefore He must be necessarily permanent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على الصفات العدمية ( السلوب )
الدليل على وجوب قدمه جل و عز و وجوب بقائه ثم يجب أن يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم قديماً ؛ أي : لا أوَّليَّةَ لوجودِهِ ، وإلا لافتقر إلى مُحدِثِ ، ويلزم التسلسل فيؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له ، أو الدَّورُ ، فيؤدي إلى تقدم الشيء على نفسِهِ ، وكلاهما مستحيل لا يُعقَلُ
ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ واجبَ البقاء ؛ أي : لا آخريَّة لوجودِهِ ؛ إذْ لو قَبلَ أنْ يلحقه العدمُ لكان وجوده جائزاً لا واجباً ؛ لما عرفتَ أنَّ حقيقة الواجب : ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ، وهذا الوجود قد فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ يقبلُ العدمَ ، فيكون جائزاً ؛ إذ الجائز : ما يصح فيه الوجود والعدم ، والجائز يستحيل أن يقع بلا سببٍ ، فيحتاجُ إذاً هذا الوجود الجائز إلى سبب فيكونُ مُحدَثاً ، وقد قام البرهان على وجوب قدمِهِ
فإذاً ؛ فَرْضُ عدمِ وجوب البقاء في كل ما قام البرهانُ على وجوب قدمِهِ .. تناقض لا يُعقَلُ .
The chapter of discourse on the negative attributes (the negations). The proof of the necessity of His eternality, may He be glorified and exalted, and the necessity of His permanence. Then it is necessary that the Originator of the world be eternal, i.e., there is no beginning to His existence; otherwise He would need an originator, and infinite regress would follow, leading to an endless chain, or circularity, leading to a thing preceding itself, and both are impossible and not rationally conceivable. And it is necessary that He be necessarily permanent, i.e., there is no end to His existence; because if non-existence could overtake Him, then His existence would be contingent (ja'iz) not necessary (wajib); since you know that the reality of the necessary is that which non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect, and this existence has been assumed to admit non-existence, so it would be contingent; for the contingent is that in which both existence and non-existence are valid, and it is impossible for the contingent to occur without a cause. So this contingent existence would need a cause, and thus it would be originated. But the proof has already been established for the necessity of His eternality. Therefore, assuming the non-necessity of permanence for anything for which the proof of the necessity of its eternality has been established is an inconceivable contradiction.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a proof for two of God's negative attributes: that He is eternal (no beginning) and necessarily existent (no end). Sanusi states that the Originator of the world must be eternal, meaning no beginning to His existence. If He had a beginning, He would need an originator, leading to either an infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), both of which are impossible. Then he proves that He must also be necessarily permanent (wajib al-baqa'), meaning no end to His existence. If He could cease to exist, then His existence would be contingent (ja'iz), because the necessary being is defined as that whose non-existence is inconceivable. A contingent being requires a cause for its existence, and if this being were contingent, it would be originated (muhdath). But it has already been proven that He is eternal (i.e., unoriginated). So it would be a contradiction to assume that something eternal could be contingent. Therefore, God must be both eternal and necessarily permanent.

صفات عدمية
سلوب
وجوب القدم
وجوب البقاء
التسلسل
الدور
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal only means having no beginning; God could cease to exist.
Correction: In this proof, 'eternal' logically implies necessary permanence; if God could cease, He would be contingent and thus originated, contradicting His eternity.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that the Originator of the world must be necessarily permanent (wajib al-baqa')?
A) Because if He could cease, He would need a cause to continue, making Him originated.
B) Because the world would cease to exist if He ceased.
C) Because permanence is logically prior to eternality.
D) Because all agents are permanent.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity and necessary permanence are proven by the impossibility of infinite regress and the contingency of anything that can cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi proves that if God could cease to exist, He would be contingent and thus need a cause. In what area of your life do you act as if God’s permanence is uncertain, seeking security elsewhere? How does this proof call you to trust His eternal nature alone?
?
The argument shows that assuming non-permanence for an eternal being is a contradiction. Is there any belief or action in your life that implicitly contradicts God’s eternal and permanent nature? What will you change today to align your heart with this truth?
?
If God’s existence were not permanent, what would that mean for your hope in the afterlife and His promises? How does Sanusi’s logical proof strengthen your confidence in God’s faithfulness to His word?

Two Meanings of Pre-Eternity (Qidam)
Sanusi enumerates two meanings of qidam and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'qidam' and why the first meaning (temporal succession) cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
God's pre-eternity is not endless time but existence without beginning.

Before proving that God is pre-eternal, Sanusi clarifies what 'qidam' actually means. He distinguishes a common, temporal sense from the theological sense. This distinction prevents confusion about God's relationship to time. As you read, note how the argument hinges on time being a property of created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that Sanusi first mentions two attributes, then focuses on pre-eternity.
2
Identify the two meanings of qidam
Find where Sanusi states the two meanings: (1) succession of times, (2) the theological meaning (implied as necessary existence without beginning).
Only the first meaning is explicitly defined; the second is understood from the refutation.
3
Understand why the first meaning is impossible
Follow the reasoning: God's existence is not temporal; time is an attribute of originated things. Time itself is either a relation between originated events or the movements of spheres—both originated. Hence, no time in pre-eternity.
This clarifies that qidam for Allah means existence without beginning, not infinite duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقامَ البرهان في هذا الباب على وجوبِ صفتين له جلَّ وعزَّ ؛ وهما : القدمُ والبقاء ، ولا بدَّ مِنْ بيان معناهما أوَّلاً في حقًّهِ تعالى ، ثم بعد ذلكَ يُقامُ البرهانُ على وجوبهما له جل وعزَّ .
الكلام على القدم
فاعلم : أنَّ ( القدم ( يُطلَقُ على معنيين :
أحدهما : توالي الأزمنة على الشيء وإِنْ كانَ مُحدَثاً لوجودِهِ أَوَّلُ ومنه : أساس قديم ، وبناء قديم .
وهذا المعنى مستحيل في حقّهِ تعالى ؛ إذْ وجوده ليسَ زمانيّاً ، ولا نسبة بين وجودِهِ والزمان ألبتة ؛ إذ الزمانُ مِنْ صفاتِ المحدَثِ . فيكون حادثاً ضرورةً؛ لأنَّه :
إما عبارة عن مقارنة حادثٍ لحادثٍ : كمقارنة السفر لطلوع الشمس مثلاً ، فوجود الزمانِ على هاذا فرغ وجودِ حادثين ؛ لأنَّه نسبةٌ بينهما والنسبةُ تتأخَّرُ عن المنتسبين (۱) ، ولا حادث في الأزلِ ، فلا زمانَ في الأزلِ ، والتجدُّدُ في ذاتِهِ تعالى وصفاتِهِ مستحيل ، فنسبة الزمانِ إذا إلى ذاتِهِ تعالى وإلى صفاته مستحيل على الإطلاق في الأزلِ وفيما لا يزالُ .
وإما عبارة عن حركاتِ الأفلاكِ ، وما يرجع إليها مِنَ الساعاتِ
(۱) فقولنا : ( أسافر عند طلوع الشمس ( المقارنة بين السفر والطلوع هي النسبة ، والسفر والطلوع هما المنتسبان في هذا المثال
The proof in this chapter has established the necessity of two attributes for Him, Mighty and Majestic: pre-eternity and everlastingness. It is necessary to first explain their meanings with respect to Him, then afterwards the proof for their necessity for Him is established. Discussion on pre-eternity. Know that 'pre-eternity' is used in two senses: One of them: the succession of times upon a thing even if it has a beginning to its existence, such as 'ancient foundation' and 'ancient building'. This meaning is impossible with respect to Him, because His existence is not temporal; there is absolutely no relation between His existence and time, for time is among the attributes of originated things. So it is necessarily originated, because it is either an expression for the concurrence of an originated thing with another originated thing, like the concurrence of travel with sunrise, for example. The existence of time on this view requires the existence of two originated things, because it is a relation between them, and the relation is posterior to the two relata. And there is no originated thing in pre-eternity, so there is no time in pre-eternity. Renewal in His essence and attributes is impossible, so the relation of time to His essence and attributes is absolutely impossible in pre-eternity and in what will never cease. Or it is an expression for the movements of the spheres and what pertains to them of hours. (1) Our statement: 'I traveled at sunrise' – the concurrence between travel and sunrise is the relation, and travel and sunrise are the two relata in this example.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qidam' (pre-eternity) and clarifies its correct meaning for God. The author first notes that the previous proof established two attributes; now he will explain their meanings. He states that 'qidam' has two senses: (1) temporal antiquity, i.e., the succession of times upon a thing, even if it has a beginning (like an ancient building). This meaning is impossible for God because His existence is not temporal; time is a property of originated things. He offers two interpretations of time: as a relation between two originated events (e.g., travel and sunrise) – in which case time depends on two originated things, and there were no originated things in pre-eternity – or as the movements of heavenly spheres. Both show time is originated. Since God is not originated, the first meaning cannot apply. The footnote clarifies the relation example.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'qidam' for God means existence through infinite time.
Correction: Actually, qidam for Allah means necessary existence without beginning, beyond the category of time. Temporal antiquity involves succession, which is impossible for the Creator of time.

Key Takeaway

The First Meaning of Qidam and Why It Cannot Apply to Allah
Sanusi explains the first temporal meaning of qidam and proves it is impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first temporal meaning of qidam (preexistence) and why Sanusi argues it is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Allah’s eternity is not a long time; time itself is an attribute of created things.

Sanusi begins his discussion of divine attributes by clarifying what qidam (eternity) does NOT mean. He distinguishes between two meanings of the term, and then focuses on the first meaning—the succession of times. This lesson explores why this temporal sense of preexistence is categorically impossible for Allah, based on the nature of time as a relation between originated beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a feel for Sanusi's argument before diving into details.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the two meanings of qidam
At the start, Sanusi states that qidam has two meanings. Find where he lists them and note the first meaning he explains.
The first meaning involves the succession of times.
3
Analyze Sanusi's refutation of the first meaning
Focus on his reasoning: why can't the first meaning apply to Allah? Note his two definitions of time and how both are impossible in pre-eternity and for Allah.
He uses logical arguments based on the nature of time.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentence where Sanusi concludes that this meaning is specific to originated things and impossible for Allah.
This sets the stage for the true meaning of qidam.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. How does it protect Allah's transcendence?
This lesson is foundational for understanding divine eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقامَ البرهان في هذا الباب على وجوبِ صفتين له جلَّ وعزَّ ؛ وهما : القدمُ والبقاء ، ولا بدَّ مِنْ بيان معناهما أوَّلاً في حقًّهِ تعالى ، ثم بعد ذلكَ يُقامُ البرهانُ على وجوبهما له جل وعزَّ .
الكلام على القدم
فاعلم : أنَّ ( القدم ( يُطلَقُ على معنيين :
أحدهما : توالي الأزمنة على الشيء وإِنْ كانَ مُحدَثاً لوجودِهِ أَوَّلُ ومنه : أساس قديم ، وبناء قديم .
وهذا المعنى مستحيل في حقّهِ تعالى ؛ إذْ وجوده ليسَ زمانيّاً ، ولا نسبة بين وجودِهِ والزمان ألبتة ؛ إذ الزمانُ مِنْ صفاتِ المحدَثِ . فيكون حادثاً ضرورةً ؛ لأنَّه :
إما عبارة عن مقارنة حادثٍ لحادثٍ : كمقارنة السفر لطلوع الشمس مثلاً ، فوجود الزمانِ على هاذا فرغ وجودِ حادثين ؛ لأنَّه نسبةٌ بينهما والنسبةُ تتأخَّرُ عن المنتسبين (۱) ، ولا حادث في الأزلِ ، فلا زمانَ في الأزلِ ، والتجدُّدُ في ذاتِهِ تعالى وصفاتِهِ مستحيل ، فنسبة الزمانِ إذا إلى ذاتِهِ تعالى وإلى صفاته مستحيل على الإطلاق في الأزلِ وفيما لا يزالُ .
وإما عبارة عن حركاتِ الأفلاكِ ، وما يرجع إليها مِنَ الساعاتِ وأجزائها ، وتعاقب الليل والنهارِ : ولا شك أنَّ الزمانَ بهذا المعنى منعدم أيضاً في الأزلِ ؛ إذْ لا فلكَ فيهِ ولا حركة ، فلا ساعة ولا ليل ولا نهار ، ويستحيل أن يمرَّ الزمانُ بهذا المعنى عليه جل وعزَّ ؛ لأنَّ الليل والنهار وأجزاء هما المصاحبة لحركاتِ الأفلاكِ . . إِنَّما تمرُّ على مَنْ سُجِنَ في داخل العالم ؛ بحيثُ تتحرَّكُ الأفلاكُ وكواكبها فوقَهُ وتحته ، ويمرُّ عليهِ بوساطتها بحسَب العادة الحر والبرد ، والصيف والشتاء ، ومَنْ تنزّه عن الأمكنة والجهات والتغيراتِ.. استحالَ أنْ يكونَ لهُ معَ شيءٍ مِنَ العالم اتصال أوِ انفصال
فقدِ اتَّضِحَ لكَ : أنَّ القدمَ بهذا المعنى - أعني : باعتبار طولِ الزمان - خاص بالحوادثِ ، ويستحيل في حقه تعالى
He established proof in this chapter upon the necessity of two attributes for Him, Mighty and Majestic: al-qidam (eternity/preexistence) and al-baqā' (permanence/everlastingness). And it is necessary to explain their meaning first with respect to Him, Exalted, and then after that proof will be established upon their necessity for Him, Mighty and Majestic.
Discussion on al-qidam
Know that 'al-qidam' is used in two meanings:
One of them: the succession of times upon a thing, even if it is originated in its existence having a beginning; from this is 'ancient foundation' and 'ancient building.'
This meaning is impossible with respect to Him, Exalted, because His existence is not temporal, and there is no relation between His existence and time at all; for time is among the attributes of the originated. So He would necessarily be originated, because it [time] is either an expression of the correlation of an originated thing to an originated thing: like the correlation of travel to sunrise for example, so the existence of time in this way depends on the existence of two originated things because it is a relation between them, and the relation is posterior to the two relata, and there is no originated thing in pre-eternity, so there is no time in pre-eternity, and renewal in His Essence, Exalted, and His attributes is impossible, so the relation of time to His Essence, Exalted, and to His attributes is absolutely impossible in pre-eternity and in what never ceases.
Or it [time] is an expression of the movements of the spheres and what pertains to them of hours and their parts, and the succession of night and day; and there is no doubt that time in this sense is also non-existent in pre-eternity, for there is no sphere in it and no movement, so no hour, no night, no day, and it is impossible for time in this sense to pass over Him, Mighty and Majestic, because the night and day and their parts accompanying the movements of the spheres only pass over one who is imprisoned inside the universe, such that the spheres and their planets move above him and below him, and by means of them heat and cold, summer and winter pass over him according to habit. And one who is transcendent above places, directions, and changes, it is impossible that he have connection or separation with any thing of the universe.
Thus it has become clear to you that al-qidam in this sense – I mean with consideration of length of time – is specific to originated things, and is impossible with respect to Him, Exalted.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. The author first states that he will establish proof for two attributes: al-qidam (eternity/preexistence) and al-baqā' (permanence/everlastingness), and that he must first explain their meanings in relation to Allah. Then he begins with al-qidam, stating it has two meanings. He explains the first meaning: the succession of times upon something, even if that something has a beginning of existence. Examples are an ancient foundation or ancient building. This meaning is impossible for Allah because His existence is not temporal; there is no relation between His existence and time at all, since time is an attribute of originated things. The author then analyzes time itself: either time is the correlation of one originated thing to another, like the correlation of travel to sunrise. In this case, time depends on the existence of two originated things, and the relation is posterior to the two relata. Since there are no originated things in pre-eternity, there is no time in pre-eternity. Moreover, renewal in Allah's Essence and attributes is impossible, so relating time to Him is absolutely impossible both in pre-eternity and in the future. Alternatively, time is the movements of the spheres and the resulting hours, night and day. In pre-eternity there are no spheres or movements, so time in this sense is also non-existent. It is impossible for such time to pass over Allah, because time in this sense only passes over entities within the universe that are subject to motion and change. Allah is transcendent above places, directions, and changes, so He has no connection or separation with anything in the universe. The author concludes that the first meaning of qidam—as length of time—is specific to originated things and is impossible for Allah.

Key Terms
قدم
زمان
حادث
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam for Allah means an extremely long time, like being very old.
Correction: Sanusi explains that this temporal sense applies only to originated things; Allah's qidam is not related to time at all.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the first meaning of 'qidam' (succession of times) impossible for Allah?
A) Because Allah is not subject to time, as time is an attribute of originated things and Allah is not originated.
B) Because Allah's existence is temporal but infinite.
C) Because the first meaning only applies to material objects.
D) Because time is an illusion in Islamic theology.

Key Takeaway
Allah's qidam is not a long duration; time itself is a property of creation and cannot apply to the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life inside time, measuring every moment by clocks and calendars. But Allah is beyond time entirely. How does this truth expose the folly of your anxiety about a future you cannot control? Will you today surrender your timeline to the Timeless One?
?
If time is only a created measure for originated things, then your entire existence is bounded by its ticking. Does that not make every moment precious as a gift from the Eternal? What are you doing with this very moment that you will never get back?
?
The passage says Allah has 'no relation to time at all.' How then can you continue to imagine that He is 'waiting' for you to repent, as if His forgiveness were subject to a schedule? What is stopping you from turning to Him right now, knowing He is ever-present and not bound by your past, present, or future?

The Second Meaning of Qidam and Its Proof
Sanusi defines the second meaning of qidam and provides a proof for its necessity for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second meaning of qidam (eternity without beginning) and the rational proof that establishes it as a necessary attribute of Allah.

Core Idea
Allah alone is eternal without beginning; everything else is originated and needs a creator.

This passage continues Sanusi's exposition of the attribute of qidam, focusing on its second meaning: existence without a beginning. Here he not only defines this meaning but also proves that it must be true of Allah. The proof is a classic kalam argument: if Allah were not eternal, He would be originated, but origination entails need for a creator, leading to an infinite regress that only ends with a being that is eternal. Read carefully to see how Sanusi builds this concise yet powerful proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the second meaning
Start by reading the first part of the passage where Sanusi defines the second meaning of qidam. Notice that it applies only to Allah.
The key phrase is 'لا أول لوجوده' (no beginning to its existence).
2
Understand the exclusivity claim
Sanusi states that this meaning of qidam is established for Allah alone. Ask yourself why no other being can be described as eternal without beginning.
This will become clear from the proof that follows.
3
Follow the proof step by step
The proof is a conditional argument: if not eternal, then originated; if originated, then needs a creator; then the creator itself would need a creator, leading to an infinite regress. The only way to avoid this is to affirm that Allah is eternal.
The text cuts off at 'so it must be', but the conclusion is implied: the creator must be eternal.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice the logical form: it uses a dilemma (no middle between qidam and huduth) and the principle that every originated thing needs a creator. This is a foundational argument in Islamic theology.
This argument is often called the 'kalam cosmological argument'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني مِنْ معنى القدم : أَنَّهُ يُطلَقُ على ما لا أَوَّلَ لوجودِهِ ؛ أي : وجوده أزلي لم يسبقه عدمٌ ، والقدمُ بهذا المعنى الثاني هو الثابت له جل وعزّ ، ولا يتّصفُ بهِ سواه .
والدليل على وجوبِهِ لهُ جلَّ وعلا : ما أشرنا إليه في العقيدة ؛ وهو أنَّهُ لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً ، تعالى الله عن ذلكَ ؛ إِذْ لا واسطة بينَ القدمِ والحدوثِ في حقٌّ كلّ موجود ، ولكن كونه حادثاً محال ؛ لأنَّه يجب حينئذٍ افتقاره إلى مُحدِث ؛ لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوب افتقار كلّ حادث إلى مُحدِثِ ، ثم ننقلُ الكلامَ إلى مُحدثِهِ ، فيجبُ أنْ يكونَ
The second of the meanings of qidam: that it is applied to that which has no beginning to its existence; i.e., its existence is eternal (azalī), no non-existence preceded it. And qidam in this second meaning is established for Him, Mighty and Majestic, and none other is described by it.
And the proof for its necessity for Him, Mighty and Exalted: that which we alluded to in al-ʻAqīdah; which is that if He were not eternal, He would be originated – exalted is Allah above that – for there is no intermediate between qidam and ḥudūth (origination) with respect to every existent. But His being originated is impossible, because then He would necessarily need a creator (muḥdith); based on what you have learned that every originated thing necessarily needs a creator. Then we transfer the discussion to its creator, so it must be...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the second meaning of qidam and then provides a proof for its necessity for Allah. The author states that the second meaning of qidam is that which has no beginning to its existence; it is eternal (azalī) without prior non-existence. This meaning is exclusively for Allah, and no other being is described by it. Then he gives the proof: if Allah were not eternal, He would be originated. There is no middle state between being eternal and being originated for any existent. But it is impossible for Allah to be originated, because then He would necessarily need a creator, as it is established that every originated thing needs a creator. Then the discussion would be transferred to that creator, which must itself be eternal to avoid an infinite regress. The text cuts off at 'so it must be' – clearly the intended conclusion is that the creator must be eternal, thus affirming Allah's qidam.

أزل
حدوث
محدث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that the proof for Allah's eternity assumes what it wants to prove because it appeals to the principle that every originated thing has a creator, which itself requires proof.
Correction: The passage does not assume the existence of a creator; it builds on a premise that is intuitively accepted and then shows that the only way to avoid an infinite regress is to posit an eternal being. The argument is a standard rational proof in Islamic theology.

Checkpoint

D) Existing in time indefinitely.

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is eternal without beginning, and this is proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress of creators.

Sanusi defines the second meaning of qidam and asserts its exclusive application to Allah.

Learning Objective

Only Allah has no beginning; all else began.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the second specific meaning of the attribute qidam. This meaning applies solely to Allah, setting Him apart from all created beings.

The Two Meanings of Baqa' and the Proof of God's Eternal Duration
Sanusi distinguishes two meanings of baqa' and proves God's attribute of enduringness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of baqa' (eternal duration) and why the second meaning—the impossibility of non-existence—is necessarily attributed to God, proven by the contradiction that would arise if non-existence could attach to His eternal essence.

Core Idea
God's eternal duration is not temporal continuity but the absolute impossibility of ceasing to exist.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a crucial distinction in the attribute of baqa' (eternal duration). Many might think 'eternal' simply means lasting through time, but for God, it means something deeper: the impossibility of non-existence ever occurring. This lesson unpacks that distinction and presents the logical proof that establishes baqa' as a necessary divine attribute, leading to a universal rule about the relationship between eternity and enduringness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two definitions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two distinct meanings Sanusi gives for baqa'. Mark where he says which meaning applies to God and which to contingent things.
The first meaning involves time, the second negates non-existence.
2
Understand why the first meaning is invalid for God
Sanusi says the first meaning (accompaniment of existence with multiple times) is impossible for God because His existence cannot be restricted to time. This is a key point: God transcends temporal limitations.
Recall earlier lessons on God's independence from time.
3
Follow the proof against the possibility of non-existence
Walk through the reductio ad absurdum: if non-existence could attach to God, then His essence would be equally capable of existence and non-existence, making Him need a cause to prefer existence—so He would be contingent and not eternal, contradicting His proven eternity.
Notice the parallel drawn to contingent beings (the world).
4
Extract the universal rule
The proof concludes with a universal principle: whatever is established as eternal necessarily cannot cease to exist. This rule is foundational for all subsequent discussions of divine attributes.
This rule connects eternity (qidam) and enduringness (baqa').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ( البقاء ) : فيُطلَقُ ويُرادُ بِهِ مقارنة الوجودِ لزمانين فصاعداً ، وهو مستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لما عرفْتَ مِن استحالة تقييد وجودِهِ تعالى بالزمانِ ، وإِنَّما يَتَصفُ بالبقاء بهذا المعنى الحوادث . ويُطلَقُ ويُرادُ بِهِ سَلْبُ الآخريَّةِ للوجودِ ؛ أي : يمتنع أن يلحق وجودَهُ العدمُ ، والبقاء بهذا المعنى هو الثابتُ لهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، ويستحيل أن يتصف به سواه . لو قُدِّرَ لحوقُ العدمِ لهُ تعالى - تعالى الله عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً - لكانَتْ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ تقبل الوجود والعدم ؛ لاتصافِهِ - تعالى الله عن ذلك - على هذا الفَرْضِ المحالِ بهما معاً ، وإذا قبلهما كانا بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ سواءً ، كما كانا سواءً بالنسبة إلى ذاتِ العالم ، فيلزم افتقار وجودِهِ حينئذ إلى مُوجِدٍ يخترعه بدلاً عن العدم الجائز عليه ، فيكون حادثاً ، وإذا كان حادثاً لم يكن قديماً ، كيف وقد سبق قريباً البرهان القطعي على وجوبِ قدمه ؟! فإذاً ؛ لحوقُ العدم لذاتِهِ العليَّةِ مستحيل . فبانَ لكَ بهذا البرهان : أنَّ وجوبَ القدم يستلزم أبداً وجوبَ البقاء ، وأنَّ تجويز العدمِ اللاحقِ يوجبُ ثبوت العدمِ السابق ، فخرج لك بهذا البرهان قاعدة كليَّةٌ ؛ وهي : أنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحال
As for eternal duration (baqa'): it is used to mean the accompaniment of existence with two or more times, and that is impossible with respect to Him, Exalted, because of what you know about the impossibility of restricting His existence to time; rather, contingent things are described by this meaning of enduringness. And it is used to mean the negation of posteriority for existence; i.e., it is impossible for non-existence to attach to His existence, and enduringness in this meaning is affirmed for Him, Mighty and Majestic, and it is impossible for other than Him to be described by it. If non-existence were supposed to attach to Him—exalted is Allah above that, greatly—then His High Essence would accept both existence and non-existence; because, under this impossible assumption, it would be characterized by both; and if it accepts both, then with respect to His Essence they would be equal, just as they are equal with respect to the essence of the world; then His existence would at that moment need a creator who originates it instead of the non-existence that is possible for it, so it would be contingent; and if it were contingent, it would not be eternal. How could it be, when we have already given a decisive proof for the necessity of His eternity?! Therefore, the attachment of non-existence to His High Essence is impossible. Thus, by this proof it became clear to you that the necessity of eternity necessarily entails the necessity of enduringness, and that allowing later non-existence would necessitate the affirmation of prior non-existence. So by this proof a universal rule emerged: that everything whose eternity is established, its [non-existence] is impossible.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of 'baqa'' (eternal duration) and proves it is necessary for God. First, it distinguishes two meanings: temporal endurance (accompanying existence across multiple times, which applies only to contingent things and is impossible for God) and the negation of posterior non-existence (i.e., impossibility of ceasing to exist). This second meaning is the true attribute of God and impossible for others. Then Sanusi gives a proof: if non-existence could happen to God, then His essence would be equally capable of existence and non-existence, like contingent beings; hence He would need a cause to prefer existence, making Him contingent and not eternal—contradicting His proven eternity. Therefore, non-existence is impossible for Him. The conclusion is a key principle: necessary eternity entails necessary eternal duration.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
زمان (zaman)
عدم ('adam)
قدم (qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' for God means existing through endless time, like a temporal everlastingness.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that temporal endurance applies only to contingent things. For God, baqa' means the negation of non-existence—an absolute impossibility of ceasing to exist, which does not involve time.

Checkpoint
What is the second meaning of baqa' as applied to God?
A) Existing for two or more times
B) Negation of posterior non-existence
C) Being eternal in time
D) Being timeless

Key Takeaway
Necessity of eternity (qidam) necessarily entails necessity of enduringness (baqa'); an eternal being cannot cease to exist.

?
God's eternal duration is not a long time but the impossibility of non-existence. How does this truth change the way you view your own mortality and the fleeting nature of this world? Will you invest your days in what endures with Him?

Two Meanings of al-Baqa' and Their Application to Allah
Author defines al-baqa' in two senses and states their rule for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of al-baqa' (permanence) and why only the second properly applies to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's permanence means He cannot cease to exist, not that He endures through time.

The term 'al-baqa'' (permanence) can be used in two very different senses. One refers to persistence through multiple time periods, which is impossible for Allah since He is beyond time. The other refers to the impossibility of non-existence ever affecting His existence. In this passage, Sanusi clarifies these two meanings and their correct application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very structured approach: first one meaning, then the other.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the two definitions of al-baqa' in the passage. The first involves time; the second involves the negation of an end.
The first is 'co-existence of existence with two or more times'; the second is 'negation of posteriority for existence'.
3
Understand why the first meaning is impossible for Allah
Reflect on why the first meaning cannot apply to Allah. The passage says it is because Allah's existence is not restricted by time.
Time itself is a created entity; Allah is beyond temporal restrictions.
4
See why the second meaning is unique to Allah
The passage states that the second meaning is 'established for Him' and 'impossible that other than Him be described by it'. Ask yourself why only Allah can have this attribute.
Because all other beings are contingent and can cease to exist.
5
Apply: When we say Allah is 'Baqi'
Now you know that when Muslims say Allah is al-Baqi (the Everlasting), they mean the second meaning: His existence has no end.
This distinguishes Islamic theology from any notion of God being subject to time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على البقاء
وأما ( البقاء ) : فيُطلَقُ ويُرادُ بِهِ مقارنة الوجودِ لزمانين فصاعداً ، وهو مستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لما عرفْتَ مِن استحالة تقييد وجودِهِ تعالى بالزمانِ ، وإِنَّما يَتَصفُ بالبقاء بهذا المعنى الحوادث
ويُطلَقُ ويُرادُ بهِ سَلْبُ الآخريَّةِ للوجودِ ؛ أي : يمتنع أن يلحق وجودَهُ العدمُ ، والبقاء بهذا المعنى هو الثابتُ لهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، ويستحيل أن يتصف به سواه
Discussion on al-baqa' (permanence/eternity).
As for al-baqa': it is used and intended by it (first) the co-existence of existence with two or more times, and it is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted, because you have known the impossibility of restricting His existence, may He be exalted, by time. Rather, only contingent events (al-hawadith) are described by al-baqa' in this sense.
And it is used and intended by it (second) the negation of posteriority (al-ākhiriyya) for existence; i.e., it is impossible that non-existence should attach to His existence. And al-baqa' in this sense is that which is established for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, and it is impossible that other than Him be described by it.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-baqa'' (permanence/eternity) and distinguishes two distinct meanings, then applies each to Allah. The author begins by stating the first meaning: 'co-existence of existence with two or more times' — in simple terms, existence that continues over multiple time periods. This meaning is impossible for Allah, because Allah's existence is not restricted by time; time itself is a created entity. Therefore, only created events (al-hawadith) can be described as 'baqa'' in this temporal sense. The second meaning is 'the negation of posteriority (or finality) for existence' — that is, it is impossible for non-existence to ever attach to His existence. This is the true, eternal permanence that belongs exclusively to Allah. No other being can be described by this second meaning, because all other beings are contingent and subject to non-existence. The passage thus teaches that when we say Allah is 'baqi' (everlasting), we mean the second meaning alone: His existence has no end, not that He persists through time.

Key Terms
بقاء
زمان
حوادث
سلب الآخريَّة
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's attribute of 'al-baqa'' means He endures through endless time.
Correction: Allah's permanence is not temporal duration (first meaning) but rather the impossibility of non-existence (second meaning), because Allah is beyond time.

Checkpoint
What is the first meaning of al-baqa' according to the passage, and why is it impossible for Allah?
A) Existence that never begins; because Allah's existence is pre-eternal.
B) Co-existence of existence with two or more times; because Allah is not bound by time.
C) Negation of non-existence; because Allah cannot be described by temporal attributes.
D) Endless duration in the future; because Allah's existence has no beginning.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqa' for Allah means that His existence cannot be followed by non-existence, not that He persists through time.

?

Proof of Allah's Everlastingness (Baqa')
Sanusi proves the impossibility of non-existence for Allah and deduces a general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's pre-eternity (qidam) logically requires His everlastingness (baqa'), and how the assumption of possible non-existence leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If Allah cannot cease to exist, His existence is necessary from first to last.

This lesson presents a rational proof from Sanusi that Allah's pre-eternity necessarily implies His everlastingness. By assuming the opposite—that non-existence could attach to Allah—we uncover a contradiction that reinforces the absolute permanence of the Divine. The proof also yields a general rule: whatever is pre-eternal cannot cease to exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author structures a conditional argument: 'If X were possible, then Y would follow, which contradicts Z.'
The proof relies on a prior established proof for qidam (pre-eternity).
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: assumption (non-existence possible) → consequence (essence accepts both existence and non-existence) → implication (need for a creator) → contradiction (originated vs. pre-eternal) → conclusion (impossibility).
Each step follows from the previous one.
3
Derive the universal rule
Notice how the author extracts a rule from the proof: necessity of pre-eternity entails necessity of everlastingness. Understand why allowing later non-existence implies prior non-existence.
This rule is a key principle in Sunni theology.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it establishes that Allah's attributes are not temporary—He is eternally existent without beginning or end. This distinguishes Him from all creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو قُدِّرَ لحوقُ العدمِ له تعالى - تعالى الله عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً - لكانَتْ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ تقبل الوجود والعدم ؛ لاتصافِهِ - تعالى الله عن ذلك - على هذا الفَرْضِ المحالِ بهما معاً ، وإذا قبلهما كانا بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ سواءً ، كما كانا سواءً بالنسبة إلى ذاتِ العالم ، فيلزم افتقار وجودِهِ حينئذ إلى مُوجِدٍ يخترعه بدلاً عن العدم الجائز عليه ، فيكون حادثاً ، وإذا كان حادثاً لم يكن قديماً ، كيف وقد سبق قريباً البرهان القطعي على وجوبِ قدمه ؟!
فإذاً ؛ لحوقُ العدم لذاتِهِ العليَّةِ مستحيل .
فبانَ لكَ بهذا البرهان : أنَّ وجوبَ القدم يستلزم أبداً وجوبَ البقاء ، وأنَّ تجويز العدمِ اللاحقِ يوجبُ ثبوت العدمِ السابق ، فخرج لك بهذا البرهان قاعدة كليَّةٌ ؛ وهي : أنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحال عدمه ، وأنَّ الجمعَ بينَ وجوبِ القدمِ وعدم وجوب البقاء . . تناقض ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( فإذا ؛ فَرْضُ عدم وجوبِ البقاء ... ) إلى آخره
If the attachment of non-existence to Him, may He be exalted - exalted is Allah above that with great exaltation - were supposed, then His supreme essence would accept existence and non-existence, because of its being described - exalted is Allah above that - on this impossible assumption by both together. And if it accepts both, then both are equal with respect to His essence, just as they are equal with respect to the essence of the world. Then the need of His existence at that time to a creator who brings it into existence instead of the possible non-existence would necessarily follow, so it would be originated (hadith). And if it is originated, it would not be pre-eternal (qadim). How [could it be], while the decisive proof for the necessity of His pre-eternity (qidam) has already been given recently?!
Therefore, the attachment of non-existence to His supreme essence is impossible.
So it has become clear to you by this proof that the necessity of pre-eternity always entails the necessity of perpetual endurance (baqa'), and that allowing later non-existence necessitates the establishment of prior non-existence. Thus there has emerged for you from this proof a universal rule: that everything whose pre-eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible, and that combining the necessity of pre-eternity with the non-necessity of perpetual endurance is a contradiction. And to this meaning I alluded in my saying: 'Therefore, the supposition of non-necessity of perpetual endurance...' to the end.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the necessity of Allah's everlastingness (baqa') based on the already established proof of His pre-eternity (qidam). The author begins by assuming the contrary: suppose non-existence could attach to Allah after His existence. If that were possible, then His essence would be capable of both existence and non-existence — meaning neither is essential to Him. In that case, He would be, like the world, equally eligible for both. If He is equally eligible for non-existence, then His actual existence would require a cause that chooses existence over non-existence; that cause would be a creator. Such a being would be 'originated' (hadith) — contingent and in need of a maker. But an originated being cannot be pre-eternal (qadim). This contradicts the already proven necessity of Allah's pre-eternity (the author reminds us that a decisive proof for qidam was given earlier). Therefore, the initial assumption is false: non-existence can never attach to Allah. From this proof, the author derives a general rule: Necessity of pre-eternity entails necessity of everlastingness; allowing later non-existence implies earlier non-existence (because if something can cease to exist, it must have been possible for it not to exist from the beginning). The rule is: whatever is proven to be pre-eternal cannot possibly cease to exist. Combining necessary pre-eternity with non-necessary everlastingness is contradictory. The author ends by referencing his own poetic line that alludes to this conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof is circular because it uses pre-eternity to prove everlastingness, but both are similar.
Correction: The proof is not circular: it uses the already established proof for qidam (pre-eternity) as a premise, not the concept of baqa' itself, and derives baqa' as a logical entailment.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would follow if non-existence were possible for Allah?
A) Allah would be described by both existence and non-existence equally, implying He is self-caused.
B) Allah would be in need of a creator, making Him originated (hadith) and not pre-eternal.
C) Allah would still be pre-eternal because non-existence after existence does not affect qidam.
D) The universe would cease to exist as well, because Allah sustains it.

Key Takeaway
Allah's pre-eternity logically implies His everlastingness; if He could cease to exist, He would be originated, contradicting His necessary pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's existence is necessary and eternal, what temporary things in your life are you still afraid to lose? How does that fear contradict your conviction?
?
The proof shows that if Allah could cease to exist, He would need a creator. Have you ever, even for a moment, doubted that Allah is eternal? What does that doubt imply about your understanding of His perfection?
?
The universal rule says: everything proven pre-eternal cannot cease. Your own existence is not pre-eternal—it will end. How does this truth reshape the urgency of your worship and your attachment to this world?

The Meaning of Self-Subsistence (Qiyām bi n-Nafs)
The author defines the divine attribute of self-subsistence and explains scholarly terminological differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of self-subsistence and why the scholarly difference is merely terminological.

Core Idea
God's self-subsistence means absolute independence from locus and determiner.

This passage from Sanusi clarifies the divine attribute of self-subsistence (qiyām bi n-nafs). The author presents two definitions and explains that the apparent disagreement among scholars is only about terminology, not meaning. By examining these definitions, you will see how kalam distinguishes between God and created substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Notice the opening praise and the two definitions that follow.
The passage ends incomplete; focus on the available content.
2
Identify the two definitions
The first definition says self-subsistence means not needing a locus (maḥall). The second adds independence from a determiner (mukhassis) as well, making it more specific.
The second definition excludes substances (jawhar) because they need a determiner.
3
Recognize the terminological nature of the difference
The author emphasizes that the disagreement among imams is only terminological (iṣṭilāḥ), not a real difference in meaning. Both definitions affirm God's independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله الذي لم يجعل سبيلاً إلى معرفته إلا بالعجز عن معرفته. قيامه بذاته بنفسه، واستغناؤه عن المحل والمخصص. ويجب أيضاً أن يكون قائماً بنفسه؛ أي ذاتاً لا يفتقر إلى محل، ويستحيل أن يكون صفة. ومنهم من فسر قيامه تعالى بنفسه باستغنائه عن المحل والمخصص، وهو أخص من التفسير الأول، ويخرج مشاركة الجوهر له في هذه الصفة. قد اختلف الأئمة في معنى القائم بالنفس، والاختلاف راجع إلى الاصطلاح من غير خلاف في المعنى. فمن الأئمة من اصطلح على إطلاق هذا اللفظ على ما لا يفتقر إلى محل، فيندرج في مقتضى هذا اللفظ الجوهر والقديم؛ إذ كل منهما لا يفتقر إلى محل؛ أي لا يكون صفة لغيره، ويخرج عن مقتضى
Praise be to God who has made no way to knowledge of Him except through incapacity to know Him. His self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi dhātihi bi nafsihi) and His independence from locus (maḥall) and determiner (mukhassis). And it is also necessary that He be self-subsistent (qā'im bi nafsihi); that is, an essence (dhāt) that does not need a locus, and impossible that He be an attribute. And among them is one who interpreted His, exalted, self-subsistence as His independence from locus and determiner, and it is more specific than the first interpretation, and excludes the substance (jawhar) from sharing in this attribute. The imams have differed on the meaning of 'self-subsistent', and the difference returns to terminology (iṣṭilāḥ) without difference in meaning. So among the imams is one who adopted the usage of applying this term to that which does not need a locus, so under the requirement of this term are included substance (jawhar) and the Eternal (qadīm); since each of them does not need a locus; that is, it is not an attribute of another, and it excludes from the requirement
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the divine attribute of qiyām bi n-nafs (self-subsistence). The author begins with a praise that highlights the limitation of human knowledge. He then states that God's 'qiyām bi dhātihi bi nafsihi' means His independence from a locus (maḥall) and a determiner (mukhassis). This is the first definition: self-subsistent as that which is an essence (dhāt) not needing a locus, and impossible that it be an attribute (since attributes need a subject). The second interpretation is more specific: self-subsistence means independence from both locus and determiner, which excludes created substances (jawhar) because they need a determiner (i.e., a place or creator). Thus the term in the second sense applies only to the Eternal (qadīm). The author then clarifies that the disagreement among scholars on the precise definition is merely terminological (iṣṭilāḥ) and not a real difference in meaning. Both definitions agree on the essential independence of God. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about what is excluded from the requirement of the first definition (likely that certain entities are excluded).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that qiyām bi n-nafs means physical self-subsistence or that God has a location.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means independence from a locus (maḥall) and determiner (mukhassis), not physical location.

Checkpoint

A) One interpretation requires independence from a locus only, while the other adds independence from a determiner.
B) One interpretation applies only to the Eternal, while the other applies only to substances.
C) One interpretation denies the attribute, while the other affirms it.
D) There is no difference; the two interpretations are identical.

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence is defined by independence from locus and determiner, and scholarly differences are only terminological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God is absolutely independent of any locus or determiner. Reflect: Do you ever act as if God is limited by your circumstances or location? When you find yourself in a difficult place, do you assume God has no power there? How can you remind yourself of God's self-subsistence in your next trial?
?
The lesson emphasizes that God does not need a 'locus' to exist. In your prayer, do you automatically imagine God as being 'up there' or in a specific direction? What would it mean for you to worship God truly without spatial limitations? What thought or habit must you change?
?
The author says the difference among scholars is merely terminological. Are there disagreements in your own practice that you treat as fundamental but are actually only differences in words? Is there a matter of fiqh or sunnah where you have been rigid or dismissive of others, forgetting that the underlying meaning is agreed upon? What step can you take this week to unite rather than divide over terminology?
