Two Interpretations of Qā'im bi Nafsih
Sanusi explains two definitions of qā'im bi nafsih and their consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting) and why the second interpretation uniquely applies to Allah.

Core Idea
The second interpretation of self-subsistence excludes even the substance, making it exclusive to Allah.

This passage from Sanusi's al-Muqaddimāt clarifies the attribute of self-subsistence (qā'im bi nafsih). Sanusi explains that scholars offer two definitions: one broad, one narrow. The difference is terminological but has important theological consequences for distinguishing Allah from created substances. Let's unpack these two definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Notice the two definitions presented.
The passage is cut off at the end; we cover the content present.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation: no need for a locus (maḥall). The second: no need for locus or specificator (mukhakhkhiṣ). Note how they differ.
3
Compare the scope
Under the first, both substance (jawhar) and Allah are self-subsisting. Under the second, only Allah qualifies because the substance still needs a specificator.
This is the key doctrinal point.
4
Understand why substance is not truly self-subsisting
Even though the substance is not an attribute (does not need a locus), it requires a specificator for its existence and preservation. So it is not independent in the full sense.
5
Reflect on the grammatical note
Sanusi explains that the phrase 'i.e., a self' is an appositive or substitute for 'self-subsisting'.
This shows careful textual analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أيضاً : أنْ يكونَ قائماً بنفسِهِ ؛ أي : ذاتاً لا يفتقر إلى محل ، ويستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ صفةً .
ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَ قيامَهُ تعالى بنفسه ؛ باستغنائه عن المحل والمخصص ، وهو أخص مِنَ التفسير الأوَّلِ ، ويُخرِجُ مشاركة الجوهر له في هذه الصفة .
قد اختلف الأئمةُ في معنى القائم بالنفس ، والاختلاف راجع إلى الاصطلاحِ مِنْ غيرِ خلافِ في المعنى .
فمِنَ الأئِمَّةِ مَنِ اصطلح على إطلاق هذا اللفظ على ما لا يفتقر إلى محل ، فيندرج في مقتضى هذا اللفظ الجوهر والقديم ؛ إذْ كلٌّ منهما لا يفتقر إلى محلّ ؛ أي: لا يكون صفةً لغيره ، ويخرج عن مقتضى هذا اللفظ : الصفةُ ، قديمة كانَتْ أو حادثةً ؛ لأنَّ الصفة مطلقاً لا بدَّ لها مِنْ محل تقوم به .
وذهب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني إلى أنَّ القائم بالنفسِ : هو ما لا يفتقر وجودُهُ إلى أمرٍ آخرَ غيرِ وجودِهِ ، وإن شئت قلت : هو ما يستغني عن المحل والمخصص .
ولا فرق بين العبارتين في المعنى ، ولا قائم بنفسِهِ على هذا الاصطلاح إلا القيُّومُ سبحانَهُ ؛ فإِنَّ الجوهر وإن استغنى عن المحل ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ ليسَ صفةٌ لذاتٍ أخرى .. فهو مُفتقِرُ أعظم افتقار إلى المُخصّص لذاتِهِ بالوجودِ بدلاً عن العدم الذي كانَتْ عليه ، وبالمقدار والصفة اللذين هي عليهما دون غيرهما ، ثم هو بعد محتاج في بقاء ذاتِهِ وصفاتها إلى مولاه جلَّ وعزَّ ، ولولا إبقاؤُهُ تعالى الكائناتِ إلى ما شاءَ مِنَ الآجال . . لانعدمتْ كلُّها في الحال .
فقد استبانَ : أنَّ القيام بالنفس بهذا التفسير الثاني أخص منه بالتفسير الأوَّلِ ؛ لأنَّ هذا التفسير الثاني فيه ما في الأَوَّلِ وزيادة ، ولهذا كان الجوهرُ في التفسير الأوَّلِ يشاركُهُ تعالى في مجرَّدِ التسمية بالقيام بالنفس ، وفي التفسير الثاني لا يشاركه في التسمية به . فقوله : ( أي : ذاتاً ) تفسير لقوله : ( قائماً بنفسِهِ ) فيُعربُ : إِمَّا بدلاً ، أو عطف بيان .

And it is also necessary that He be self-subsisting (qā'im bi nafsih), meaning a self that does not depend on a locus (maḥall), and it is impossible that He be an attribute. And among them are those who interpreted His self-subsistence as His independence from locus and specificator (mukhakhkhiṣ), and it is more specific than the first interpretation, and it excludes the substance (jawhar) from sharing in this attribute. The imams indeed differed concerning the meaning of the self-subsisting, and the difference goes back to terminology without difference in meaning. For among the imams, some adopted the convention of applying this term to what does not depend on a locus, so under this term fall the substance and the eternal, since each of them does not depend on a locus; i.e., it is not an attribute of something else, and excluded from this term is the attribute, whether eternal or originated, because the attribute absolutely must have a locus in which it inheres. And the master Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī held that the self-subsisting is that whose existence does not depend on something other than its existence, and if you wish you may say: it is that which is independent of locus and specificator. There is no difference between the two expressions in meaning, and there is no self-subsisting on this convention except the Self-Subsistent (al-Qayyūm), glorified be He. For the substance, even if it is independent of locus in the sense that it is not an attribute of another essence, is yet in the greatest need of the specificator for its essence by existence instead of the non-existence it had, and by the quantity and attribute that it has rather than others, and then it is afterward in need in the permanence of its essence and its attributes of its Lord, mighty and majestic. And were it not for His preserving the existences until the terms He wills, all of them would become non-existent instantly. Thus it became clear that self-subsistence according to this second interpretation is more specific than according to the first interpretation, because this second interpretation includes what is in the first and more. Therefore the substance in the first interpretation shares with Him, exalted, in the mere naming of self-subsistence, while in the second interpretation it does not share with Him in that naming. So His saying: (i.e., a self) is an interpretation of His saying: (self-subsisting), and it is analyzed either as a substitute or as an explicative apposition. His saying: (and it is more specific) the pronoun refers to the second interpretation, or to
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the attribute of self-subsistence (qā'im bi nafsih) for God. The author begins by stating that it is necessary for God to be self-subsisting, meaning a self that does not depend on a locus (maḥall), and cannot be an attribute. Then he notes that some scholars interpret self-subsistence as independence from both locus and specificator (mukhakhkhiṣ), which is more specific than the first interpretation and excludes the substance (jawhar) from sharing this attribute. The author clarifies that the difference among imams is terminological, not substantive. He then expounds the first interpretation: those who define qā'im bi nafsih as what does not need a locus include both the substance and the eternal (al-Qadīm, i.e., God) under this term, because neither is an attribute; attributes inhere in a locus. The second interpretation is attributed to Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī: the self-subsisting is that whose existence does not need anything else. This second definition essentially means independence from locus and specificator. The author emphasizes that there is no difference in meaning between the two expressions, but on this second convention only God is called self-subsisting because the substance, though independent of locus, still requires a specificator to bring it into existence and to preserve it. Thus the second interpretation is more specific, excluding the substance from the term. The passage ends with a grammatical note about the phrase 'i.e., a self' as an appositive or substitute for 'self-subsisting'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'self-subsisting' simply means not needing a place (locus).
Correction: The second interpretation shows it also excludes need for a specificator, making it unique to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the second interpretation of qā'im bi nafsih, why is the substance (jawhar) not considered self-subsisting?
A) Because it depends on a locus.
B) Because it needs a specificator for its existence.
C) Because it is an attribute.
D) Because it is originated.

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation of self-subsistence is more specific and uniquely applies to Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the realization that even the substance (jawhar) is utterly dependent on a specificator challenge any sense of autonomy you might hold?
?
Which of the two interpretations of 'self-subsisting' did you unconsciously believe about Allah before reading this lesson? Now that you see the second interpretation uniquely applies to Him, how must your worship and reliance change to reflect His absolute independence?
?
If you truly internalized that only Allah is qā'im bi nafsih in the fullest sense, what specific habits or attachments would you need to abandon to live in complete dependence on Him alone? What is one step you can take today to acknowledge His sole self-subsistence?

Classification of Positive Attributes and the Term 'Attributes of Meanings'
Opening a new chapter on positive attributes and their classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold classification of positive attributes and the correct interpretation of the phrase 'attributes of meanings'.

Core Idea
Positive attributes fall into two categories: those essential for God's actions and the rest.

After completing the discussion of negative attributes (sifat salbiyya), the author now turns to the positive attributes (sifat thubutiyya). He immediately introduces a classification: some positive attributes are necessary for God's actions, while others are not. He also clarifies the technical term 'attributes of meanings' to avoid misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the structure: a chapter title, a transitional sentence, a classification, and a clarifying note.
2
Identify the two categories of positive attributes
Look for the phrase 'وهي قسمان' (and they are two divisions). The first category is 'ما تتوقف عليه أفعاله تعالى' (that upon which His actions depend). The second is 'ما لا يكون كذلك' (that which is not like that).
The first category includes power, will, knowledge, and life.
3
Understand the commentary on 'attributes of meanings'
The author explains that the genitive construction in 'صفات المعاني' is for clarification (idafat al-bayan). It means the attributes themselves that are existent meanings, not separate entities.
This corrects a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب : الدليل على وجوب صفات المعاني ووجوب أحكامها له تعالى ووجوب القدم والبقاء بجميعها ، وما يتعلق بذلك وفيه فصول : لما فرغ من ذكر الصفات السلبية شرع في ذكر الصفات الثبوتية ؛ وهي قسمان : الأول : ما تتوقف عليه أفعاله تعالى الثاني : ما لا يكون كذلك والأول : القدرة والإرادة والعلم والحياة ، والثاني : ما سوى ذلك . قوله : (على وجوب صفات المعاني، ووجوب أحكامها) قد قدمنا أن الإضافة في (صفات المعاني) للبيان، وأن المراد : أنفس المعاني (١)، يعنون بها : الصفات التي هي المعاني الوجودية . (١) تقدم قريباً (ص ٢٥٥)
Chapter: The proof for the necessity of the attributes of meanings and the necessity of their rulings for Him, and the necessity of pre-eternity and everlastingness with all of them, and what pertains to that. And it contains sections: When he finished mentioning the negative attributes, he began to mention the positive attributes; and they are two divisions: The first: that upon which His actions depend; the second: that which is not like that. And the first: power, will, knowledge, and life; and the second: what is other than that. His saying: 'On the necessity of the attributes of meanings and the necessity of their rulings' – we have previously explained that the genitive construction in 'attributes of meanings' is for clarification, and that the intended meaning is the meanings themselves (1), by which they mean the attributes that are the existent meanings. (1) It has preceded recently (p. 255).
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter (bab) dedicated to proving the necessity of the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) and their rulings, as well as the necessity of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa') for all of them. The author first notes that after completing the discussion of negative attributes (sifat salbiyya), he now turns to the positive attributes (sifat thubutiyya). He then divides them into two categories: (1) those upon which God's actions (af'al) depend, and (2) those that are not like that. The first category includes power (qudra), will (irada), knowledge ('ilm), and life (hayat). The second category comprises the remaining positive attributes. He then comments on the phrase 'the necessity of the attributes of meanings' from the chapter title, clarifying that the construct 'attributes of meanings' is a genitive of elucidation (idafat al-bayan), meaning the attributes themselves that are existent meanings (ma'ani wujudiyya). He references an earlier passage (p. 255) where this was explained.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'attributes of meanings' refers to separate entities distinct from God.
Correction: The phrase is a clarifying genitive; it means the attributes themselves as existent meanings, not separate entities.

Two Categories of Positive Attributes
Sanusi divides positive attributes into two categories based on relation to acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of God's positive attributes into two categories based on their relation to His acts.

Core Idea
Positive attributes are either act-dependent or not.

After covering the negative attributes, Sanusi now introduces the positive attributes. He immediately classifies them into two kinds, based on whether God's acts depend on them. This simple but crucial division structures the entire discussion of divine attributes in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi first transitions from negative attributes to positive attributes, then presents the division.
The passage is very concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two categories: (1) attributes on which God's acts depend, (2) attributes not like that. Notice the specific attributes listed in the first category.
The second category is defined only negatively—'what is other than that.'
3
Understand the rationale for the division
Ask why power, will, knowledge, and life are singled out as act-dependent. The commentary will explain that these are prerequisites for any act to occur.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this division helps avoid confusion about the relationship between God's attributes and His actions. It clarifies which attributes are necessary for action.
This guards against thinking all positive attributes are equally related to acts.

لمَّا فرغَ مِنْ ذكرِ الصفاتِ السلبيَّةِ شرع في ذكرِ الصفاتِ الثبوتيةِ؛ وهي قسمان: الأولُ: ما تتوقَّفُ عليه أفعالُهُ تعالى، الثاني: ما لا يكون كذلكَ. والأولُ: القدرة والإرادة والعلم والحياة، والثاني: ما سوى.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the positive (thubutiyya) attributes of God. After finishing the discussion of negative (salbiyya) attributes, the author turns to positive attributes. He divides them into two kinds: (1) attributes upon which God's acts depend, namely power, will, knowledge, and life; and (2) attributes that are not like that, i.e., attributes that do not serve as prerequisites for God's acts. This division establishes a foundational taxonomy for understanding the divine attributes.

The Theory of States in Divine Attributes
Sanusi explains the theory of states and presents the two positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theory of states (ahwal) as it relates to divine attributes, and the two major positions among Ash'ari theologians on whether states are real entities.

Core Idea
A state is neither existent nor non-existent—a third ontological category.

The theory of states (ahwal) was developed to explain how attributes relate to the essence without implying multiplicity. Sanusi presents the two main positions within the Ash'ari school: those who affirm states as realities intermediate between existence and non-existence, and those who deny them, like al-Ash'ari himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's flow settle.
Notice the technical terms: hal, dhat, sifa wujudiyya.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts two camps: those who affirm states (Imam al-Haramayn, al-Qadi) and those who deny them (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari). Mark the transition phrase: 'As for the one who denies them'.
3
Define the state according to affirmers
According to the affirmers, what exactly is a 'hal'? It is a positive attribute that subsists in an existent but is neither existent nor non-existent. Understand this paradoxical description.
They call it an intermediate between existence and non-existence.
4
Understand the Ash'ari view
Al-Ash'ari denies any third category. For him, the essence being knowing means simply that knowledge subsists in it. There is no additional 'state' of being knowing.
This avoids the ontological puzzle of states.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for divine attributes. Affirming states might help explain how attributes are distinct yet not separate, but denying them keeps ontology simpler.
This connects to the broader kalam discussion on divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما مِنْ صفةٍ وجوديَّةٍ تقومُ بمحل إلا ويكتسب منها ذلكَ المحل حالاً لا يثبت له عند عدم تلك الصفة. هذا عندَ مَنْ يُثبِتُ أحوالاً زائدةً على قيام الصفة بالمحل، ويجعلُها واسطة بين الوجودِ والعدمِ؛ وهو مذهبُ إمام الحرمين والقاضي وجماعة، وحقيقةُ الحالِ عندهم: صفةً ثبوتيةً تقوم بموجود وليسَتْ هي موجودةً ولا معدومةً. وأمَّا مَنْ نفاها - كالشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري رضيَ الله تعالى عنه - فليس عندَهُ إلا الذات والصفاتُ الوجوديَّةُ القائمة بها، وليس ثَمَّ معنى ثالث يقوم بالذاتِ ليسَ بموجودِ ولا معدوم، وما معنى كون الذاتِ عالمةً بالشيء مثلاً عندَهُ إلا أنَّهُ قامَ بها علمٌ يتعلَّق بذلكَ الشيء؛ أي: إدراكاً له.
For there is no existent attribute that subsists in a subject except that that subject acquires from it a state (hal) that is not established for it in the absence of that attribute. This is according to the one who affirms states (ahwal) that are additional to the subsistence of the attribute in the subject, and makes them an intermediate between existence and non-existence; and this is the madhhab of Imam al-Haramayn, al-Qadi, and a group. And the reality of the state (al-hal) according to them is: a positive attribute that subsists in an existent while it is neither existent nor non-existent. As for the one who denies them — like Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him — then according to him there is only the essence (al-dhat) and the existent attributes that subsist in it, and there is no third meaning that subsists in the essence that is neither existent nor non-existent. And the meaning of the essence being knowing of something, for example, according to him is only that knowledge related to that thing subsists in it; i.e., a perception of it.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the theory of states (ahwal) as applied to divine attributes. The author begins with a general principle: every existent attribute that subsists in a subject gives rise to a state (hal) in that subject that is not present when the attribute is absent. For example, the attribute of knowledge gives rise to the state of 'being knowing' in the subject. This is the view of those who affirm states as realities intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author identifies this as the madhhab of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani) and others. According to them, a state is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) that subsists in an existent but is itself neither existent nor non-existent. Then the author presents the opposing view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who denies states. For al-Ash'ari, there is only the essence (dhat) and the existent attributes (sifat wujudiyya) that subsist in it; there is no third entity. The meaning of 'the essence is knowing' is simply that knowledge related to the object subsists in it, i.e., it perceives that object. This passage is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari position on the relationship between attributes and their effects.

Key Terms
الأحوال
الصفات الوجودية
الحال
الذات
الإمام الحرمين
القاضي
أبو الحسن الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the state is an additional existent attribute separate from the essence, like a second attribute.
Correction: Both sides agree that the state is not an additional existent attribute. The affirmers say it is a positive attribute but neither existent nor non-existent, while the deniers say it is merely the attribute's subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi, what is a 'hal' (state)?
A) An existent attribute that subsists in the essence
B) A non-existent attribute
C) A positive attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent
D) A negative attribute

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school is divided on whether states are real entities that mediate between essence and attribute, with al-Ash'ari himself denying them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows theologians debating the exact ontological status of the relationship between Allah and His attributes. When you say 'Allah is Knowing,' do you ever pause to consider what that statement actually means about His essence? Does your understanding rest on clear categories or on acceptance of mystery?
?
The scholars differed on whether a 'state' is real or not. How do you handle theological concepts that resist neat definition—are you quick to dismiss them, or do you sit with the uncertainty? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in human reason versus divine revelation?
?
Al-Ash'ari denied states, affirming only essence and attributes. If you were pressed to defend why we say 'Allah is knowing' without adding a third thing, would you be able to articulate it from this lesson? Or would you rely on vague familiarity? Test yourself: could you explain the two positions to someone else?

The Concept of Ahwal (States) in Divine Attributes
Sanusi defines ahwal and presents the affirmers' position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of ahwal (states) as intermediate realities between existence and non-existence, and how they arise from the attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
Ahwal are neither existent nor non-existent; they are the states that attributes impart to essences.

This lesson introduces the Ash'ari doctrine of ahwal, which Sanusi presents as the necessary states that accompany existential attributes like knowledge and power. We will explore how the 'judgment' of an attribute is precisely this state, and how some scholars affirm ahwal as real yet intermediate entities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
Read Sanusi's analogy of 'degree of knowledge' and 'silk garment' to see how addition (idafa) specifies the kind. This sets up the idea that attributes bestow something on their loci.
The analogy is from common Arabic usage.
2
Grasp the definition of 'ahkam'
Notice that 'its judgments' (ahkamiha) refers to the spiritual states necessary for attributes. For knowledge, its judgment is that the locus becomes knowing. This general principle applies to all existential attributes.
The pronoun in 'ahkamiha' refers back to 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani).
3
Learn the affirmers' view
Those who affirm ahwal (like Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi) consider them as realities that are neither existent nor non-existent but intermediate. Understand the definition of hal as an affirmative attribute subsisting in an existent.
This position makes ahwal a third category beyond existence and non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة مثلاً ، ونظير هذه الإضافة قولُكَ : بلغ فلانٌ درجة العلم ، ومرتبة الإمامة ؛ أي : درجةً هي العلم ، ومرتبةً هي الإمامة ، ويصح أن تكونَ الإضافة في جميع ذلك بتقدير (مِنْ) كقولك : ثوبُ خزّ ، ونحوه . والضمير في ( أحكامها ( يعود على ( صفاتِ المعاني ) ، والمراد بأحكامها : الأحوال المعنويَّةُ اللازمة لها ؛ كالعلم مثلاً ؛ فإنَّ حكمه : أنَّ مَنْ قامَ بهِ يكتسب منه حالاً ؛ وهي أنْ يكونَ عالماً بما تعلَّقَ بهِ ذلكَ العلم ، مُدرِكاً له ، وقس على هذا ، فما مِنْ صفةٍ وجوديَّةٍ تقومُ بمحل إلا ويكتسب منها ذلكَ المحل حالاً لا يثبت له عند عدم تلك الصفة . هذا عندَ مَنْ يُثبِتُ أحوالاً زائدةً على قيام الصفة بالمحل ، ويجعلُها واسطة بين الوجودِ والعدمِ ؛ وهو مذهبُ إمام الحرمين والقاضي وجماعة ، وحقيقةُ الحالِ عندهم : صفةً إثبات تقوم بموجود وليسَتْ هي موجودةً ولا معدومةً . وأمَّا مَنْ نفاها (۱) - كالشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري رضيَ الله تعالى عنه - : فليس عندَهُ إلا الذات والصفاتُ الوجوديَّةُ القائمة بها ، وليس ثَمَّ معنى ثالث يقوم بالذاتِ ليسَ بموجودِ ولا معدوم ، وما معنى كون الذاتِ عالمةً بالشيء مثلاً عندَهُ إلا أنَّهُ قامَ بها علمٌ يتعلَّق بذلكَ الشيء ؛ أي : إدرااً لهُ . والنفسُ أميلُ إلى المذهب الأولِ ؛ لأنَّ التعلق الذي للعلم مثلاً لو لم يكتسب محله منه مثلَهُ لَمَا فُرِّقَ بينَ ذلك المحل وغيره ممَّا لم يقم به علمٌ ؛ لأنَّ المدرِكَ على هذا التقدير العلم ، لا محله ، والذي يقتضيهِ النظرُ والحنُّ أنَّ المحلَّ الذي يقومُ بهِ العلمُ مثلاً يكتسب بقيام العلمِ بهِ حالة زائدة على مُجرَّدِ قيام العلمِ بهِ ؛ وهو أن ينكشف له الشيء الذي تعلَّق بهِ العلمُ القائم به . وبالجملة : فالمسألةُ مشهورةُ الخلافِ ، وأدلة الفريقين فيها في المطوّلات (۱) ، والوهُمُ فيها قوي المعارضة للعقل [؟] طة مبسود [؟] والجهل فيها لا يضرُّ بالعقائدِ . قوله : ( وما يتعلَّق بذلك ) يعني : كاستدلاله على استحالة أن يكون فعله جلَّ وعزَّ بطبع أو تعليل ، واستحالة أن تكون أحكامه تعالى أو أفعاله لغرض ، واستحالة اتصاف ذاتِهِ العليَّة بالحوادث .
Like knowledge, power, and will for example, and an example of this addition is your saying: So-and-so attained the degree of knowledge and the rank of imamate; that is, a degree which is knowledge, and a rank which is imamate. And it is valid for the addition in all that to be with the estimation of (min), like your saying: a garment of silk, and the like. And the pronoun in 'its judgments' (ahkamiha) refers back to 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani), and the intended meaning by 'its judgments' is the spiritual states (ahwal ma'nawiyya) necessary for them; like knowledge for example; for its judgment is that the one in whom it subsists acquires from it a state (hal), namely that he becomes knowledgeable of what that knowledge is related to, perceiving it. And analogize to this, for there is no existential attribute subsisting in a locus except that that locus acquires from it a state which is not established for it in the absence of that attribute. This is according to those who affirm states (ahwal) additional to the subsistence of the attribute in the locus, and make them an intermediate between existence and non-existence; and this is the school of Imam al-Haramayn, al-Qadi, and a group. And the reality of the state (hal) according to them is: an affirmative attribute subsisting in an existent yet it is neither existent nor non-existent. As for those who deny them (1)—like Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him—then according to him there is only the essence (dhat) and the existential attributes subsisting in it, and there is no third meaning subsisting in the essence which is neither existent nor non-existent. And what is the meaning of the essence being knowing of something, for example, according to him except that there subsists in it a knowledge that relates to that thing, i.e., an apprehension of it. And the soul is more inclined to the first school because the relation (ta'alluq) which belongs to knowledge, for example, if its locus did not acquire from it something like it, then there would be no distinction between that locus and another which had no knowledge subsisting in it; for the perceiver on this estimation is the knowledge, not its locus. And what is required by rational consideration and [؟] is that the locus in which knowledge subsists, for example, acquires by the subsistence of knowledge in it an additional state beyond mere subsistence of knowledge in it, namely that the thing to which the subsisting knowledge is related becomes disclosed to it. In summary: this issue is a well-known point of disagreement, and the proofs of the two groups regarding it are in the lengthy works, and the suspicion (wahm) in it strongly opposes reason [؟] and ignorance of it does not harm the beliefs. His statement: 'And what pertains to that' means: like his inference of the impossibility that His action, exalted and majestic, be by nature or necessitation, and the impossibility that His judgments or actions be for a purpose, and the impossibility of the attributes of His supreme essence being subject to temporal events.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the doctrine of 'ahwal' (states) as it pertains to the attributes of God. The author, Sanusi, explains the concept and presents the two main positions in the Ash'ari school. 

He begins with an analogy: saying 'knowledge degree' is like saying 'silk garment' where the addition specifies the kind. Then he clarifies that the term 'judgments' (ahkam) of the attributes of meanings refers to the necessary spiritual states (ahwal ma'nawiyya) that accompany those attributes. For example, the 'judgment' of knowledge is that the subject becomes knowing and perceiving of the known object. He generalizes this to all existential attributes: every attribute bestows a state on its locus that is absent when the attribute is absent.

He then contrasts two views: those who affirm states (ahwal) as additional realities intermediate between existence and non-existence—attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani?), and a group—and those who deny them, notably Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari himself. For the affirmers, a state is an 'affirmative attribute' that subsists in an existent but is neither existent nor non-existent. For the deniers, there are only essences (dhat) and existential attributes; being knowledgeable simply means that knowledge subsists in the essence and relates to the object.

Sanusi then expresses his inclination toward the first view (affirmation of states). His argument: if the relation (ta'alluq) of knowledge did not impart a state to its locus, there would be no distinction between a knowing being and a non-knowing one, since the perceiver would be the knowledge itself, not the subject. Reason and [؟] indicate that the locus acquires an additional state of disclosure.

He concludes that the issue is well-known, the proofs are lengthy, and that suspicion strongly opposes reason, but ignorance of this subtle matter does not harm one's core beliefs about God. Finally, he notes an application of this discussion: inferences that God's actions cannot be by nature or for a purpose, and that His essence cannot be subject to temporal events—likely referring to arguments that rely on the concept of states.

حال
صفات المعاني
أحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That ahwal are either fully existent or non-existent like other entities.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that ahwal are neither existent nor non-existent; they are an intermediate category.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the 'judgment' (hukm) of an attribute like knowledge?

B) That the locus acquires a state of being knowing and perceiving.
C) That the attribute is non-existent.
D) That the locus becomes identical with the attribute.

Key Takeaway
Ahwal are the states that attributes bestow on their loci, affirmed as realities that are neither existent nor non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have attributes like knowledge and patience. Do the states of being 'knowing' and 'patient' truly manifest in your actions, or are you content with just possessing the attributes without the states? What must you change so that your states are real, even if imperfect?
?
The affirmers say a state is 'neither existent nor non-existent'—it is an intermediate reality. Consider your faith: are there areas where you pretend to have full certainty or full ignorance when in truth you are in the middle? Will you honestly admit your intermediate state and seek to move toward genuine existence?
?
Every existential attribute you have gives you a state. What state does your attribute of 'belief' (iman) give you right now? If that state is weak or absent, what are you willing to sacrifice to make the attribute truly produce its judgment in your life?

The Debate over Ahwal: Al-Ash'ari's Denial and Sanusi's Argument
Sanusi presents the deniers' view and his argument for affirming ahwal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument between those who affirm ahwal (states) and those who deny them, and Sanusi's reasoning for favoring the affirmation view.

Core Idea
Knowing is not just having knowledge; the locus acquires a state of disclosure.

In this lesson, we dive into the famous debate on ahwal. After presenting the affirmers' view, Sanusi contrasts it with the denial of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, then gives his own argument that if the relation of knowledge did not impart a state to its locus, there would be no distinction between a knower and a non-knower.

Guided Path
1
Learn the deniers' position
Read how al-Ash'ari and his followers deny any additional state beyond the essence and its attributes. For them, being knowing simply means knowledge subsists in the essence and relates to the object.
The deniers reject a third category between existence and non-existence.
2
Follow Sanusi's argument
Sanusi argues that if the relation (ta'alluq) did not impart a state to the locus, there would be no distinction between a knower and a non-knower, since the perceiver would be the knowledge itself, not the subject. Reason indicates that the locus acquires an additional state of disclosure.
This is the core of the argument for affirming ahwal.
3
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that the issue is well-known and proofs are lengthy, but ignorance of it does not harm core beliefs. He then notes applications: God's actions cannot be by nature or for a purpose, etc.
The final gloss shows practical implications of the ahwal debate.

Like knowledge, power, and will for example, and an example of this addition is your saying: So-and-so attained the degree of knowledge and the rank of imamate; that is, a degree which is knowledge, and a rank which is imamate. And it is valid for the addition in all that to be with the estimation of (min), like your saying: a garment of silk, and the like. And the pronoun in 'its judgments' (ahkamiha) refers back to 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani), and the intended meaning by 'its judgments' is the spiritual states (ahwal ma'nawiyya) necessary for them; like knowledge for example; for its judgment is that the one in whom it subsists acquires from it a state (hal), namely that he becomes knowledgeable of what that knowledge is related to, perceiving it. And analogize to this, for there is no existential attribute subsisting in a locus except that that locus acquires from it a state which is not established for it in the absence of that attribute. This is according to those who affirm states (ahwal) additional to the subsistence of the attribute in the locus, and make them an intermediate between existence and non-existence; and this is the school of Imam al-Haramayn, al-Qadi, and a group. And the reality of the state (hal) according to them is: an affirmative attribute subsisting in an existent yet it is neither existent nor non-existent. As for those who deny them (1)—like Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him—then according to him there is only the essence (dhat) and the existential attributes subsisting in it, and there is no third meaning subsisting in the essence which is neither existent nor non-existent. And what is the meaning of the essence being knowing of something, for example, according to him except that there subsists in it a knowledge that relates to that thing, i.e., an apprehension of it. And the soul is more inclined to the first school because the relation (ta'alluq) which belongs to knowledge, for example, if its locus did not acquire from it something like it, then there would be no distinction between that locus and another which had no knowledge subsisting in it; for the perceiver on this estimation is the knowledge, not its locus. And what is required by rational consideration and [؟] is that the locus in which knowledge subsists, for example, acquires by the subsistence of knowledge in it an additional state beyond mere subsistence of knowledge in it, namely that the thing to which the subsisting knowledge is related becomes disclosed to it. In summary: this issue is a well-known point of disagreement, and the proofs of the two groups regarding it are in the lengthy works, and the suspicion (wahm) in it strongly opposes reason [؟] and ignorance of it does not harm the beliefs. His statement: 'And what pertains to that' means: like his inference of the impossibility that His action, exalted and majestic, be by nature or necessitation, and the impossibility that His judgments or actions be for a purpose, and the impossibility of the attributes of His supreme essence being subject to temporal events.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the doctrine of 'ahwal' (states) as it pertains to the attributes of God. The author, Sanusi, explains the concept and presents the two main positions in the Ash'ari school. 

He begins with an analogy: saying 'knowledge degree' is like saying 'silk garment' where the addition specifies the kind. Then he clarifies that the term 'judgments' (ahkam) of the attributes of meanings refers to the necessary spiritual states (ahwal ma'nawiyya) that accompany those attributes. For example, the 'judgment' of knowledge is that the subject becomes knowing and perceiving of the known object. He generalizes this to all existential attributes: every attribute bestows a state on its locus that is absent when the attribute is absent.

He then contrasts two views: those who affirm states (ahwal) as additional realities intermediate between existence and non-existence—attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani?), and a group—and those who deny them, notably Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari himself. For the affirmers, a state is an 'affirmative attribute' that subsists in an existent but is neither existent nor non-existent. For the deniers, there are only essences (dhat) and existential attributes; being knowledgeable simply means that knowledge subsists in the essence and relates to the object.

Sanusi then expresses his inclination toward the first view (affirmation of states). His argument: if the relation (ta'alluq) of knowledge did not impart a state to its locus, there would be no distinction between a knowing being and a non-knowing one, since the perceiver would be the knowledge itself, not the subject. Reason and [؟] indicate that the locus acquires an additional state of disclosure.

He concludes that the issue is well-known, the proofs are lengthy, and that suspicion strongly opposes reason, but ignorance of this subtle matter does not harm one's core beliefs about God. Finally, he notes an application of this discussion: inferences that God's actions cannot be by nature or for a purpose, and that His essence cannot be subject to temporal events—likely referring to arguments that rely on the concept of states.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sanusi outright rejects the affirmers' view or that ignorance of this issue is harmful.
Correction: Sanusi leans toward affirmation but says ignorance does not harm beliefs.

C) The essence would become identical with knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi argues that affirming ahwal is necessary to explain the distinction between a knowing subject and a non-knowing one, though ignorance of this subtle point does not harm core beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know Allah', do you truly believe that this knowledge changes the very state of your soul? Or do you treat it as mere information stored in your mind, with no transformative disclosure?
?
If the relation of your knowledge to the truth did not create a new state in you—a state of conviction and disclosure—then what distinguishes you from someone who merely recites facts without belief? Will you today make your knowledge a state that unveils the truth to your heart?
?
Sanusi says ignorance of this subtle issue does not harm core beliefs—but what if your neglect of such precise truths has dulled your awareness of how your knowledge of God should transform you? Is your faith merely a set of propositions, or a state of disclosure that changes how you see everything?

Three Impossibilities Regarding Divine Acts and Attributes
The author enumerates three impossibilities related to God's acts and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three things that are impossible regarding Allah's acts and attributes according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Allah's actions are not like ours — they are free from nature, purpose, and temporality.

Sanusi mentions three key impossibilities that protect the transcendence of Allah. These are foundational for understanding divine attributes in Islamic theology. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path

The Four Required Topics on Divine Power
Sanusi enumerates the four required topics on divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four essential topics that must be addressed when discussing God's power in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
The discussion of divine power requires four distinct inquiries.

Sanusi outlines the four foundational topics that structure any theological treatment of God's power. Before providing proofs, he establishes the framework: proving divine power, its nature as an additional attribute, its eternal permanence, and its universal scope. This lesson introduces that framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Note how the four topics are listed sequentially.
The passage uses 'الأوّل', 'الثاني', 'الثالث', 'الرابع' to enumerate.
2
Identify each topic
Find each of the four topics. The first is proving that God is powerful. The second is that this power is additional to the essence. The third is the necessity of its eternality and permanence. The fourth is its relation to all possible things.
The second topic explicitly denies identity between power and essence.
3
Consider the logical order
Notice the progression: first establish that God is powerful, then define the nature of that power, then discuss its temporality, then its scope.
This order moves from existence to attributes to modality to extent.
4
Reflect on the purpose
This list is an outline for the chapter. Each topic will be treated in detail. Ask why each topic is necessary for a complete understanding of divine power.
The list itself is not a proof but a roadmap.

They are: (1) proving that God is powerful, (2) that this power is an attribute additional to the essence (not identical), (3) that this power is necessarily eternal and permanent, and (4) that this power relates to all possible things. Each of these will be addressed in detail in subsequent sections.

Defining al-Qādir: The Powerful with Will
Sanusi defines al-qādir and begins distinguishing it from cause and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-qādir as a voluntary agent with will, and why causes and natures are not called powerful.

Core Idea
True power requires will and the ability to act or refrain.

Before proving that God is powerful, Sanusi carefully defines what 'powerful' means. He distinguishes divine power from mere causal efficacy, showing that a cause or nature cannot be called powerful because they lack will and cannot choose to refrain. This lesson establishes the precise meaning of a key attribute in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Let the author's wording settle. Then read the translation to confirm understanding.
The passage is incomplete because it continues onto the next page, but the definition and the start of the distinction are present.
2
Identify the definition of al-qādir
Find the sentence that defines al-qādir. Notice that it includes both action and refraining, and ties possibility to will.
3
Contrast with cause and nature
Sanusi explicitly excludes causes and natures from being called 'powerful'. Identify the two reasons: lack of will and inability to refrain from their effect.
Then he begins a further distinction between cause and nature, but that part is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المطلب الأوَّلُ - وهو إثباتُ كونِهِ تعالى قادراً - : فينبغي أَنْ نُبَيِّنَ أوَّلاً معنى القادرِ ، وحينئذٍ نذكرُ الدليل على ثبوتِهِ .
أما معناه فنقولُ : القادرُ : هو الذي يصح منه الفعلُ والترْكُ بحسَبِ إرادتِهِ ، فلا تُسمَّى العلَّةُ قادرة على معلولها ، ولا الطبيعة قادرةً على مطبوعها ; لعدم الإرادة منهما ، وعدم تأتي تركهما لأثرهما لو أَثَرا .
[ الفرقُ بينَ العلَّةِ والطبيعةِ ]
والفرقُ بينَ العلَّةِ والطبيعةِ عندَ مَنْ يقولُ بتأثيرهما مِنَ الملحدة أبعدهم الله تعالى : أنَّ العلَّةَ لا يتوقَّفُ تأثيرها على شيءٍ (۱) ، ومِنْ ثَمَّ استحال وجود
As for the first topic—which is proving that He, exalted, is powerful—it is fitting that we first explain the meaning of al-qādir (the powerful), and then we mention the proof for its affirmation. As for its meaning, we say: al-qādir is the one from whom both action and refraining are possible according to His will. So a cause is not called powerful over its effect, nor is a nature called powerful over its natural product, because of the absence of will from both of them, and the absence of the possibility of them refraining from their effect if they were to effect. [The difference between cause and nature] And the difference between cause and nature according to those who assert their efficacy from among the atheists—may Allah distance them—is that the cause's effect does not depend on anything, and from that, existence is impossible...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage contains the definition of al-qādir (the powerful) as a prelude to proving God's power. The author first states that before proving that God is powerful, we must define what 'powerful' means. The definition is: one from whom both action and refraining are possible in accordance with His will. This definition excludes mere causes (like a fire burning) and natures (like fire naturally burning), because they lack will and cannot refrain from their effect. Then the author begins a distinction between cause and nature as understood by atheists who believe in their efficacy: a cause does not depend on anything for its effect, whereas a nature requires conditions and absence of obstacles. The passage cuts off mid-sentence at 'existence is impossible' (implying that a nature can be prevented, while a cause cannot).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'powerful' applies to any cause or natural phenomenon.
Correction: Sanusi restricts 'powerful' to agents with will and the ability to act or refrain. Causes and natures lack will and cannot refrain, so they are not called powerful.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition, why is a natural cause not called 'powerful'?
A) Because it does not have knowledge.
B) Because it lacks will and cannot refrain from its effect.
C) Because it is not eternal.
D) Because it is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
Al-qādir is defined as one who can act or refrain according to will, which excludes mere causes and natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, are you doing it with deliberate intention (niyya) or merely out of habit? What would change if you reminded yourself that your action is your chosen response to the Powerful One who acts with will?
?
You have free will to act or refrain. Are there moments when you act like a cause—mindlessly reacting without conscious intention? How can you practice being more deliberate in your obedience, imitating the attribute of willful action that distinguishes the Creator?
?
If God's power is tied to His will, then your existence and every moment are not random outcomes of blind nature. How does knowing that your life is sustained by a Willful, Personal Being affect your trust in Him today? What would you do differently if you truly believed that?

Definition of Qadir (القادر)
Sanusi defines qadir and contrasts it with cause and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'qadir' (the powerful) as defined by Sanusi and why causes and natures cannot be called qadir.

Core Idea
To be qadir is to have both the ability to act and the ability to refrain, by will.

Before proving that Allah is powerful, Sanusi first clarifies what 'powerful' means. His definition immediately excludes two alternative conceptions of agency: cause and nature. Understanding this definition is essential to grasping the Islamic position on divine power and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of qadir. Note the key components: act (الفعل), abstention (الترك), and will (الإرادة).
The phrase 'بحسب إرادته' is crucial.
2
Contrast with cause and nature
Notice why Sanusi says cause and nature are not called qadir: they lack will and cannot refrain from their effects.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If something always produces its effect without the possibility of refraining, can it be called powerful in this sense? Why or why not?
Think about fire and heat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأوّلُ : إثباتُ كونه تعالى قادراً .
الثاني : [كون ذلك تكون معها شيئاً واحداً] بقدرة زائدة على الذاتِ ، لا مُتَحدةٍ بها .
الثالثُ : وجوب قدم تلك القدرة ووجوب بقائها .
الرابع : أنَّ تلكَ القدرةَ مُتعلّقة بجميعِ الممكنات .
دليل كونه تعالى قادراً
أما المطلب الأوَّلُ - وهو إثباتُ كونِهِ تعالى قادراً - : فينبغي أَنْ نُبَيِّنَ أوَّلاً معنى القادرِ ، وحينئذٍ نذكرُ الدليل على ثبوتِهِ .
أما معناه فنقولُ : القادرُ : هو الذي يصح منه الفعلُ والترْكُ بحسَبِ إرادتِهِ ، فلا تُسمَّى العلَّةُ قادرة على معلولها ، ولا الطبيعة قادرةً على مطبوعها ؛ لعدم الإرادة منهما ، وعدم تأتي تركهما لأثرهما لو أَثَرا .
الفرقُ بينَ العلَّةِ والطبيعةِ
والفرقُ بينَ العلَّةِ والطبيعةِ عندَ مَنْ يقولُ بتأثيرهما مِنَ الملحدة أبعدهم الله تعالى :
أنَّ العلَّةَ لا يتوقَّفُ تأثيرها على شيءٍ ، ومِنْ ثَمَّ استحال وجود العلَّة دونَ معلولها ؛ كحركة الإصبع بالنسبة إلى حركة الخاتم المجعولة فيه مثلاً .
وأما الطبيعة فقد يتوقَّفُ تأثيرها على شرط ونفي مانع ؛ كتأثير النارِ في الإحراق عندَهم ؛ فإِنَّهُ يتوقَّفُ على شرط ؛ وهو مماسة النارِ عندهم للشيء المحترق ، ونفي مانع ؛ وهو عدم بلل ذلك الشيء المحترق .
فصارَتْ أقسامُ الفاعل بحسَبِ تقديرِ العقل ثلاثة : قادرٌ ويُسمَّى مختاراً ؛ وهو الذي تقدَّم ، وعلةٌ ، وطبيعة ، وكلُّها موجودة عند ملحدة الفلاسفة أهلكَهمُ الله تعالى ، ومذهب أهل الحق قاطبةً : بطلان تأثير القسمين الأخيرين ، وإنَّما الموجودُ القسم الأول ، ثم لا يصدق حقيقةً إلا على مولانا جل وعزَّ ؛ لاستحالةِ أنْ يكونَ لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى جملةً وتفصيلاً تأثير في أثر ما .
First: Proving that He, exalted, is powerful (qadir).
Second: [That that is with it something one] with a power additional to the essence, not united with it.
Third: The obligation of the pre-eternity of that power and the obligation of its permanence.
Fourth: That that power is connected to all possible things.
Proof that He, exalted, is powerful
As for the first demand—which is proving that He, exalted, is powerful—it is necessary that we first explain the meaning of 'powerful' (al-qadir), and then we mention the proof of its affirmation.
As for its meaning, we say: The powerful (al-qadir) is the one from whom action (al-fi'l) and abstention (al-tark) are possible according to His will (iradatihi). So the cause (al-illa) is not called powerful over its effect, nor is nature (al-tabi'a) called powerful over its natural product, due to the absence of will from both of them and the absence of the possibility of their refraining from their effect if they were to effect.
The difference between the cause and nature:
And the difference between the cause and nature according to those who assert their effecting from among the atheists—may Allah distance them—is:
That the cause's effecting does not depend on any condition, and hence the existence of the cause without its effect is impossible, like the movement of the finger in relation to the movement of the ring placed on it, for example.
As for nature, its effecting may depend on a condition and the absence of an impediment, like the effect of fire in burning according to them; for it depends on a condition, which is the contact of the fire with the combustible thing according to them, and the absence of an impediment, which is the absence of wetness of that combustible thing.
So the divisions of the agent (al-fa'il) according to intellectual estimation became three: a powerful one (qadir)—also called a free chooser (mukhtar)—which is what preceded, a cause (illa), and a nature (tabi'a). All of them exist according to the atheist philosophers—may Allah destroy them. And the madhhab of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) entirely is the nullity of the effecting of the last two divisions, and that which exists is only the first division. Then it is not truly applicable except to our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, due to the impossibility that for everything other than Him, exalted, collectively and individually, there be any effecting in any effect.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. Sanusi begins by enumerating four topics related to God's power (qudra): proving He is qadir, that His power is additional to His essence, that it is eternal, and that it extends to all possibles. He then focuses on the first topic and states the method: first define 'qadir', then prove it. The definition of qadir is given as 'the one from whom action and abstention are possible according to His will'. This excludes causes and natures, which lack will and cannot refrain from their effects. Sanusi then explains the difference between a cause and a nature according to atheist philosophers: a cause produces its effect necessarily without condition, while a nature requires a condition and absence of impediment. He then classifies agents into three types: qadir (mukhtar), cause, and nature. According to the atheist philosophers, all three exist, but according to Ahl al-Haqq, only the first exists, and it truly applies only to God, since nothing else has any real effect.

Key Terms
القادر
الفعل
الترك
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'powerful' simply means 'able to do something', without the aspect of will and abstention.
Correction: Sanusi specifies that qadir is the one from whom both action and abstention are possible according to will. This excludes mere ability without choice.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition, what is the key element that distinguishes a qadir from a cause or nature?
A) The ability to act
B) The ability to refrain from acting
C) Both action and abstention according to will
D) The absence of conditions

Key Takeaway
To be qadir means having the willful choice to act or refrain, which is unlike the necessary effect of a cause or nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit you feel is 'just your nature'. According to this lesson, is that habit truly beyond your control, or have you simply abandoned the use of your God-given power to refrain? What one step will you take today to exercise that power?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel like it was inevitable—like a cause producing its effect? How does Sanusi's definition of qadir expose that excuse as false? What will you do the next time that excuse arises?
?
If you believe that God is qadir, do you live as if you are qadir over your own actions? Or have you surrendered your will to habits and desires, acting more like a cause or nature? What change must you make to align your self-perception with this truth?

Distinction between Cause and Nature and the Three Kinds of Agents
Sanusi explains the difference between cause and nature and divides agents into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a cause and a nature according to atheist philosophers, and the three-fold classification of agents that follows.

Core Idea
A cause produces its effect necessarily without conditions; a nature requires conditions; only a qadir acts by will.

Having defined qadir, Sanusi now elaborates on the two alternative conceptions of agency: cause (illa) and nature (tabi'a). He explains their differences as understood by atheist philosophers, then concludes that only the first kind of agent truly exists according to Ahl al-Haqq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
Read the description of cause and nature. Note that a cause never exists without its effect, while a nature may require conditions.
Example: finger movement causes ring movement necessarily.
2
Identify the three divisions
Sanusi then lists three types of agents: qadir (mukhtar), cause, and nature. Notice that he attributes the latter two to atheist philosophers.
3
Learn the position of Ahl al-Haqq
Understand that according to the people of truth, only the first agent truly exists, and it applies ultimately only to Allah.
Why can nothing else truly act? Because all effects ultimately come from Allah.

Proof that God is Powerful through Creation of the World
Sanusi proves God's power via creation's contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues for God's power (qudra) by showing that both action and abstention are possible for Him, based on the existence and temporality of the world.

Core Idea
God's power means He can act or not act—both are possible.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a logical proof for one of God's essential attributes: power. He defines power as the ability to act or abstain, then demonstrates that both must be true of God, otherwise contradictions would arise. This argument relies on the prior established fact that the world is temporally originated (hadith) and not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Notice the concise argumentative style.
The passage contains a disjunctive syllogism.
2
Identify the two horns of the argument
Look for the two conditional statements: (1) if God could not abstain, He would be a cause/nature and the world would be eternal; (2) if God could not act, He would be incapable and the world would not exist.
Both conclusions contradict known facts.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since both alternatives lead to impossibilities, both abstention and action must be possible for God, which is the definition of qadir.
4
Connect to other attributes
Sanusi ends by noting that the act indicates attributes like knowledge, life, power, and will, which are necessary for the act to occur.
This links to the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أنَّهُ تعالى قادرٌ - أي : يصح منه الفعل والترك - : إيجاده جل وعزَّ للعالم ؛ إذ لو لم يتأت منه الترك لكان علة أو طبيعةً ، تعالى عن ذلكَ ، فيلزمُ قدم العالم على ما يأتي في [فضل] الإرادة ، وقد عرفت برهان حدوثِهِ ، ولو لم يتأتَ منه الفعلُ لكان عاجزاً ، تعالى وجل عن كل نقص ، فيلزمُ ألا يُوجَدَ العالم ، وقد سبق برهان احتياج الفعل من العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة ؛ وهي الصفات التي دلّ عليها فعله تعالى ، واستحال أن يكون الفعل بدونها .
And the proof that He, exalted, is powerful (qadir)—i.e., that action and abstention are possible from Him—is His, may He be glorified and exalted, bringing into existence the world (al-alam). For if abstention were not possible from Him, He would be a cause or a nature—exalted is He above that—then the pre-eternity of the world would follow, as will come in [the section on] will, and you have already known the proof of its temporal origination (huduth). And if action were not possible from Him, He would be incapable (ajiz)—exalted and glorified is He above every deficiency—then it would follow that the world would not exist. And the proof has already preceded that the act requires the attributes of knowledge, life, power, and will; and they are the attributes that His act, exalted, indicates, and it is impossible for the act to be without them.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's power (qudra). Sanusi presents an argument based on the creation of the world. The proof is structured as a disjunctive syllogism: either God can abstain from acting or He cannot. If He cannot abstain, He would be a necessary cause or nature, which would imply the world is eternal (qadim). But the world is temporally originated (hadith), as previously proven, so this is impossible. If God cannot act, He would be incapable (ajiz). But the world exists, so He must be able to act. Therefore, both action and abstention are possible for Him, which is the definition of qadir. Sanusi also references that the act requires attributes like knowledge, life, power, and will, which are indicated by the act, and the act cannot exist without them.

Key Terms
القادر
الفعل
الترك
العلة
الطبيعة
القديم
الحادث
العاجز
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes that God created the world, which already presupposes His power.
Correction: The proof does not assume God's power in the premise; it takes the fact of creation as given (from scripture or earlier proofs) and uses it to deduce that both action and abstention are possible, thereby establishing the attribute of power.

Checkpoint
In the proof, what would follow if God could not abstain from acting?
A) He would be incapable
B) He would be a cause or nature
C) The world would be contingent
D) He would lack will

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves God's power (qudra) by showing that both action and abstention are possible for Him, otherwise contradictions arise from the fact of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could have refrained from creating you, do you live as though your existence is a necessary consequence of His power, or do you recognize it as a free gift? What would change in your gratitude if you truly believed He could have withheld existence from you?
?
The proof shows that if God were a necessary cause, He would be like nature—without choice. Do you ever treat God's commands or blessings as automatic, forgetting that they come from His free will? How does that affect your worship and humility?
?
God's power includes the ability to not create you. Does this reality challenge any sense of entitlement you have? What would it mean to live daily with the awareness that your very existence is a non-necessary act of divine generosity?

Proof of God's Power Through Creation
Sanusi proves God's power through the existence of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that God is able (qādir) by citing creation and why both action and refraining are possible for Him.

Core Idea
Creation itself proves God's power over both acting and refraining.

Sanusi begins by establishing the key proof for divine power. He argues that the very existence of the created world demonstrates that God can both act and refrain. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why the alternative would lead to unacceptable consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a definition of qādir first.
2
Identify the two conditional arguments
Find the two conditional statements: if refraining were not possible, and if action were not possible. Note the consequences drawn for each.
3
Reflect on the proof's necessity
Ask yourself why both conditions must be true for God to be qādir. How does the existence of the world guarantee both?
This connects to the learning objective.

والدليل على أنَّهُ تعالى قادرٌ - أي : يصح منه الفعل والترك - : إيجاده جل وعزَّ للعالم ؛ إذ لو لم يتأت منه الترك لكان علة أو طبيعة ، تعالى عن ذلكَ ، فيلزمُ قدم العالم على ما يأتي في فضل الإرادة ، وقد عرفت برهان حدوثِهِ ، ولو لم يتأتَ منه الفعلُ لكان عاجزاً ، تعالى وجل عن كل نقص ، فيلزمُ ألا يُوجَدَ العالم ، وقد سبق برهان احتياج للفعل ؛ من العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة ؛ وهي الصفات التي دلّ عليها فعله تعالى ، واستحال أن يكون الفعل بدونها . فإن قيل : لو كانَ المؤثر في العالم قادراً لكان قادراً على الفعل والتركِ ؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي إنْ شاءَ فعلَ ، وإِنْ شاءَ تركَ ، لكنَّ الترك يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ مقدوراً ؛ لأنَّهُ نفي وعدمٌ صِرْفٌ ، والقدرة لا بدَّ لها مِنْ أثرٍ ، والعدمُ ليسَ أثراً ، وإلا لزم قدم العالم ، وأيضاً : فالتركُ عدم مستمر ، فلو كان أثراً لزم تحصيل الحاصل فالجواب من وجهين : الأوّلُ : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الترك ليسَ مقدوراً للقادرِ . قولُكم : ( التركُ : نفى محض ، وعدم مستمر ) ، قلنا : ممنوع ؛ فإن الترك هو الكفُّ والإمساك عن الفعل ، وهو أمرٌ وجودي وقولكم : ( يلزمُ عليهِ قدم العالم ، وتحصيل الحاصل ) قلنا : ممنوع ؛ لأنَّ الفعلَ مِنْ حيث هو فعل ينافي الأزل ، وإنَّما تتأتّى حقيقته فيما لا يزالُ ، فلا تركَ إذاً في الأزلِ ، وإِنَّما يتأتى فيما لا يزالُ ، وإذا كانَ التركُ مُتجدّداً بعدَ أنْ لم يكن بطل ما قلتُموهُ : إِنَّهُ تحصيل الحاصل . ومن هنا تعرف : أنَّ قولَ مَنْ قالَ مِنَ الفقهاء : ( إِنَّ التركَ فعل )
The proof that He, Exalted and Glorified, is able (qādir) — i.e., that action (fiʿl) and refraining (tark) are possible from Him — is His creation of the world. For if refraining were not possible from Him, He would be a cause or nature — Exalted is He above that — and then the preeternity of the world would follow, as will come in the section on will, and you already know the proof of its origination. And if action were not possible from Him, He would be incapable — Exalted and Glorified is He above every deficiency — and then the world would not exist, and the proof of the world's need for action (from knowledge, life, power, and will) has already preceded, and these are the attributes indicated by His action, and it is impossible for action to exist without them. For if it is said: If the effective agent in the world were able, he would be able to act and to refrain, because the able is one who, if he wills, does, and if he wills, refrains. But refraining is impossible to be an object of power (maqdūr) because it is pure negation and non-existence, and power must have an effect, and non-existence is not an effect; otherwise the preeternity of the world would follow. Also, refraining is continuous non-existence, so if it were an effect, it would entail the acquisition of what is already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). The answer is from two aspects: The first: We do not accept that refraining is not an object of power for the able. Your statement: 'Refraining is pure negation and continuous non-existence' — we say: It is prevented (mamnūʿ); for refraining is the abstention and withholding from action, and it is an existential matter (amr wujūdī). And your statement: 'It would entail the preeternity of the world and the acquisition of what is already acquired' — we say: It is prevented; because action, inasmuch as it is action, is incompatible with preeternity, and its reality only occurs in what is not yet (fīmā lā yazāl). So there is no refraining in preeternity; it only occurs in what is not yet. And if refraining is renewed after it was not, then what you said, that it is the acquisition of what is already acquired, is invalid. And from this you know that the statement of those jurists who said: 'Refraining is an action' ...
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God is qādir (able/powerful), meaning that both action (fiʿl) and refraining (tark) are possible from Him. The proof is His creation of the world: if refraining were not possible, He would be a necessary cause or nature, implying the world is eternal (contradicting its proven origination). If action were not possible, He would be incapable, and the world would not exist — but it does exist, so action is possible. The proof also references prior arguments for the world's need of attributes like knowledge, life, power, and will. The passage then addresses an objection: if God is able, He must be able to act and refrain, but refraining (tark) cannot be an object of power (maqdūr) because it is pure non-existence, and power requires an existent effect. The answer is twofold: (1) Refraining is not pure non-existence; it is the existential act of abstaining (al-kaff wa al-imṣāk). (2) The consequences raised (preeternity of the world, acquisition of the already acquired) do not follow, because action is impossible in preeternity (it only occurs in time), so refraining is also temporal and renewed, not continuous. Thus, the objection fails. The passage ends by noting that this explains the statement of some jurists that 'refraining is an action' (though the text cuts off).

قادر
فعل
ترك
علة
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the proof that God is able (qādir) according to Sanusi?
A) The world's existence shows both action and refraining are possible.
B) The Quran mentions His power.
C) Reason alone cannot prove it.
D) The prophets taught it.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of the world proves that He is able to both act and refrain, because the alternatives would lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has the power to both act and refrain, and He chose to create you, does your existence feel like a gift or an entitlement? How will you live today in light of that choice?
?
When you sin, do you sometimes act as if God is incapable of holding you accountable, or as if He is a blind force that must punish? How does knowing that God deliberately refrains from punishing you right now shape your repentance?
?
The proof from creation shows that God is not a necessary cause like fire that must burn. Does your prayer reflect a relationship with a free Agent who can answer or refrain as He wills, or do you treat God like an automatic dispenser? What needs to change?

Answering the Objection on Refraining (Tark) as an Object of Power
Sanusi responds to the objection that refraining cannot be an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's two-part refutation of the claim that refraining (tark) cannot be an object of divine power because it is pure non-existence.

Core Idea
Refraining is not mere non-existence; it is a positive act of withholding.

The objection claims that if God is able, He must be able to refrain, but refraining is pure non-existence and thus cannot be an effect of power. Sanusi offers a two-pronged response: first, refraining is an existential act; second, the consequences (preeternity of the world, etc.) do not follow because refraining is temporal. This lesson explains his logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection is based on the definition of qādir: one who can act or refrain. But it argues refraining cannot be maqdūr because it is pure non-existence. Read this part carefully.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
Sanusi's answer has two aspects (wajhayn). Find each one and note how he refutes the objection each time.
First: refraining is existential. Second: refraining is temporal.
3
Reflect on why the consequences are avoided
Think about why the objection's claim that refraining would lead to preeternity or tahsīl al-ḥāṣil doesn't hold if refraining is temporal.
This shows the importance of distinguishing eternal from temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أنَّهُ تعالى قادرٌ - أي : يصح منه الفعل والترك - : إيجاده جل وعزَّ للعالم ؛ إذ لو لم يتأت منه الترك لكان علة أو طبيعة ، تعالى عن ذلكَ ، فيلزمُ قدم العالم على ما يأتي في فضل الإرادة ، وقد عرفت برهان حدوثِهِ ، ولو لم يتأتَ منه الفعلُ لكان عاجزاً ، تعالى وجل عن كل نقص ، فيلزمُ ألا يُوجَدَ العالم ، وقد سبق برهان احتياج للفعل ؛ من العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة ؛ وهي الصفات التي دلّ عليها فعله تعالى ، واستحال أن يكون الفعل بدونها . فإن قيل : لو كانَ المؤثر في العالم قادراً لكان قادراً على الفعل والتركِ ؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي إنْ شاءَ فعلَ ، وإِنْ شاءَ تركَ ، لكنَّ الترك يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ مقدوراً ؛ لأنَّهُ نفي وعدمٌ صِرْفٌ ، والقدرة لا بدَّ لها مِنْ أثرٍ ، والعدمُ ليسَ أثراً ، وإلا لزم قدم العالم ، وأيضاً : فالتركُ عدم مستمر ، فلو كان أثراً لزم تحصيل الحاصل فالجواب من وجهين : الأوّلُ : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الترك ليسَ مقدوراً للقادرِ . قولُكم : ( التركُ : نفى محض ، وعدم مستمر ) ، قلنا : ممنوع ؛ فإن الترك هو الكفُّ والإمساك عن الفعل ، وهو أمرٌ وجودي وقولكم : ( يلزمُ عليهِ قدم العالم ، وتحصيل الحاصل ) قلنا : ممنوع ؛ لأنَّ الفعلَ مِنْ حيث هو فعل ينافي الأزل ، وإنَّما تتأتّى حقيقته فيما لا يزالُ ، فلا تركَ إذاً في الأزلِ ، وإِنَّما يتأتى فيما لا يزالُ ، وإذا كانَ التركُ مُتجدّداً بعدَ أنْ لم يكن بطل ما قلتُموهُ : إِنَّهُ تحصيل الحاصل . ومن هنا تعرف : أنَّ قولَ مَنْ قالَ مِنَ الفقهاء : ( إِنَّ التركَ فعل )

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refraining (tark) is pure non-existence, so God cannot be its agent.
Correction: Sanusi argues that refraining is not pure non-existence; it is an existential act of withholding (kaff wa imsāk). Moreover, even if it were non-existence, the objection fails because refraining is temporal, not eternal.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's first answer to the objection that refraining cannot be an object of power?
A) He accepts the objection but limits it.
B) He denies that refraining is pure non-existence, calling it an existential act.
C) He claims refraining only applies to creatures.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Refraining (tark) is an existential act of withholding, not mere non-existence, and it is temporal, so it can be an object of divine power without logical problems.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you treated your own refraining from sin as a passive 'not doing' rather than an active, deliberate act of obedience? How would seeing it as an existential act of withholding (kaff wa imsāk) change your spiritual effort today? Commit to one specific act of refraining this week and treat it as a positive worship.
?
If God's power includes both action and refraining, do you trust that His withholding from you is a deliberate act of wisdom and mercy? Think of one thing you feel He is withholding from you. Will you thank Him for that specific refraining today as a positive act of care?
?
The scholars said 'al-tark fi'l' (refraining is an action). How does this challenge your view of your own self-control? In what area of your life do you need to actively refrain as an act of worship, and what step will you take right now to make it a deliberate, existential choice?
