Proof That the Creator Must Possess Will
Sanusi presents a proof that the creator must have will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the Creator must possess will (irada) and why will is necessary for the specification of creation.

Core Idea
Without will, the world would remain non-existent forever.

This lesson presents a concise but profound proof from Imam Sanusi that the Creator of the world must have will (irada). The proof builds on the observation that the world exists with specific qualities, time, and measure, which cannot be explained by mere power alone. You will see how the argument flows and why it establishes a key divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source text aloud, then read the English translation. Let the original words and their meaning sink in before analyzing.
Notice the key term 'مريداً' (willing) and 'قاصداً' (intending).
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'الإرادة' (will), 'القصد' (intention), and 'التخصيص' (specification). Understand their role in the proof.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the logical structure
The proof is a conditional argument: if there were no will (intention), then no specification would occur, and the world would remain non-existent. Since the world exists, will must exist.
This is an example of a proof by contradiction.
4
Examine the marginal note
Read the marginal note that clarifies the relationship between will (irada) and intention (qasd). It explains two types of attachment: relational (suluhiyy) and actualizing (tanjiziyy).
This note connects to earlier definitions in the text.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that will is unnecessary because God is all-powerful. The proof shows why power alone is insufficient for specification.
This deepens your appreciation of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثبوتِ صفة القدرة لهُ تعالى ] ويلزمُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم مريداً ؛ أي : قاصداً لفعله (١) ؛ إذْ لولا قصده لتخصيص الفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص على مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة .. للزمَ بقاؤُهُ على ما كانَ عليهِ مِنْ عدم ذلكَ كَلِّهِ أَبدَ الآبادِ(1) في هامش ( ح ) : ( لو قال : « قامت به الإرادة ) لكان أولى ؛ لأن الإرادة سابقة على القصد ( أو أنه مشى على أن للإرادة تعلقاً صُلوحياً وعليه التعريف الأول ، وتنجيزياً وعليه التعريف الثاني ، أعني : المتقدمين شرحاً .
Proof of the establishment of the attribute of power to Him, exalted is He. And it also follows that the originator of the world must be willing (muridan), i.e., intending (qasidan) His act (1). For if not for His intention to specify the act with existence in a particular time, in a particular measure, and with a particular attribute, it would follow that it would remain as it was of non-existence of all that forever and ever. (1) In the margin of (H): 'If he had said: "will subsists in Him (qamat bihi al-irada)" it would be more appropriate, because will precedes intention. Or that he proceeded on the basis that will has a relational (suluhiyy) attachment, and upon that is the first definition, and an actualizing (tanjiziyy) attachment, and upon that is the second definition, meaning that of the later scholars, explanation.'
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that the creator of the world must possess the attribute of will (irada). The proof begins by stating that the originator (muhdith) of the world must be willing—that is, intending (qasid) his action. The reasoning is: if the creator did not intend to specify the act of creation with a particular time, measure, and quality, then the world would remain in its original state of absolute non-existence forever. Since the world does exist, there must be a specification, which requires will. The marginal note offers a clarification: it would be better to say 'will subsists in Him' because will precedes intention. The note also explains that the two definitions given earlier correspond to two types of attachment: relational (suluhiyy) and actualizing (tanjiziyy). The first definition (preponderance) relates to the relational attachment, and the second (intention) relates to the actualizing attachment.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
القصد (al-qasd)
المحدث (al-muhdith)
التخصيص (al-takhsis)
التعلق الصلاحي (al-ta'allaq al-suluhiyy)
التعلق التنجيزي (al-ta'allaq al-tanjiziyy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that will is unnecessary because God is all-powerful and could create by sheer power alone.
Correction: Sanusi shows that power alone cannot specify which possible world to create; will is needed to choose among infinite possibilities. Without will, no particular creation would occur.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's proof, what would happen if the creator did not have will?
A) The world would still exist but differently
B) The world would exist eternally
C) The world would remain non-existent forever
D) The world would exist by necessity

Key Takeaway
Will is necessary for the Creator because without it, the world would not have been brought into existence with its specific characteristics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that the entire universe, including every detail of your life, is not a product of chance but of God's specific will. Where in your life have you been living as if your circumstances are random or beyond divine care? What would change today if you truly believed that God willed your exact situation for a purpose?
?
If without intention the world would remain non-existent, then your own existence is a direct result of God's deliberate act of will. How does this confront your tendency to take your own existence for granted? Will you set aside time tonight to reflect on the 'particular time, measure, and quality' of your life as signs of God's specific will?
?
The proof distinguishes between will and mere power. In your daily actions, do you act with intentional will aligned with God's commands, or do you often act out of habit without conscious intention? Pick one area today where you will replace automatic behavior with a deliberate intention seeking God's pleasure.

Definition and Rulings of Divine Will
Sanusi defines divine will and enumerates its necessary rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine will (irada) and the necessary properties that belong to it according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Divine will is the attribute that tips the scales of possibility toward existence.

Before proving that God must have will, Sanusi first defines what will is and what necessary rulings apply to it. This lesson presents the definition of irada as the attribute by which one possible outcome is chosen over another, and lists its essential properties: eternality, permanence, generality, and freedom from purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Notice that will is defined in two equivalent ways: as the attribute of preponderance and as intention.
The two definitions are meant to make the concept clear.
2
Identify the four rulings
Find the four necessary properties of divine will: eternality, permanence, generality over all possibles, and absence of purpose.
3
Reflect on why these rulings matter
Consider why each ruling is necessary for the divine attribute of will. For example, if will were temporal, it would imply change in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الثاني في إثبات الإرادة وأحكامها
الإرادة : صفةٌ يتأتى بها ترجيح وقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، وإنْ شئت قلت : هي القصد لوقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، ومراده بأحكامِها : ما أثبتَهُ مِنْ وجوب قدمها وبقائها ، ووجوب عمومها لجميع الممكنات ، واستحالة أن تكونَ لغرض
ويلزمُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم مريداً ؛ أي : قاصداً لفعله ؛ إذْ لولا قصده لتخصيص الفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص على مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة .. للزمَ بقاؤُهُ على ما كانَ عليهِ مِنْ عدم ذلكَ كَلِّهِ أَبدَ الآبادِ
يعني : أنَّهُ لَوِ انتفَتْ إرادة الباري تعالى للفعل؛ أي : القصدُ إليهِ . . لاتّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكراهة لوجودِ الفعل، ويلزمُ أنْ تكون تلك الكراهة قديمةً؛ لاستحالةِ اتّصافِهِ تعالى بالحوادثِ، فلا تنعدم أبداً، فلا يُوجَدُ ضدُّها الذي هو القصد أبداً، وذلك يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ حادث أبد الآبادِ؛ لعدم إمكان وجودِ قصدِ مِنَ الفاعل إلى نقلِهِ عن العدمِ الذي كانَ عليهِ إلى الوجودِ.
والقدرة لا تصلحُ أنْ تكونَ مُخصصة للفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص وعلى مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة بدلاً عما يقابل ذلك؛ لأنَّ نسبة القدرةِ إلى جميع الممكنات في كل زمن وعلى كل حال . . نسبة واحدة.
وأيضاً : فشأنُ القدرة التأثيرُ والإيجادُ، والموجدُ مِن حيث هو مُوجِدٌ غيرُ المرجّح مِنْ حيث هو مُرجّح؛ لتوقف الإيجادِ على الترجيح.
وكذا العلم لا يصلح للتخصيص؛ لأنَّ التخصيص للممكن ببعض ما جازَ عليهِ تأثير فيهِ، والعلم ليسَ مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة، وإلا لَمَا تعلَّق بالواجب والمستحيل.
The second section on proving the will and its rulings. The will: an attribute by which the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible occurs. And if you wish, you may say: it is the intention for the occurrence of one of the two sides of the possible. And by its rulings he means what he has established of the necessity of its eternality and permanence, and the necessity of its generality over all possibles, and the impossibility that it be for a purpose. It is also necessary that the Maker of the world be willing, i.e., intending His action; for if it were not for His intention to specify the action with existence at a particular time, to a particular measure, and with a particular quality, then the world would have remained in what it was upon of non-existence of all that forever. That is, if the will of the Most High Creator for the action ceased, i.e., the intention towards it, then the Most High Creator would be described with aversion (karaha) for the existence of the action, and it would be necessary that that aversion be eternal due to the impossibility of the Most High being characterized by temporal events, so it would never cease, so its opposite, which is intention, would never exist, and that necessitates that no temporal event ever occur because of the impossibility of the existence of intention from the agent to transfer it from the non-existence upon which it was to existence. And power (qudra) is not suitable to be the specifier of the action with existence at a particular time, to a particular measure, and with a particular quality instead of what is opposite to that; because the relationship of power to all possibles at every time and in every state is one relationship. Also, the function of power is effect and bringing into existence, and the bringer into existence (al-mujid) qua bringer into existence is other than the preponderator (al-murajjih) qua preponderator, because bringing into existence depends on preponderance (tarjih). Likewise, knowledge (ilm) is not suitable for specification, because specification of the possible to some of what is permissible for it is an influence upon it, and knowledge is not among the effective attributes, otherwise it would not apply to the necessary and the impossible.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the divine attribute of will (irada). It begins by defining irada as the attribute by which one of the two mutually exclusive possibilities is given preponderance, or equivalently, as the intention to bring about one side of a possible. The author then states that by 'its rulings' he means the necessary properties of irada: its eternity, its permanence, its generality over all possibles, and its exclusion of any purpose. 

Next, the proof is presented: The creator of the world must be willing (i.e., have intention) for His action. If He had no intention to specify the action with existence at a particular time, measure, and quality, then the world would remain in non-existence forever. The reasoning is unpacked: if God's will for the action were absent, then God would have aversion (karaha) to the action's existence. Since God cannot acquire new attributes, this aversion would be eternal; therefore, its opposite—intention—would never arise, and consequently no temporal event would ever occur. 

The author then shows that other attributes cannot substitute for will. Power (qudra) alone cannot serve as the specifier because its relation to all possibles at all times is uniform—it does not incline toward any particular possibility. Moreover, the function of power is to bring into existence (ijad), while preponderance (tarjih) is a distinct act; existence depends on preponderance, so power presupposes will. Similarly, knowledge (ilm) is not suitable for specification because specification is an influence on the possible, and knowledge is not an effective attribute—if it were, it could not also know the necessary and the impossible, which are unaffected by influence. 

Thus, the passage establishes that will is a necessary divine attribute distinct from power and knowledge.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الترجيح
الممكن
القصد
قدم
بقاء
عموم
غرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that divine will implies deliberation or change.
Correction: This passage clarifies that will is eternal and not for any purpose.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the following is NOT a ruling of divine will?
A) Eternality
B) Generality
C) Temporality
D) Absence of purpose

Key Takeaway
Divine will is the eternal attribute by which God chooses among possibilities, and it is characterized by being universal and purposeless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's will is not for any purpose. Yet you often act only when you see a benefit. How does your transactional approach to obedience reveal that you have not truly recognized His absolute sovereignty? Will you today choose to obey without seeking a worldly gain?
?
If divine will is universal over all possibles, then nothing in your life escapes His choice. Which area of your life have you been acting as if your own will is the final decider? How will you consciously submit that area to His decree today?
?
The attribute of will is eternal—it never began and never ends. You, however, were created in time. Does your worship reflect that you serve an eternal Being, or do you reduce Him to a timeline like your own? What specific change in your daily routine would show you live in light of His eternal will?

Proof of the Necessity of Divine Will
Sanusi proves the necessity of divine will through the argument from specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the necessity of divine will, based on the impossibility of specification without intention.

Core Idea
Without will, the world would never have come into existence from non-existence.

Sanusi now presents a powerful proof that the Creator must have will. The argument hinges on the need for specification: why does a particular possible thing come into existence at a particular time and in a particular manner? Without an act of will, there would be no reason for one possibility to be chosen over another, and the world would remain in eternal non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The world could have existed differently or not at all; its actual existence requires specification.
2
Follow the reasoning chain
Absence of will implies aversion to existence, which would be eternal, preventing intention and thus any temporal event.
3
Conclude the necessity
Since temporal events exist, divine will must exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الثاني في إثبات الإرادة وأحكامها
الإرادة : صفةٌ يتأتى بها ترجيح وقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، وإنْ شئت قلت : هي القصد لوقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، ومراده بأحكامِها : ما أثبتَهُ مِنْ وجوب قدمها وبقائها ، ووجوب عمومها لجميع الممكنات ، واستحالة أن تكونَ لغرض
ويلزمُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم مريداً ؛ أي : قاصداً لفعله ؛ إذْ لولا قصده لتخصيص الفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص على مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة .. للزمَ بقاؤُهُ على ما كانَ عليهِ مِنْ عدم ذلكَ كَلِّهِ أَبدَ الآبادِ
يعني : أنَّهُ لَوِ انتفَتْ إرادة الباري تعالى للفعل؛ أي : القصدُ إليهِ . . لاتّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكراهة لوجودِ الفعل، ويلزمُ أنْ تكون تلك الكراهة قديمةً؛ لاستحالةِ اتّصافِهِ تعالى بالحوادثِ، فلا تنعدم أبداً، فلا يُوجَدُ ضدُّها الذي هو القصد أبداً، وذلك يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ حادث أبد الآبادِ؛ لعدم إمكان وجودِ قصدِ مِنَ الفاعل إلى نقلِهِ عن العدمِ الذي كانَ عليهِ إلى الوجودِ.
والقدرة لا تصلحُ أنْ تكونَ مُخصصة للفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص وعلى مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة بدلاً عما يقابل ذلك؛ لأنَّ نسبة القدرةِ إلى جميع الممكنات في كل زمن وعلى كل حال . . نسبة واحدة.
وأيضاً : فشأنُ القدرة التأثيرُ والإيجادُ، والموجدُ مِن حيث هو مُوجِدٌ غيرُ المرجّح مِنْ حيث هو مُرجّح؛ لتوقف الإيجادِ على الترجيح.
وكذا العلم لا يصلح للتخصيص؛ لأنَّ التخصيص للممكن ببعض ما جازَ عليهِ تأثير فيهِ، والعلم ليسَ مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة، وإلا لَمَا تعلَّق بالواجب والمستحيل.
The second section on proving the will and its rulings. The will: an attribute by which the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible occurs. And if you wish, you may say: it is the intention for the occurrence of one of the two sides of the possible. And by its rulings he means what he has established of the necessity of its eternality and permanence, and the necessity of its generality over all possibles, and the impossibility that it be for a purpose. It is also necessary that the Maker of the world be willing, i.e., intending His action; for if it were not for His intention to specify the action with existence at a particular time, to a particular measure, and with a particular quality, then the world would have remained in what it was upon of non-existence of all that forever. That is, if the will of the Most High Creator for the action ceased, i.e., the intention towards it, then the Most High Creator would be described with aversion (karaha) for the existence of the action, and it would be necessary that that aversion be eternal due to the impossibility of the Most High being characterized by temporal events, so it would never cease, so its opposite, which is intention, would never exist, and that necessitates that no temporal event ever occur because of the impossibility of the existence of intention from the agent to transfer it from the non-existence upon which it was to existence. And power (qudra) is not suitable to be the specifier of the action with existence at a particular time, to a particular measure, and with a particular quality instead of what is opposite to that; because the relationship of power to all possibles at every time and in every state is one relationship. Also, the function of power is effect and bringing into existence, and the bringer into existence (al-mujid) qua bringer into existence is other than the preponderator (al-murajjih) qua preponderator, because bringing into existence depends on preponderance (tarjih). Likewise, knowledge (ilm) is not suitable for specification, because specification of the possible to some of what is permissible for it is an influence upon it, and knowledge is not among the effective attributes, otherwise it would not apply to the necessary and the impossible.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the divine attribute of will (irada). It begins by defining irada as the attribute by which one of the two mutually exclusive possibilities is given preponderance, or equivalently, as the intention to bring about one side of a possible. The author then states that by 'its rulings' he means the necessary properties of irada: its eternity, its permanence, its generality over all possibles, and its exclusion of any purpose. 

Next, the proof is presented: The creator of the world must be willing (i.e., have intention) for His action. If He had no intention to specify the action with existence at a particular time, measure, and quality, then the world would remain in non-existence forever. The reasoning is unpacked: if God's will for the action were absent, then God would have aversion (karaha) to the action's existence. Since God cannot acquire new attributes, this aversion would be eternal; therefore, its opposite—intention—would never arise, and consequently no temporal event would ever occur. 

The author then shows that other attributes cannot substitute for will. Power (qudra) alone cannot serve as the specifier because its relation to all possibles at all times is uniform—it does not incline toward any particular possibility. Moreover, the function of power is to bring into existence (ijad), while preponderance (tarjih) is a distinct act; existence depends on preponderance, so power presupposes will. Similarly, knowledge (ilm) is not suitable for specification because specification is an influence on the possible, and knowledge is not an effective attribute—if it were, it could not also know the necessary and the impossible, which are unaffected by influence. 

Thus, the passage establishes that will is a necessary divine attribute distinct from power and knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that power alone is sufficient to bring about the world.
Correction: This proof shows that power without will cannot account for why this world exists at this time.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if God did not have will for the action?
A) The action would still occur by power.
B) God would have eternal aversion to the action, preventing its existence.
C) Knowledge would specify the action.
D) The action would occur at a random time.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is necessary because without it, specification of a particular possible world could not occur, and the world would remain in eternal non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah had not willed specifically for you to exist at this time and place, you would have remained in non-existence forever. How does this realization stir you to be more grateful for every moment of your life? What is one concrete action you can take today to show that gratitude?
?
The proof shows that without divine will, there would be no order, no specification—only eternal non-existence. Do you truly believe that every detail of your life, including trials, is deliberately willed by Allah? If so, how does that change the way you respond to difficulty?
?
Sanusi argues that if the Creator had aversion to the world's existence, it would be eternal, and no temporal event could occur. Could it be that your own neglect of Allah's commands stems from a hidden aversion to His will? What would it take for you to surrender completely to His choice for you?

Why Power and Knowledge Cannot Replace Will
Sanusi explains why power and knowledge cannot function as the specifier of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why neither power nor knowledge can replace will in specifying which possible things exist.

Core Idea
Power gives existence, but will chooses what to exist.

Having established that will is necessary, Sanusi now addresses potential objections: Couldn't God's power or knowledge alone be enough to determine which possibilities become reality? He shows why both are insufficient: power relates equally to all possibilities, and knowledge is a passive attribute that does not influence existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of power
Power has a uniform relation to all possibilities, so it cannot specify one. Also, existence depends on preponderance, which is distinct from power.
2
Understand the role of knowledge
Knowledge is not an effective attribute; specification requires influence, which knowledge does not provide.
3
Reflect on the distinctness of will
Will is the only attribute that can break the symmetry of possibilities and determine actual creation.

The Necessity of Will for Creation
Sanusi deduces a chain of consequences from the absence of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that without divine will, no contingent being could ever exist, because God's lack of will would imply eternal aversion.

Core Idea
Without will, creation is impossible.

Sanusi begins his proof for the necessity of will by considering what would happen if God lacked will for any act. This conditional argument reveals that without will, God would be described with aversion, which being eternal would prevent any intention to create, thus no contingent thing could ever emerge. This lesson walks you through that logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if will were absent, then aversion would follow, leading to...'
The argument is a chain of necessary implications.
2
Follow the logical chain
Trace each step: absence of will → aversion → eternity of aversion → no intention → no originated things. Ensure you see why each step is necessary.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the implication: creation exists, so will must exist. This is a proof by contradiction of the opposite.
This sets up the overall argument for will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : أنَّهُ لَوِ انتفَتْ إرادة الباري تعالى للفعل ؛ أي : القصدُ إليه . . لاتّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكراهة لوجودِ الفعل ، ويلزمُ أنْ تكون تلك الكراهة قديمةً ؛ لاستحالةِ اتّصافِهِ تعالى بالحوادثِ ، فلا تنعدم أبداً ، فلا يُوجَدُ ضدُّها الذي هو القصد أبداً ، وذلك يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ حادث أبد الآبادِ ؛ لعدم إمكان وجودِ قصدِ مِنَ الفاعل إلى نقلِهِ عن العدمِ الذي كانَ عليهِ إلى الوجودِ . والقدرة لا تصلحُ أنْ تكونَ مُخصصة للفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص وعلى مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة بدلاً عما يقابل ذلك ؛ لأنَّ نسبة القدرةِ إلى جميع الممكنات في كل زمن وعلى كل حال . . نسبة واحدة . وأيضاً : فشأنُ القدرة التأثيرُ والإيجادُ ، والموجدُ مِن حيث هو مُوجِدٌ غيرُ المرجّح مِنْ حيث هو مُرجّح ؛ لتوقف الإيجادِ على الترجيح . وكذا العلم لا يصلح للتخصيص ؛ لأنَّ التخصيص للممكن ببعض ما جازَ عليهِ تأثير فيهِ ، والعلم ليسَ مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة ، وإلا لَمَا تعلَّق بالواجب والمستحيل . وأيضاً : فالعلم بالوقوع تابع للوقوع ، فلو كان الوقوع تابعاً لذلك العلم لزم الدور . وأمَّا الحياةُ والكلامُ والسمع والبصرُ : فلا يخفى أنَّها لا تصلح للتخصيص ؛ لأنَّ الحياة ليسَتْ مِنَ الصفات المتعلقة بالغيرِ ، ولأنَّها أيضاً كالقدرة في تساوي النسبة ، والسمع والبصر كالعلم في التبعيَّةِ ، والكلام لا تعلُّق لهُ بالإيجادِ ، فلا بدَّ إِذا مِنْ صفةٍ أخرى خاصيَّتُها الترجيح والتخصيص ، وهي المسماة بالإرادة .
Meaning: If the will of God Most High for the act—i.e., the intention towards it—were absent, then God Most High would be described as aversion (karāha) to the existence of the act. It would then be necessary that that aversion be eternal, because it is impossible for Him to be described by originated accidents. So it would never cease, and thus its opposite, which is intention, would never exist. This necessarily implies that no originated thing would ever exist, because it is impossible for an agent to have an intention to transfer it from the non-existence which it had to existence. And power (qudra) is not suitable to be a specifier of an act for existence at a particular time, in a particular measure, and with a particular quality instead of what opposes that, because the relation of power to all possibles in every time and every state is one and the same relation. Also, the function of power is effect and bringing into existence, and the bringer into existence qua bringer into existence is other than the preponderator (murajjiḥ) qua preponderator, because bringing into existence is dependent on preponderance. Likewise, knowledge (ʿilm) is not suitable for specification, because specification of a possible to some of what is possible for it is an effect upon it, and knowledge is not among the effective attributes; otherwise it would not apply to the necessary and the impossible. Also, knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence; if occurrence followed that knowledge, there would be circularity. As for life, speech, hearing, and sight: it is not hidden that they are not suitable for specification, because life is not among the attributes that relate to others, and also it is like power in having equal relation; hearing and sight are like knowledge in their subordination; and speech has no connection to bringing into existence. So it is necessary, then, to have another attribute whose property is preponderance and specification, and that is what is called will (al-irāda).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a sequential proof establishing that the Creator must be described with a specific attribute called will (al-irāda) that is distinct from other attributes like power and knowledge. Sanusi begins with a conditional: if the will of God were absent for an act, then He would be described with aversion to that act. Since God cannot be described with originated accidents, that aversion would be eternal, so its opposite (intention) would never arise, and no contingent thing could ever come into existence. This shows that will is necessary for creation. Next, Sanusi examines power (qudra). Power alone cannot be the specifier because its relation to all possibles at all times is equal; it does not bias one possibility over another. Also, the function of power is bringing into existence, which depends on preponderance, while the preponderator itself is distinct. Then he considers knowledge (ʿilm): specification is an effect on the possible, but knowledge is not an effective attribute; if it were, it would apply to the impossible as well. Furthermore, knowledge of occurrence follows the occurrence, so if occurrence followed knowledge, this would create circularity. He then reviews life, speech, hearing, and sight. Life has no relation to others; it too has equal relation like power. Hearing and sight are subordinate like knowledge. Speech has no connection to bringing into existence. Since no attribute other than will is apt for specification, there must be an attribute whose specific property is preponderance and specification, and that is will. The argument follows a clear pattern of elimination: each candidate is shown to be insufficient, leaving will as the only necessary attribute for contingent creation.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
كراهة (karāha)
حادث (ḥādith)
قصد (qaṣd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can create without having a specific intention or will, simply by power or knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi shows that without will, there would be eternal aversion, preventing any act of creation. Thus will is necessary.

Checkpoint
What would God be described with if He lacked will for an act?
A) Indifference
B) Aversion (karāha)
C) Power (qudra)
D) Knowledge (ʿilm)

Key Takeaway
Without divine will, no contingent being could ever come into existence, so will is a necessary attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence depends on God's ongoing intention towards you, how should this change the way you approach your day? Will you live with the awareness that without His will you would cease to be?
?
Sanusi argues that God's will is eternal and unchanging. What does that mean for your confidence in His promises? Do you truly believe His will for you is fixed and reliable, or do you live as if He might change His mind?
?
Could you honestly say you have ever meditated on the fact that your very existence is held in place by God's will? How would it change your relationship with Him if you paused right now and acknowledged that He is actively willing you to be here?

Why Power Cannot Be the Specificator
Sanusi argues that power cannot be the specificator due to equal relation and dependency of existence on preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power (qudra) alone cannot specify which possible events occur, because it relates equally to all possibilities.

Core Idea
Power relates equally to all possibles; specification needs will.

After showing the necessity of will, Sanusi examines whether power could serve as the specificator. He gives two reasons: power's neutral relation to all possibles, and the distinction between bringing into existence and preponderating. This lesson unpacks those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on power
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on why power is insufficient. Notice the phrases 'nisba wāḥida' (one relation) and the distinction between 'mūjid' and 'murajjiḥ'.
2
Understand the first argument
Power relates equally to all possible acts, times, and qualities. It does not favor one over another, so it cannot specify.
Equal relation means no preference.
3
Understand the second argument
The function of power is to bring into existence (ījād), but bringing into existence depends on preponderance (tarjīḥ). The preponderator must be distinct from the power itself.
This shows specification precedes effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : أنَّهُ لَوِ انتفَتْ إرادة الباري تعالى للفعل ؛ أي : القصدُ إليه . . لاتّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكراهة لوجودِ الفعل ، ويلزمُ أنْ تكون تلك الكراهة قديمةً ؛ لاستحالةِ اتّصافِهِ تعالى بالحوادثِ ، فلا تنعدم أبداً ، فلا يُوجَدُ ضدُّها الذي هو القصد أبداً ، وذلك يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ حادث أبد الآبادِ ؛ لعدم إمكان وجودِ قصدِ مِنَ الفاعل إلى نقلِهِ عن العدمِ الذي كانَ عليهِ إلى الوجودِ . والقدرة لا تصلحُ أنْ تكونَ مُخصصة للفعل بالوجودِ في زمنٍ مخصوص وعلى مقدار مخصوص وصفةٍ مخصوصة بدلاً عما يقابل ذلك ؛ لأنَّ نسبة القدرةِ إلى جميع الممكنات في كل زمن وعلى كل حال . . نسبة واحدة . وأيضاً : فشأنُ القدرة التأثيرُ والإيجادُ ، والموجدُ مِن حيث هو مُوجِدٌ غيرُ المرجّح مِنْ حيث هو مُرجّح ؛ لتوقف الإيجادِ على الترجيح . وكذا العلم لا يصلح للتخصيص ؛ لأنَّ التخصيص للممكن ببعض ما جازَ عليهِ تأثير فيهِ ، والعلم ليسَ مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة ، وإلا لَمَا تعلَّق بالواجب والمستحيل . وأيضاً : فالعلم بالوقوع تابع للوقوع ، فلو كان الوقوع تابعاً لذلك العلم لزم الدور . وأمَّا الحياةُ والكلامُ والسمع والبصرُ : فلا يخفى أنَّها لا تصلح للتخصيص ؛ لأنَّ الحياة ليسَتْ مِنَ الصفات المتعلقة بالغيرِ ، ولأنَّها أيضاً كالقدرة في تساوي النسبة ، والسمع والبصر كالعلم في التبعيَّةِ ، والكلام لا تعلُّق لهُ بالإيجادِ ، فلا بدَّ إِذا مِنْ صفةٍ أخرى خاصيَّتُها الترجيح والتخصيص ، وهي المسماة بالإرادة .
Meaning: If the will of God Most High for the act—i.e., the intention towards it—were absent, then God Most High would be described as aversion (karāha) to the existence of the act. It would then be necessary that that aversion be eternal, because it is impossible for Him to be described by originated accidents. So it would never cease, and thus its opposite, which is intention, would never exist. This necessarily implies that no originated thing would ever exist, because it is impossible for an agent to have an intention to transfer it from the non-existence which it had to existence. And power (qudra) is not suitable to be a specifier of an act for existence at a particular time, in a particular measure, and with a particular quality instead of what opposes that, because the relation of power to all possibles in every time and every state is one and the same relation. Also, the function of power is effect and bringing into existence, and the bringer into existence qua bringer into existence is other than the preponderator (murajjiḥ) qua preponderator, because bringing into existence is dependent on preponderance. Likewise, knowledge (ʿilm) is not suitable for specification, because specification of a possible to some of what is possible for it is an effect upon it, and knowledge is not among the effective attributes; otherwise it would not apply to the necessary and the impossible. Also, knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence; if occurrence followed that knowledge, there would be circularity. As for life, speech, hearing, and sight: it is not hidden that they are not suitable for specification, because life is not among the attributes that relate to others, and also it is like power in having equal relation; hearing and sight are like knowledge in their subordination; and speech has no connection to bringing into existence. So it is necessary, then, to have another attribute whose property is preponderance and specification, and that is what is called will (al-irāda).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a sequential proof establishing that the Creator must be described with a specific attribute called will (al-irāda) that is distinct from other attributes like power and knowledge. Sanusi begins with a conditional: if the will of God were absent for an act, then He would be described with aversion to that act. Since God cannot be described with originated accidents, that aversion would be eternal, so its opposite (intention) would never arise, and no contingent thing could ever come into existence. This shows that will is necessary for creation. Next, Sanusi examines power (qudra). Power alone cannot be the specifier because its relation to all possibles at all times is equal; it does not bias one possibility over another. Also, the function of power is bringing into existence, which depends on preponderance, while the preponderator itself is distinct. Then he considers knowledge (ʿilm): specification is an effect on the possible, but knowledge is not an effective attribute; if it were, it would apply to the impossible as well. Furthermore, knowledge of occurrence follows the occurrence, so if occurrence followed knowledge, this would create circularity. He then reviews life, speech, hearing, and sight. Life has no relation to others; it too has equal relation like power. Hearing and sight are subordinate like knowledge. Speech has no connection to bringing into existence. Since no attribute other than will is apt for specification, there must be an attribute whose specific property is preponderance and specification, and that is will. The argument follows a clear pattern of elimination: each candidate is shown to be insufficient, leaving will as the only necessary attribute for contingent creation.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ)
ترجيح (tarjīḥ)
نسبة (nisba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's power alone determines what happens, without need for will.
Correction: Sanusi shows power's equal relation and dependency on preponderance, proving will is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why can't power be the specificator according to the first argument?
A) Power is not eternal
B) Power has equal relation to all possibles
C) Power is not an attribute of God
D) Power only applies to necessary things

Key Takeaway
Power alone is insufficient for specification because it relates equally to all possibilities and because specification logically precedes the act of bringing into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you attribute the outcomes in your life to your own ability (qudra) without acknowledging that God's will (irāda) is the true specifier? What would change if you lived with the constant awareness that your power is only effective because of His prior choice?
?
If power relates equally to all possibilities, then your success in any endeavor is not due to your superior effort alone. Are you willing to accept that even your most carefully planned achievements were ultimately specified by God? How does that humility sit with your ego?
?
Sanusi argues that bringing into existence depends on preponderance, not power. When you look at the specific circumstances of your life—your health, wealth, relationships—are you quick to credit your own choices and strengths, or do you pause to recognize the divine specification behind them? Commit today to a specific habit that reminds you of this dependency.

Why Knowledge and Other Attributes Cannot Specify
Sanusi shows knowledge and other attributes cannot specify, then concludes will is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge (ʿilm) and other attributes like life, speech, hearing, and sight cannot serve as the specificator, leaving will as the only suitable attribute.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows occurrence; it cannot cause it.

Sanusi continues his elimination of candidate attributes for specification. He first argues that knowledge is not an effective attribute and would lead to circularity if it were the cause of occurrence. Then he quickly dismisses life, speech, hearing, and sight as unsuitable. This lesson examines these arguments and concludes that will is the necessary specificator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on knowledge
Read how Sanusi argues against knowledge: it is not effective (ghayr mu'aththira) and if it were, it would apply to the impossible; also, knowledge follows occurrence, so circularity would result.
2
Understand the circularity argument
If occurrence depended on knowledge, and knowledge depended on occurrence (since knowledge of it follows occurrence), we would have a vicious circle.
This is a classic logical pitfall.
3
Review the dismissal of other attributes
Life, speech, hearing, sight are shown to be unsuitable for similar reasons: life does not relate to others, hearing/sight follow events, speech has no connection to existence.
4
Conclude with the necessity of will
Since no other attribute suffices, there must be an attribute whose property is preponderance and specification—that is will.
This completes the proof.

Why Only Will Specifies Creation
Sanusi eliminates other attributes to prove will is the specifying attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only divine will, and not any other attribute, can be the specifying attribute for creation.

Core Idea
Only will can break the symmetry of possibility.

Sanusi continues his proof for divine will by systematically showing that no other attribute can perform the specifying function. He argues that knowledge, life, power, speech, hearing, and sight all fail for different reasons, leaving will as the only candidate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi moves through each rejected attribute.
2
Understand the problem with knowledge
Sanusi first addresses knowledge: if occurrence depended on knowledge, it would create a circle. Understand why this is invalid.
Knowledge depends on the known, not vice versa.
3
Examine why life, power, hearing, sight, and speech are unsuitable
Life is not relational to others; power has equal relation to opposites; hearing and sight depend on their objects like knowledge; speech does not cause existence. Identify each reason.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
After eliminating all other possibilities, Sanusi concludes that an attribute with the property of preponderance and specification is necessary, and that is will.
This is a clear argument by elimination.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how Sanusi uses a process of elimination to prove the necessity of will. This is a common method in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً: فالعلم بالوقوع تابع للوقوع، فلو كان الوقوع تابعاً لذلك العلم لزم الدور. وأمَّا الحياةُ والكلامُ والسمع والبصرُ: فلا يخفى أنَّها لا تصلح للتخصيص؛ لأنَّ الحياة ليسَتْ مِنَ الصفات المتعلقة بالغيرِ، ولأنَّها أيضاً كالقدرة في تساوي النسبة، والسمع والبصر كالعلم في التبعيَّةِ، والكلام لا تعلُّق لهُ بالإيجادِ، فلا بدَّ إِذا مِنْ صفةٍ أخرى خاصيَّتُها الترجيح والتخصيص، وهي المسماة بالإرادة.
And also: knowledge of occurrence is dependent on the occurrence, for if occurrence were dependent on that knowledge, circularity would result. As for life, speech, hearing, and sight: it is not hidden that they are not suitable for specification; because life is not among the attributes related to others, and also it is like power in the equality of relation, and hearing and sight are like knowledge in dependency, and speech has no relation to bringing into existence. So there must be another attribute whose property is preponderance and specification, and it is called will.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage continues an argument for the necessity of divine will. Sanusi begins by rejecting other attributes as the specifying factor. He first addresses the problem of circularity in knowledge: if occurrence were dependent on knowledge, it would imply a circle. Then he examines life, speech, hearing, and sight. Life is not relational to others; power has equal relation to opposites; hearing and sight depend on their objects like knowledge; speech does not cause existence. Therefore, only will (al-irada) remains as the attribute that can specify one possibility over another.

Key Takeaway
Will alone can specify because it is the attribute of preponderance and specification, breaking the symmetry of possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Divine Will and Rulings Are Not for a Purpose
Sanusi states divine will is eternal, general, and without purpose; expands to rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's will is eternal and comprehensive, and that neither His will nor His rulings serve any purpose, as purpose would imply deficiency in God or obligation upon Him.

Core Idea
God’s will and rulings have no purpose; purpose would imply deficiency.

This passage from Sanusi's creedal work explains two essential aspects of divine will: its eternity and generality, and its transcendence beyond any purpose. Understanding this helps clarify why God’s actions and laws are not subject to external considerations or motivations like human purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument: he first states the attributes of will, then argues against purpose using a reductio ad absurdum.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two main claims
Find where Sanusi asserts that will is eternal and general, and then where he denies purpose for will and for divine rulings. These are the key doctrinal points.
3
Understand the argument against purpose
Sanusi gives two reasons: (1) If God had a purpose, He would be deficient and completed by His act. (2) If His creation had a purpose, He would have to provide the best for creatures. Both are impossible.
The second reason is deferred ('as will come').
4
Connect rulings to the same logic
Notice how Sanusi extends the argument to divine commands and prohibitions. All actions are equally God's creation, so specifying some as obligatory or forbidden is purely by will, not for any purpose.
This reinforces God's absolute sovereignty.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: God is utterly independent and His will is not constrained by external factors. This safeguards divine transcendence and the nature of worship.
It also rejects any notion of 'obligation' on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ ذلك بإرادة قديمة عامة في جميعِ الممكناتِ ، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً؛ لما عرفت قبل في القدرة، وأن تكونَ إرادته لا لغرض له ، وإلا لكان ناقصاً في ذاتِهِ مُتكملاً بفعلِهِ ، وذلك محال ، ولا لغرض لخلْقِهِ وإلا وجب عليهِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم ، وهو لما سيأتي محال. وكما استحالَ أنْ يريد سبحانَهُ أو يفعل لغرض .. كذلكَ استحالَ أنْ يكونَ حكمه على فعل بوجوب أو تحريم أو غيرهما مِنَ الأحكام الشرعيَّةِ لغرضِ مِنَ الأغراض ؛ لأنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مستويةً في أنَّها خلقه واختراعه ، فتعين بعضها للإيجاب وبعضها للتحريم أو غيره .. واقع بمحض.
And it follows that that is by an eternal will, general over all possible things, whether good or evil, because of what you knew before concerning power, and that His will is not for any purpose, otherwise He would be deficient in His essence and perfected by His action, and that is impossible; nor for a purpose of His creation, otherwise He would have to observe what is beneficial and most beneficial for them, and that is impossible as will come. And just as it is impossible that He, exalted, intend or act for a purpose, similarly it is impossible that His judgment on an action regarding obligation or prohibition or other shar‘i judgments be for any purpose, because actions are all equal in that they are His creation and invention, so specifying some for obligation and some for prohibition or other is by sheer [will].
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement on the nature of divine will (irādah) and its corollaries. The author begins by stating that it follows necessarily that the creation is by an eternal will (irādah qadīmah) that encompasses all possibles (al-mumkināt), both good and evil, as previously discussed regarding power (al-qudrah). Next, he asserts that this will is not for any purpose (gharaḍ). If God had a purpose, He would be inherently deficient (nāqis fī dhātihī) and would be completed by His action (mutakamillan bi-fi‘lih), which is impossible. Similarly, He has no purpose in creation, for if He did, He would be obliged to observe what is beneficial (ṣalāḥ) and most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for His creatures, which is impossible for reasons that will be mentioned later. The author then extends this reasoning to divine legislation: just as it is impossible for God to intend or act for a purpose, so it is impossible for His rulings (ḥukm) on actions—whether obligation (wujūb), prohibition (taḥrīm), or other shar‘i judgments—to be for any purpose. The rationale is that all actions are equally His creation and invention (khalq wa ikhtirā‘), so singling out some for obligation and others for prohibition is purely by sheer divine will (bi-mahḍ), not because of any external consideration.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة
غرض
ناقص في ذاته
متكمل بفعله
صلاح وأصلح
أحكام شرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will has a purpose similar to human will, or that His rulings are based on external considerations like benefit.
Correction: The passage argues that a purpose would imply deficiency in God's essence or an obligation upon Him, both of which are impossible. God's will and rulings are purely by His will, not for any purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for God's will or actions to be for a purpose?
A) Because a purpose would require God to be influenced by creation
B) Because a purpose would imply God is deficient in His essence and perfected by His action
C) Because a purpose would make God's will non-eternal
D) Because a purpose would limit God's power

Key Takeaway
God's will and His rulings are absolutely free of any purpose; purpose would imply deficiency or obligation upon God, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems utterly pointless, do you find yourself searching for a hidden purpose to make sense of it? How will you train your heart to submit to God's sheer will without demanding an explanation? Name one practical step you can take today to strengthen that submission.
?
You likely justify your obedience to Islamic rulings by pointing to their worldly benefits (health, morality, social harmony). After this lesson, can you honestly obey a ruling even if you see absolutely no benefit for yourself? What would that obedience look like in your daily life?
?
If God's will is completely free from any purpose, then every aspect of your life—your successes, failures, and even your faith—is by His sheer will. How does this truth challenge the way you view your own achievements or struggles? Will you let this belief transform your gratitude and patience? If yes, what is the first thing you will change in your attitude starting today?
